Summary 0:
Mutual funds and collective investment undertakings — marketing communications to investors (Regulation (EU) 2019/852) (see summary)
Mutual Funds and collective Investment undertakings – marketing communications TO INVESTIGATE IN AIFs AND UCITS
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2013/31/EU — marketing communication to investors in mutual funds
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out minimum requirements for marketing communications* to investors* in collective investment funds (AIFs and UCITS) and common principles concerning fees and charges levied on managers of collective investment undertaking* in relation to their cross-border activities.
It repeals Directive 2003/71/EC.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to:
alternative investment fund managers (alternative funds), including any UCITS which has not designated a UCITS management company;
EuVECA managers, including any EuSEF managers;
eucapital funds (e.g. venture capital funds, social entrepreneurship funds, etc.).
It does not apply to investment funds managed by a collective investment company.
The marketing communications addressed to investors must contain:
investor rights and information on investor rights;
a description of the investment fund’s assets and liabilities;
investment objectives and investment strategy;
information on the risks associated with investing in an investment fund.
Investors must be informed of:
the investment objectives and objectives of the fund;
the risk profiles of investment funds;
how the fund is managed;
its investment strategy and investment objectives.
Marketing communications must be in a language that is easily understood by investors. They must also contain information on the rights and obligations of investors, such as the investment objectives, investment strategy, investment risks and investment opportunities.
Marketing must be carried out in a way that is transparent, non-discriminatory and non-pre-emptive.
EU countries must ensure that investors do not acquire units or shares in a qualifying venture capital fund through pre-marketing and that investors contacted as part of marketing may only acquire them through marketing permitted under Article 15.3 of the regulation.
A prospectus must be published when securities are offered to the public or admitted to trading on a market. The prospectus should contain information about the investment objective, the risks involved and the investment strategy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULATION APPLY?
The directive entered into force on 31 December 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Mutual fund marketing’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Alternative investment fund: a fund managed by an independent investment fund manager (IFM) or an EuVEF manager (EuSECF manager).
* UCITS: a unit-linked investment trust (UCITS) is a type of mutual fund that provides investors with the opportunity to invest in a specific type of investment product.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2013/30/EU of 31 October 2013 laying down minimum marketing communications for investors in a mutual fund and a certain type of UCITS (OJ L 165, 31.10.2013, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Directive 2013 /30/eu have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 1:
Counterfeiting products — EU market surveillance rules
Counterfeitting products —EU market surveillance policies
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/426 on the market surveillance of counterfeit products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent counterfeit products from entering the EU market.
It lays down rules for EU countries’ market surveillance authorities.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that counterfeit products do not comply with the requirements set out in the EU harmonisation legislation on the marketing of goods;
establish a national market surveillance strategy to identify counterfeit products;
implement controls to detect and prevent the sale of counterfeit goods.
The regulation lays down the powers of EU countries to identify and detect counterfeit products and to impose penalties on those that fail to comply with these requirements.
Market surveillance authorities are responsible for:
identifying and detecting counterfeit products that are likely to pose a risk to health and safety of end users;
collecting and analysing information on the occurrence of non-compliant products.
Implementing the national surveillance strategy must include at least the following elements, when this does not compromise market surveillance activities:
the available information on non-compliance products, in particular taking into account the checks and controls referred to in Articles 11(3) and 25(1) of the regulation;
the information on air emissions from vehicles and appliances burning gaseous fuels and repealing Directive 2009/142/EC (OJ L 81, 31.3.2016, pp. 1-2);
the data on the number of counterfeit items on the EU’s market;
information on the type of counterfeit product and the nature and the overall actual or potential harm resulting from the instance of noncompliance.
Each EU country must designate a market surveillance authority to carry out market surveillance in its territory. The authority must have the powers to:
impose controls on counterfeit products to detect, investigate and prevent their sale;
identify and investigate counterfeit products which pose a threat to health or safety of the end user;
assist in the investigation and prosecution of counterfeiting offences;
provide information to the European Commission and other EU countries on the level of controls on products which present a serious risk to human health or to the environment;
make recommendations to EU countries and the European Economic Area (EEA) countries on measures to be taken in the event of a serious breach of the legislation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 30 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
  *   * * 
For more information, see:
Counterfiting products (European Commission).

Summary 2:
Copyright and rights in retransmission of television and radio programmes (Directive 2019/24/EU)
Copyright and retransmissions of TV and radio programs (DirectIVE 2019/25/EU)*
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/23/EU on the exercise of copyright and rights related to copyright in the broadcasting of television programmes and the direct injection of programmes in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that the rights exercised by broadcasting organisations in respect of their own transmissions are respected.
It sets out the rules for the rights to be exercised by rightholders of works and other protected subject matter.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to:
radio and television programmes originating in other EU countries;
online services that are ancillary to broadcasts;
the direct injection (transmission of programme-carrying signals to an organisation other than a broadcasting organisation) of audiovisual works.
EU countries must ensure that:
the rights to the right to the use of the works are respected;
rights to the reproduction of the work are protected;
right holders receive appropriate remuneration.
Rights to the rights are exercised by the broadcasting organisation and by collective management organisations (collective management organisations) which manage the rights of broadcasting organisations.
The rights of the collective management organisation may be transferred to broadcasting organisations or to collective management bodies.
Right holders are entitled to a share of the remunerations paid by the operator of a retransmitting service.
Where an agreement is not concluded between a collective management or collective management body and the broadcaster organisation regarding the authorisation for the use or reproduction of their works or other protected subjects matter, the agreement may be terminated by a mediator.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVES APPLY?
Directives (EU, EU, EFTA and non-EU countries) have applied since 15 April 2019.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive.
For more information, see:
‘The EU’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive (EU), Euratom, Euratom and EFTA of 14 April 2019 on the exercising of copyright, rights related thereto in the television broadcasting of programmes and in the direct integration of programmes into the online services of the Member States and repealing Directive 93/83/EEC (OJ L 248, 6.10.2019, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Communication from the Commission to the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee, the Committee of the Regions and the Committee on the Functioning of the EU — Commission communication on the interpretation of Council Directive (EEC) No 552/2013 on the harmonisation of the rules concerning copyright and the rights applicable to satellite broadcasting and cable broadcasting (COM(2013) 552 final - not published in the Official Journal)

Summary 3:
EU country code top-level domain (ccTLD) for the EU (2019)			
The European Union (EU) aims to build an online Union identity and to encourage cross-border online activities.
ACT
Commission Regulation (EC) No 733/2002 of 19 October 2002 establishing the.eu country code Top-Level domain for the European Union.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It establishes the EU’s.eu TLD for the purpose of building an online EU identity.
It aims to support the digital single market by allowing EU citizens to register a.eu domain name, regardless of their place of residence.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for the registration of domain names in.eu.
EU citizens can register a domain name in any EU country by providing their postal address, postal address or postal address in the EU.
The registry responsible for registering domain names is the European Network of Registrars (ENR).
The registrar is responsible for the organisation, administration and management of.eu and for the protection of the rights of domain name holders.
Registry fees are charged for the right to register domain names. These fees are directly related to the costs incurred by the registrars.
National authorities may refuse to register domains if they consider that they do not comply with the rules of the regulation or that they are contrary to EU law.
A multistakeholder advisory group is set up by the Commission to advise the Commission on the implementation of the regulations.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 19 October 2003.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been using.eu for over a decade.
For more information, see:
‘The.eu Top Level Domain’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 4:
Cohesion Fund for Europe (CEF) — cross-border projects in the field of energy, transport and digital infrastructure (2020-2025)
Cohesion fund for Europe — cross -border projects on energy and transport
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/695 on the European Union’s Cohesion Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Union (EU, or CEF) for the purpose of supporting actions in the fields of transport, energy, digital infrastructure and information and communication technology (ICT) in order to improve the free movement of persons, goods, capital and services across the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The CEF supports actions in all the fields covered by the European Commission's strategic objectives for 2025 with a view to optimising investment in digital connectivity infrastructure.
The objectives of the CEF are to:
promote the development of a high-quality, high-speed, high capacity and high-capacity transport network in the EU;
ensure the efficient use of existing infrastructure;
provide a high level of safety and security in the transport sector;
improve the efficiency of the EU's internal market;
support the sustainable development of the energy sector.
Eligible actions
Actions eligible for funding are:
transport projects;
information and communication technologies (ICTs);
energy projects; and
digital infrastructure projects.
EU countries must submit an application for selection as a cross-cross-border project in the following fields to the Commission:
Transport projects
The Commission decides on the eligibility criteria based on the following criteria:
the number of EU countries eligible for EU funding
the total amount of EU funding for the project
the duration of the project and the total amount transferred from the EU funding;
the type of action
the nature and scope of the action and the type of funding.
Projects eligible for CEF funding
The European Commission must assess the uptake of funds for projects in this field by the EU countries by the end of 2023.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Cross-border actions’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Cross-border: a project that crosses a border with another EU country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  i.e. the regulation sets out the eligibility and award criteria for actions to be funded under the European Communities’ (Cohesion) Fund for the benefit of the Member States.

Summary 5:
Hosting service providers exposed to terrorist content must inform the competent authorities of the removal of the content or the disabling of access to the terrorist content in all EU countries.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU) 2017/852 of 25 November 2017 on hosting service providers and users’ trust in the online environment
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
   
 
It sets out the procedures for the identification and removal of terrorist content hosted on a hosting service provider’s premises.
KEY POINTS
The regulation defines ‘terrorist content’ as material provided by a content provider that is considered to be a threat to the safety or security of the European Union (EU).
To identify terrorist content, the competent authority of the EU country in which the content is hosted must issue a removal order.
The competent authority must then inform the hosting provider of the reason for the removal order and the time of its removal or disabling.
Hosting providers must ensure that:
the content they host is not terrorist content;
the information they provide is protected by the protection of personal data, including human oversight and verifications, to avoid any unintended or erroneous decision leading to their removal of or disabling access to content that is terrorist content.
If a hosting provider fails to comply with an order issued by an EU country, it may be subject to an effective remedy.
Procedure for cross-border removal orders
EU countries must exchange information, coordinate and cooperate with each other and, where appropriate, with Europol, with regard to removal orders, in particular to avoid duplication of effort, enhance coordination and avoid interference with investigations in different Member States.
A host provider must inform a competent authority within 3 working days of receiving an order to remove or disable terrorist content or to disable access to it in all the EU countries, indicating, in the template set out in the regulation, the time that the order was issued and the location where it was issued.
An EU country may also issue a non-exhaustive list of the countries in which an order has been issued or which it is likely to be issued in order to identify and remove terrorist content and to enable EU countries to share information on the content that has been removed or disabled.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2009/48/EC has applied since 1 December 2009.
Regulation (EC) No 552/2013 has applied from 1 December 2013.
BACKGROUND
 			
For more information, see:
‘Terrorist content on the internet’ on the European Commission's website.
REFERENCES
Act
Entry into force
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Commission Decision 2009/488/EC

OJ L 140 of 1.12-1.13.

Summary 6:
Framework Programme for Research and Innovation 2021-2027 (FPRI 2021)
Framework Program for Research & Innovation 2021 - 2027 (FRP)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — Framework Programme for research and innovation 2021-2020
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the rules for the EU’s Framework programme for research & innovation (FRRI) 2021–2027.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The framework programme aims to support research, innovation and technological development (R&I) in the fields of democracy and governance, culture, cultural heritage and creativity, and social and economic transformations.
The objectives of the programme are to:
promote scientific, technological, economic, environmental and societal impact in pursuit of this general objective and to maximise the added value of the EU's R&I investments;
improve the quality of life of the beneficiaries;
support the development of innovative products and services;
provide the European Commission with the necessary resources to implement the programme.
Eligible actions and ethical principles
The programme is open to all EU countries and non-EU countries. It is funded by the European Union (EU), the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Social Fund (ESF).
The European Innovation Council (EIC) is responsible for implementing the programme and for coordinating the activities of the European Partnerships (EPs).
Beneficiaries must comply with the following ethical principles:
participation of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) is encouraged;
research and innovation is based on the best scientific and technological knowledge available in the EU;
the results of the research are shared and exploited by the beneficiaries, including through intermediary organisations;
ex ante demonstration of additionality and directionality of the EPPs is required;
promoting the European Partnership, including a common strategic vision of the purpose of the EPs, is required.
EU countries must ensure that beneficiaries provide open access to peer-reviewed scientific publications.
Projects must be funded by EU countries, the EIB or the ESI.
Accelerator support is provided by the EIC, the European Innovation Fund (EIF) or the European Fund for Innovation (EFI). The EIC may also provide the EIF with financial support for projects that are not covered by the EU budget.
Financing
The EU budget for the framework programme is set at EUR 60,000,000 for the period 2021 to 2027. This amount is reduced by €1.5 million for each of the following periods: 2021-23;2023-24;2025-26;2027-28;2028-29;2029-30;2030-31;2031-32;2032-33;2033-34;2034-35;2035-36;2036-37;2037-38;2038-39;2040-41;2041-42;2042-43;2045-46;2046-47;2047-48;2048-49;2049-50;2050-51;2051-52;2052-53;2053-54;2054-55;2055-56;2056-57;2058-59;2060-61;2061-62;2062-63;2063-64;2065-66;2066-69;2070-71;2071-72;2072-73;2073-74;2074-75;2075-80;2080-85;2085-90;2090-91; 2090-90);2090–91;2091-92;2092-93;2093-94;2094-95;2095-96;2096-97;2097-98;2098-99;2099-2000;202000-2001;2000-2002;2010-2011;2011-2012;2012-2013;2013-2014;2014-2015;2015-2015 and 2016-2016;2016-2016.
Implementation
The implementation of the Framework Programme is managed by the Commission and the EU countries’ competent authorities. The Commission is assisted by the Agency for Innovation and Entrepreneurship (AIE) under the European Research and Development Agency (ERDA) and by the Directorate-General for Research, Innovation and Innovation (RIRI) under Horizon Europe.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

  The regulation has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND

Summary 7:
The European Union Agency for Space Technology (EUASAT)
The EUASAT
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2017/852 — the establishment of the European Union Space Technology Agency
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE REGULATION?
   
The decision and regulation establish the EU Space Technologies Agency (EUESAAT).
KEY POINTS
The ESAAT is responsible for the management of the EU’s programme for space technology, the Galileo satellite navigation system and the European Geostationary Navigation Satellite System (EGNOS).
It is a European Agency (EASA) responsible for:
operating the European Space Agency (ESAAT);
providing user-driven space weather (SWE) services for EU countries;
promoting the user and market uptake of Galileo and EGNOS;
developing user-oriented applications for all the programme‘s components.
The EASA has the following tasks:
assessing the risks and corresponding user needs, raising the awareness of space weather risks, ensuring the provision of SWE services, and improving EU countries’ capabilities to provide the SWE service.
Providing users with information on the risks associated with space weather, including the technical specifications for SWE and the use-cases for GOVSATCOM services.
Promoting user uptake of the programmes’ components, such as the Galileo navigation system, the European Green Space Initiative (EGSI), the European satellite communication system (ESCIS) and the Europe-Mediterranean satellite communications system (EMCMS).
Providers of SST services must:
ensure that users have access to the SST infrastructure;
provide users with the information they need to make informed decisions about SST operations;
assist in the development of user-friendly SST applications;
support the development and operation of the EGSI, EGNI and EMCMS.
EU countries are responsible for ensuring that the EASAT is operational and that its services are available to all EU countries.
Operational requirements for the ESAAT are set out in the regulation.
EASAT operates under the following operational requirements:
the European Commission must adopt, by means of implementing acts, the operational requirements for ESAAT services, in the form of technical specifications;
the EU countries must provide the EUSAT with the necessary ground segment infrastructure and operation;
ESAAT must provide EU countries with the SESAT services;
EU country authorities must ensure that EU countries have the necessary infrastructure and operations to provide ESAAT with SES.
SESAT must also provide the European Commission with the operational specifications for GOSPSAT communications.
Agency staff must be independent, independent and non-political, and must have a clear and independent role in the programme.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS AND REGULATIONS APPLY?
The decisions and the regulation have applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has been responsible for implementing the EU programme for the last 5 years.
For more information, see:
‘EU Space Technologies’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS

Summary 8:
European Digital Innovation Fund (EDIF) 2021-2027 (European Commission communication)
European digital innovation fund (EDIFF) 2021 - 2027
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2021/2027 establishing the European Digital Innovation Funds (EDIFF)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE PROGRAMMING?
The decision sets out the EU’s financial framework for the EDIF, which aims to support the development of innovative digital solutions in areas of public interest or market failure.
The programme is designed to support actions in the areas of cybersecurity and trust, digital innovation and the modernisation of public administrations.
KEY POINTS
The EDIF is a multiannual financial framework, which runs from 2021 to 2027. It is funded by the European Union (EU), the European Social Fund (ESF) and the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF).
The EDF and the ESF are the main sources of funding for the EDF, while the ERDF and ESF+ are the primary sources of support for the actions under the programme.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The DECISION and the programme entered into force on 1 January 2021.
They will expire on 31 December 2027, except for the Connecting Europe Facility, which will continue to operate until 31 December 2024.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Digital Innovation Fund’ on the European Commission’�s website.

Summary 9:
EU services policy — 2020-2025
EU service policy —2020-2035
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2020/2025 — EU services policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the competitiveness of the EU’s services sector by supporting small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) to grow and diversify their activities.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the objectives of the programme, which runs from 1 January 2020 to 31 December 2023.
The objectives are to:
increase the number of SMEs engaged in services;
improve the quality of services for consumers and workers;
support SMEs to team up with SMEs from non-EU countries;
facilitate access to financial instruments and budgetary guarantees for SMEs;
promote cross-border cooperation between EU countries and SMEs in other EU countries.
Scope
The programme aims to support SMEs:
to create jobs and grow across borders, offer a wider choice of services at better prices, and maintain high standards for consumers;
to improve the quality and efficiency of services in the EU and to improve their competitiveness.
EU countries must:
ensure that SMEs have access to quality services at affordable prices;
provide SMEs with a high level of technical and organisational support;
encourage the development of the SMEs’ capacity to work together and to share best practices; and
support the growth and diversification of the services sector.
Eligible EU countries
The EU countries are eligible to apply for EU funding under the programme if they:
have a minimum gross domestic product (GDP) of at least €1.5 billion;
have at least 1 million SMEs operating in their territory; or
have an average GDP of more than €2 billion.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
 			
  EU services are a key driver of growth, competitiveness and employment in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:

Summary 10:
Weapons of mass destruction: the most dangerous firearms — EU rules on their import and trade
Rules of import and export of weapons of mass devastation: the more dangerous firearms – EU rules
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2015/2403 on the import, export and trade of firearms of mass destructiveness
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
   
 
It aims to ensure that the EU’s laws on firearms are applied uniformly throughout the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all types of firearms, including handguns, shotguns, rifles, assault rifles, pistols and revolvers.
It does not apply to shotguns, shotguns or assault rifles.
EU countries must ensure that:
all firearms are classified in the following categories:
category A: handguns;
category B: rifles;
machineguns;
explosives;
blunderbombs;
battery-operated firearms;
automatic pistols;
conventional shotguns;
handguns.
National authorities must:
implement implementing acts establishing technical specifications for the deactivation of firearms; and
establish a list of authorised persons to be authorised to possess, possess or trade such firearms.
Authorised persons may be prohibited from possessing, possessing or trading firearms if they:
have a criminal record;
have been convicted of a criminal offence; or are suspected of having committed such offences; or
have carried out a criminal act.
have not carried out an offence for more than 5 years;
are not authorised to carry out such an offence.
may be prohibited activities, such as:
employed in the manufacture, manufacture, import or export of a firearm;
carry out criminal activities;
use a firearm in a criminal activity;
operate a firearm for the purpose of killing, wounding or destroying a person;
engage in criminal activities for the purposes of committing criminal offences.
An EU country may authorise an authorised person to possess a firearm which is classified in category A if:
it has been lawfully acquired and registered before 13 June 2017;
it meets the other conditions laid down in the directive;
the firearm has been manufactured, imported, exported or placed on the market by a person authorised by an EU country.
The authorisation is valid for a period of 5 years from the date of entry into force of the implementing act. If the authorisation period is no longer met, EU countries must withdraw the respective authorisation.
Implementing acts must: be adopted by EU countries within 5 years of the date on which they are adopted;
establish technical specifications to deactivate the firearms and for their ammunition;
set out the technical requirements for the identification of the manufacturer and for the marking of the firearm; and, if the manufacturer does not comply with the technical specifications, the EU country must adopt implementing acts to identify the manufacturer.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE ENTER INTO FORCE?
It entered into force on 13 June 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 13 June 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat the proliferation of firearms. These include:
the introduction of a ban on the sale, importation, export or transfer of firearms with a high risk of being used for mass destruction;
a ban on certain types of ammunition and the ammunition for such ammunition.
For more information, see:
‘Firearms’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Directive 2015/2303/EU of 13 May 2015 on the introduction, export, transfer and trade in firearms (codified version)

Summary 11:
European Maritime Single Window (EMSWe) — information exchange between EU countries on port and maritime traffic
European Maritime single Window (EMSWe) – information sharing between EU governments and maritime transport
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1725 on the establishment of the European Maritime Single-Window environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the efficiency of maritime transport by harmonising the information that EU countries must provide to each other when a ship makes a port call.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets up a single European maritime single-window environment, known as the ‘EMSWe’.
It is designed to:
ensure that all EU countries have a single reporting environment for maritime traffic;
provide the information required by EU countries to operate a maritime national single window (MNSW);
facilitate the exchange of information between the MNSW and other EU countries’ maritime traffic management systems (such as the EU’s maritime traffic information system (MITIS) and the European Network of Maritime Traffic Management Systems (NOTMS));
reduce the duplication of information which must be provided for operational purposes;
improve the security of the information provided by maritime transport operators, such as port or terminal operators, when authorised by the NNSW.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that:
the information provided to the NMSW is accurate, reliable and up-to-date;
the data are used only by the competent authorities of the EU countries;
information is provided in a way that does not compromise the confidentiality, commercial sensitivities and legal constraints of the parties concerned;
data protection and confidentiality are guaranteed;
EU governments must ensure the confidentiality of the data provided by the parties involved.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 15 August 2018.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
 * Maritime transport: the transport of goods by sea, air or land.
* Maritime traffic management system: the system that monitors and manages maritime traffic in the EU.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to improve the efficiency of road toll collection systems (ETS) in the EU.

Summary 13:
EU countries’ immigration liaison officers — data collection and sharing
EU countries' information liaison officers – data gathering and sharing	SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/304 recasting Council Regulation (EC) No 377/2004
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It recasts and repeals Council regulation (EC), which had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2004.
It aims to ensure that the data collected and shared by EU countries and the European Union Agency for Migration and Border Control (EMBAC) are used only for the purposes of preventing and combating smuggling of migrants and trafficking in human beings and illegal immigration.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the tasks and reporting lines of EU country authorities, the European Commission and EU agencies for defining the scope and assignment of tasks and the reporting lines for their respective immigration liaison officer (i.e. border control officers) and the tasks of the Steering Board and facilitators of the network referred to in Article 5(2) of the regulation.
Tasks of EU countries
EU country authorities and the EU agencies must ensure that:
the information and statistics collected and exchanged by the immigration officers are uploaded to and exchanged via a secure web-based information exchange platform;
the data are uploaded in accordance with Article 1(2), without prejudice to Article 10(2).
The data are transmitted to the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCA), the European Migration Agency (EMA) and to the EBCA and the ECA.
The information is collected by the officers and the facilitators and transmitted by the EU country or EU agency to the relevant EU countries or international organisations. The data are not to be used for any other purpose other than the prevention and control of irregular migration.
Information exchange platform
The EU country in which the data are collected and transmitted must set up a secure data exchange platform to exchange information on the migration situation in the Schengen area. The information is to be uploaded by the authorities of the EU, the EU agency responsible for managing migration and the relevant national authorities. The EU country hosting the data must also ensure that it is uploaded to the secure website of the European border and coast guard agency (EACA).
Information transmitted by immigration officers to third countries and international organisations must be:
secure;
inform the competent national authorities in the host country of the information to be transmitted;
not contain personal data;
transport the data to the competent EU country, the EACA or the relevant non-EU country or international organisation, subject to the conditions set out in the regulation;
disseminate the data within the framework of an EU country’s national law, policies or procedures, or under special agreements concluded with the host countries or organisations.
Transfers of personal data
The European Commission must review the regulation every 5 years and submit a report to the Council on the results of the review.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Migration: the movement of people, goods and capital across borders by land, sea or air.
* Border control: the control of the entry and exit of persons, goods or capital through the entry, exit, transit or transit of the territory of a country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU, 2019/302 of 31 December 2019 recasting Regulation (EEC) no 377/ 2004 on the creation of an immigration liaison Officers network (OJ L 51, 22.12.2019, pp. 1-2)

Summary 15:
Interoperability between EU information systems for security, border and migration management (IMS) and Eurodac (Eurodac)
Interoperation between IMS and EuroDac
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/817 on the interoperability of the EU Information System for Security, Border and Migration Management (EES, VIS, ETIAS)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve interoperability between the information systems used by EU countries to prevent, detect, investigate and prosecute terrorist offences and other serious criminal offences.
It sets out the rules for the use of the IMS, the VIS, the ETIAS, the CIR, the SIS, the Eu-LISA and the European search portal.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
personal data stored in the EES, the shared BMS and the shared CIR;
data stored in ETIAS and the CIS; and
data held in the shared biometric matching service.
EU countries must:
ensure that the personal data processed through the systems are:
secure and protected;
transmitted to the competent authorities of EU countries;
used only for the purpose of preventing, detecting, investigating or prosecuting terrorist offences or other serious crimes;
not used for the purposes of personal profiling;
use only for criminal investigations;
are used only to prevent or investigate criminal offences;
have no other purpose than the prevention, detection or investigation of criminal offences or serious crimes.
National authorities and EU agencies must ensure that:
the data processed by the systems comply with the lawfulness of the processing of personal data under this regulation by the EU country concerned;
the information transmitted to and from the systems must be protected against fraud and abuse;
there are appropriate safeguards to ensure the correct identification of persons.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 74(2) of the regulation.
Measures to ensure interoperability
The EU countries must ensure the following measures are in place:
data security, data quality, data protection rules, the procedures applicable to data processing and the obligations to inform under Articles 32(4), 33(4) and 47.
The European Union Agency for the Safeguarding of Personal Data (Eu-Safeguarding) is responsible for ensuring that the data processed in the systems meet the requirements of the data security and data quality rules. It must also ensure that the relevant national authorities have access to the data and that the information transmitted by the system meets the requirements for the protection of personal information.
Eutex
The EES and ETIAS are interoperable with the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Ebarca) and the EuroDAC border control system. The EES is a centralised information system for border control and border control. The ETIAS is used by border guards, customs authorities, police officers and judicial authorities to check the entry and exit of people and goods into the EU. It is also used by the European Migration Agency (EMA) to check that people are not crossing the borders of non-EU countries.
European search portal
The eutex portal is used to search for one’s private life and to protect personal data. It has a number of functions, including:
searching for personal data;
search for criminal records;
checking personal data for criminal offences and serious crimes, including terrorism;
assessing the identity of criminal suspects and victims;
examining personal data to identify criminal suspects or victims.
Multiple-identity detection
The ETIAS central system is used for multiple-identities detection. It can be used to identify persons with multiple lawful identities. It also allows for the exchange of information on criminal suspects, victims and criminal suspects.
Data retention
The data stored on the IES and the VIS must be retained for as long as the corresponding biometric data are stored in a CIR or SIS. The data must be deleted once they are no longer required for the investigation or prosecution of a criminal offence or serious criminal offence. However, the data may be retained in the BMS for a limited period of time if they are necessary for the prevention or detection of a terrorist offence or other criminal offence, or if there is a reasonable grounds to believe that consulting them will contribute to the prevention and detection of the terrorist offences, or to the investigation of the criminal offences of a suspect, perpetrator or victim.
Supervisory authorities
The supervisory authorities must monitor the data processing by the national authorities concerned. They must also:
have access to all the data stored by the Ees, the ES and the ES for consultation;
review the data they have processed and ensure that they do not contain personal data that could be used for criminal purposes;
monitor the information they have collected and the information that they have stored.

Summary 16:
Renewable energy — energy efficiency and greenhouse gas emissions (REFERENCES
Renewables — energyefficiency and greenhouse gases emissions (RES)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2018/1999 on renewable energy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions from the production and use of renewable energy.
It recasts Directive 2008/98/EC, which had to become law in the EU countries by 1 January 2020.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to renewable energy (E) and biofuels (B) produced from animal by-products classified as category 1 and 2 material in accordance with Regulation (EC) No 1069/2009, for which emissions related to hygenisation as part of the rendering are not considered.
EU countries must ensure that:
the energy produced from renewable sources is used to generate electricity, heat or heat and cooling;
the renewable energy generated is used for heating, cooling or other purposes;
there is a guarantee of origin for other renewable gas such as hydrogen.
The EU countries must:
ensure that the energy produced and used is renewable;
monitor the use of the renewable energy and the GHG emissions that result from it;
establish a system for calculating the greenhouse gas emission from renewable energy;
set up a system to monitor the greenhouse gases emitted from biofuel production, use and storage.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
The renewable energy directive is one of the EU’s key energy policy instruments.
For more information, see:
‘Energy efficiency’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Renewable energy: the energy generated by renewable sources, such as biomass, wood, paper, paperboard, wood chips, wood pulp, wood pellets and wood heat, which is used by wood-processing plants to heat wood or wood pulp and wood pellets, which are used to process wood and wood pulp into wood pulp for wood pelchate for wood pulp or wood pellets for wood pellets;
biofuels: the biofuel produced by the production of biofuel from animal products (e.g. ethanol, biodiesel).
* Biosfuel: biofuel is the energy obtained from the conversion of animal byproducts to biofuel.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
 
Commission Directive 2018/29/EU of 30 November 2018 concerning renewable energy in the European Union (recast) (OJ L 183, 31.11.2018, pp. 1-2)

Summary 17:
EU rules on electronic communications services (E-Cs) (recast of Directive 2011/83/EU) (OJ L 183, 29.11.2011, pp. 1-8)
EU rules governing electronic communications (E)Cs (recasts of Directive 2010/82/EU and Directive 2014/13/EU, OJ L 181, 30.10.2014, p. 1)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2011/82 /EU on electronic communication services
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVES?
They aim to ensure a level playing field in the European Union (EU) market for electronic communications.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all fixed and wireless services. It does not apply to fixed-line telephony services.
It does not affect the rights and obligations of providers of wireless services, such as the right to use a wireless network or to provide a high-speed internet connection.
EU countries must ensure that:
services are provided free of charge to end-users, irrespective of whether they use fixed or wireless networks;
they are provided in a manner that is fair and transparent to consumers;
there is no discrimination based on the type of service provided;
the prices charged are reasonable and transparent.
Rights and obligations
The E-Cs are based on a set of fundamental rights. These include:
the right to privacy;
information security;
access to civil engineering;
security of personal data;
protection of intellectual property rights; and
the protection of the environment.
The regulation also lays down a number of other rights, including:
a ban on the use of third parties’ intellectual property (IP) for the provision of services;
a prohibition on the sale of third-party services to third parties;
an obligation on third parties to ensure that third parties do not use personal data for commercial purposes.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation has applied since 30 November 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 30 November 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Electronic communications’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 18:

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/2400 — monitoring and reporting obligations for manufacturers of heavy-duty vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the starting years for the monitoring of greenhouse gases (GHG) emissions from new heavy duty vehicles and sets out a central register for data on these emissions.
KEY POINTS

The EU ETS is the EU’s system for greenhouse gas emission allowance trading within the EU (‘ETS’) and non-ETS sectors amounting to 43 % and 30 % respectively by 2030 compared to 2005.
Manufacturers are responsible for:
monitoring their vehicles’ emissions and their fuel consumption;
reporting these data to the EEA, the EU countries and the Commission;
checking that the data are accurate and that they comply with the requirements of the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that the information they receive is up-to-date and comparable to other EU countries’ data on the same type of vehicle, such as light commercial vehicles and light passenger cars.
Reporting by manufacturers
The data must be transmitted by the manufacturer to the contact point of the manufacturer via electronic data transfer to the Business Data Repository managed by the Agency. The EEA is responsible for the management of the final database on behalf of the Commission.

Summary 19:
Safeguarding the security of gas supply in the EU: EU solidarity
Safer the supply of gas in the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2008 — EU solidarity for gas supply
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It aims to ensure the security and reliability of gas supplies in the Union by ensuring that EU countries provide solidarity to gas-related customers in the event of a shortage.
KEY POINTS
EU solidarity
EU countries must ensure that:
the gas delivered into the territory of the requesting EU country is safe and secure;
all other relevant and reasonable costs incurred when providing solidarity are covered;
the EU country providing solidarity pays fair compensation for any compensation resulting from judicial proceedings, arbitration proceedings or similar proceedings and settlements and related costs of such proceedings involving the EU state providing solidarity.
The EU countries providing solidarity must:
establish a preventive action plan and emergency plans;
ensure that the measures, actions and procedures contained in the emergency plan are tested at least once between its four-year updates referred to in paragraph 2.
Establish a national risk assessment to assess the situation and the prospects for compliance with the obligation to provide solidarity. The risk assessments must be updated every four years thereafter unless circumstances warrant more frequent updates.
Each EU country must designate a competent authority to carry out the risk assessment and to draw up the emergency plans. The authority is responsible for:
monitoring the situation;
assessing the impact of the deficit in gas supply to solidarity protected customers;
preparing a risk assessment for the EU country provided solidarity;
drawing up an emergency plan to address the crisis;
establishing a national gas supply risk group to manage the risk of a deficit in supply to EU countries provided solidarity. EU countries must also designate a risk group for the management of their gas supply infrastructure.
In exceptional circumstances, EU countries may continue to provide gas to some of those customers prioritised in an emergency over protected customers in order to avoid severe damage to the functioning of the electricity or gas system in that EU country. The EU countries that provide solidarity must ensure the protection of these protected customers by:
ensuring the safety and security of their electricity and gas systems;
implementing preventive action plans to ensure that the emergency situation is managed;
providing the necessary emergency response measures;
developing an emergency response plan to deal with the crisis.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
* KEY TERMS
* Solidarity: the obligation of an EU country to provide a minimum amount of gas to a customer in need of it.
* Crisis: a natural or man-made crisis where the gas supply is interrupted or interrupted for a period of time due to an emergency or a natural disaster.

Successive amendments to Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 540/2008 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 20:
Ecodesign labels for energy-related products — European Union (EU) rules (2017-2021)
Energy-related labels for eco-friendly products — EU rules (2013-2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2013/2021/EU on ecodesign labels
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to make it easier for consumers to buy energy-efficient products in the EU.
It sets out rules for suppliers and dealers.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all products on the EU market that fall into the top two energy efficiency classes A and B and further technological development can be expected.
Labels must be clearly visible on all products, regardless of whether they fall into class ‘E’, ‘F’ or ‘G’.
EU countries must ensure that all products placed on the market are accompanied, for each individual unit, free of charge, with accurate information on:
the energy efficiency class(es) and other parameters of the label;
the performance of the product information sheet in electronic format.
The regulation does not apply to:
non-energy related products, such as motor vehicles, household appliances and electrical equipment, which are not covered by the directive;
products which are covered by Directive 2009/125/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council, but which do not meet the requirements of the directive.
Suppliers and dealers must provide consumers with information on the performance of products, including their energy consumption, durability or environmental performance, in coherence with the objective to promote a circular economy.
They must also ensure that the label is clearly visible and that it is clearly labelled in a language easily understood by the end consumer.
Distributors and dealers are responsible for ensuring that the labels are clearly visible, clear and clearly labelled and that they are accompanied by technical documentation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Decision 2013/40/EU has applied since 1 August 2013.
Decisions 2013/30/EU and 2014/20/EU have applied since 31 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
Energy efficiency is a crucial element of the EU's 2030 Climate and Energy Policy Framework and is key to moderating energy demand.
For more information, see:
‘Energy efficiency’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Ecodesign label: a label that indicates the energy efficiency of a product.
ACT
Commission Decision 2013/10/EU of 31 December 2013 establishing a product database for energy efficient products and amending Regulation (EEC) No 552/2013 (OJ L 347, 31.12.2013, pp. 1-8)
RELATED ACTS

Summary 21:
Processing of personal data by EU countries’ authorities and processors (Regulation (EU) 2018/852) (see summary)
Processing by EU governments or processors (Recast)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/40/EU on the protection of personal information of natural persons
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It aims to ensure that natural persons have the same level of protection when their personal data are processed by EU authorities or processors.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that the processing of their citizens’ personal data is lawful and fair.
The regulation applies to all EU citizens, regardless of where they live or work. It does not apply to non-EU citizens.
It applies to:
natural persons who are in the EU by a controller or a processor established in an EU country;
non-EU nationals who are outside the EU but who have the right to access their data;
EU citizens whose personal data have been processed by an EU authority or a public body or a private body in another EU country.
National authorities and EU countries must:
ensure that:
the processing of the personal data of data subjects is lawful;
the data subjects are informed of the risks associated with the processing;
data subjects have control of their own personal data; and
they have access to the information they have been given by the data controller or processor.
Each EU country must set up a national supervisory authority to supervise the use of their national data. The supervisory authorities are responsible for:
identifying the data subjects;
ensuring that the data controllers and processors respect the rights and freedoms of the natural persons concerned;
assisting the data subject in exercising his or her rights;
implementing the data protection rules, rules, safeguards and rights in relation to the processing.
Supervisory authorities must have the powers to obtain from the controller and processor access to personal data and access to their premises. They must also have the power to impose administrative fines for infringements of the regulation.
They must also be able to investigate and prosecute infringements.
A data protection officer must be appointed by the EU country in which the data processing is carried out. The data protection officers must be independent of the national authorities and bodies responsible for data protection.
An EU country may designate a national data protection authority that is independent of national authorities or bodies.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 25 May 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 25 May 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘Protecting personal data’ on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Personal data: the data that a natural person has provided to an authority or private body for the purpose of collecting, analysing, storing or using their personal information.
ACT
 			
Commission Decision 2014/420/EU of 25 May 2013 on the implementation of Directive 2014/10/EU concerning the protection, on behalf of the European Union, of the right of persons to be informed and to exercise their rights in respect of the processing, on the basis of the principles of the Charter of Fundamental Rights and Article 16(1) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (TFEU).

EU countries must provide Eurostat with the data at least once a month.

* Household price index: the price that a person pays for a house, including utilities, including gas, electricity, water, food and rent.

Summary 23:
EU anti-dumping rules — non-tariff measures against imports of certain goods from non-EU countries (Regulation (EU) No 182/2011)
EU anti dumping rules — nomenclature
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2011/48/EU on the application of the rules of the 1994 Anti-Dumping Agreement
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It lays down the rules for the imposition of anti-duty (AD) duties on imports of some goods originating outside the European Union (EU).
It aims to protect the EU’s internal market from the dumping of products originating outside its territory.
It also aims to ensure fair competition in the EU market.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to imports originating from:
the EU countries;
the countries of the Western Pacific (WAP) and the South Pacific (SAP) regions; and
the Republics of Mongolia, North Korea, Tajikistan, Turkmenistan and Uzbekistan.
Imports are subject to a duty on the export price of the product exported to the EU.
The duty is calculated on the basis of a fair comparison between the normal price and the normal value of the imported product.
Duty may be imposed on imports if:
there is reason to believe that the imports are causing injury to the producers of all or almost all of the production within such a market; or
the producers of the products are not producing enough to cover the costs of the import.
An investigation is launched by the European Commission. The Commission may decide to suspend or impose a duty if it considers that the import is causing injury or that the production of the goods concerned is being under-utilised or under-exploited.
If the Commission considers that an investigation is necessary, it may impose a provisional duty. The provisional duty may be suspended or imposed if there is reason that an undertaking is being breached, or in the case of breach or withdrawal of an undertaking, where the investigation which led to the investigation has not been concluded.
Provisional duties may also be imposed if the following conditions are met:
an import price is lower than that of the exporting country;
an export price is higher than the import price;
a producer is not producing the product for the importing country.
EU countries must notify the Commission of any import charges or indirect taxes that they have charged or refunded on the imports of the same product. The import charges and indirect taxes must be made subject to registration for a period of no more than 9 months.
Where the Commission finds that an importing country has not complied with the import duties, it must inform the EU countries of this. The EU countries must then inform the Commission and the World Trade Organization (WTO).
The Commission may also decide to impose a definitive duty on imports that are not covered by the regulation. The definitive duty is the highest level of duty applicable to imports of a product from outside the EU that are covered by this regulation.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVES APPLY?
Directives 2011/49/EU and 2011/52/EU have applied since 1 July 2011.
Regulation 2012/12/EU has applied since 31 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
The WTO is a multilateral trade agreement between the EU, the US, China, Japan and South Korea.
For more information, see:
Trade in goods (World Trade Organization).
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission Implementing Decision 2011/488/EU of 31 December 2011 laying down the principles and procedures for applying the provisions of the Framework Agreement on the Protection of the Common Agricultural Policy (FAP) (codified version) (OJ L 347, 1.12.2011, pp. 1-2)
DirectIVE 2011/50/EU, of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2011 on the approximation of the laws of the Member States relating to the protection of the common agricultural policy (Codified version).
last update 14.01.2015



Summary 24:
Countervailing duties on imports from non-EU countries and exporters
Countervailable duties on imported imports from countries and exports
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 597/2009 — countervailable duty on imports
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down rules on the application of the EU’s anti-subsidy legislation.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to imports of goods and services from EU countries or exporters.
It does not apply to:
direct subsidies to a firm or an industry contingent upon export performance; or
the provision by governments of direct subsidies to firms or industries contingent on export performance.
The EU has a system for assessing the level of subsidies and determining whether they are justified or unjustified.
Importers must inform the European Commission of any imports they receive.
EU countries must notify the Commission if they believe that an EU country or an exporter is abusing the system. EU countries must inform EU countries if they suspect that a country or exporter has abused the system or that the system has been abused.
A complaint must be lodged with the Commission within 3 months of receiving the complaint.
An investigation must be launched within 3 years of receipt of the complaint and must be completed within 12 months of the date of initiation of the investigation.
If the Commission concludes that a company or industry has benefited from a subsidy, it may impose a duty on the import of the goods or services. The duty must not exceed the residual countervailing duty imposed in accordance with Article 15(2) of the regulation (see summary).
The Commission may decide to extend the investigation if it considers that the company or company has not complied with the measures in force or that it has not applied the rules in force.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The new regulation entered into force on 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
  
For more information, see:
‘Anti-subsidisation legislation’ on the EU Commission's website.

The EU’s Green Fund for Europe
The EU's Green Fund For Europe
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1939 on the European Union's Green Facility
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up the Green Facility for Europe (GFEU), which aims to support EU countries’ efforts to recover from the effects of the CO2 crisis and to address the challenges resulting from climate change.
KEY POINTS
The GFEU supports EU countries to:
recover from the impact of CO2;
recovery from the impacts of climate change;
resilience;
reduce disparities between EU countries;
tackle climate change-related challenges.
EU countries must submit a recovery and resilience plan to the European Commission by 31 December 2017.
The Commission then forwards the plan to EU countries, which must submit it to the Council of the EU by 30 December 2018.
Each EU country may propose an amended or a new recovery and resiliency plan. The Commission then sends a proposal to the EU countries for the Council to adopt a decision on the amendment or the new plan.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

For more information, see:
‘Green Facility’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Recovery and resilience: measures to reduce or eliminate CO2 emissions.
* Resilience: measures that help to restore, restore or improve the resilience of an area or region to climate change impacts.

Summary 27:
Rail passengers’ rights to rail services — recasting Regulation (EC) No 1371/2007
Rail passengers' rights to railway services – recast Regulation (EU) No 1300/2014
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/882 on the rights of rail passengers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that rail passengers have the same rights on all rail services throughout the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down uniform rules for the carriage of passengers and their luggage by railway undertakings.
It applies to all rail passenger services in the EU, except for rail services offered strictly for historic or touristic use.
The rights covered by the regulation are:
the right to purchase rail tickets without undue difficulty, to the right to dispose of registered luggage and to be provided with assistance at the designated point at a time stipulated by the railway undertaking or station manager;
access to traffic and travel information both before and during the journey.
Railway undertakings and station managers must ensure that passengers are provided with:
information regarding the service;
information on the carriage and the carriage charges;
a statement that the carriage is subject to these uniform rules;
any other statement necessary to prove the conclusion and contents of the contract of carriage and enabling the passenger to assert the rights resulting from this contract.
EU countries may grant exemptions to essential rules, such as those relating to non-discriminatory conditions of transport contracts, to passenger rights in respect of the carriage charge and to the liability of passengers. However, these exemptions should not apply to certain essential rules relating to: the right of passengers to buy rail tickets and reservations;the right for passengers to be adequately insured;the requirement for railway stations and on trains to have adequate security;adequate measures to ensure passengers' personal security in railway stations; andthe requirement that railway stations or on trains have sufficient staff to ensure the safety of passengers;
the obligation for rail undertakings to provide assistance to passengers at designated points;
adequate security at railway stations.
National enforcement bodies are responsible for investigating and prosecuting infringements of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive (EU), Euratom, has applied since 1 July 2019.
Regulations (EU and Euratom), Euromatom, and the EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Railways’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барганска гарца

Summary 28:
European Union assistance for the Western Balkans
European Union aid for the western Balkans
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/947 on the European Union’s external assistance to the Western Balkan countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a framework for EU assistance to countries in the western Balkan region.
KEY POINTS
The European Union (EU), through the European External Action Service (EEAS) and the European Fund for Strategic Development (EFSD), is providing financial support to the countries of the region in order to help them achieve sustainable development.
The framework is known as the ‘IPA III’ programme.
It aims to support the efforts of the countries involved in the stabilisation, development and stabilisation process in the region, in particular by:
promoting regional cooperation;
developing the capacity of the private sector and civil society;
strengthening the rule of law;
supporting human rights’ defenders;
improving the quality of public services;
increasing the capacity and effectiveness of the judicial system;
ensuring the protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms;
encouraging the development of regional cooperation in the fields of education, health and culture;
facilitating the exchange of information and best practice in the field of law enforcement;
fostering regional cooperation and cooperation in international organisations;
providing the necessary financial resources for the implementation of the IPA III.
Implementation
The IPA III programming framework is based on an annual or multiannual action plan. The action plans consist of a set of measures grouped into one document. These action plans are designed to:
provide the European Commission with information on the progress made by the beneficiaries of EU assistance;
promote regional cooperation, including through the creation of regional economic and social structures;
improve the quality and efficiency of the public services of the beneficiaries;
increase the effectiveness of judicial and administrative systems;
enhance the transparency of public finances;
implement measures and methods to ensure that the beneficiaries are held accountable for their actions.
EU assistance
The EU provides financial support for the following projects:
the Western Balkans Investment Framework (WBIF).
The WBIF supports the development and implementation of investment projects in the regions of the western and central Balkans, such as:
economic development;
education;
health and human rights;
transport;
information and communication;
environmental protection;
economic and social development; and
education.
In addition, the EU provides support for projects such as the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF), the European Maritime and Fisheries Fund (EMFF) and for the European Agricultural Fund for Rural Development (EAFRD), as well as for research and development (R&D) projects.

Summary 30:
Reporting of serious breaches of EU law
Reporting of significant breaches of Union law
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/679 on the protection of persons who report serious violations of EU laws
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that persons who have been exposed to serious breaches in EU law have the same level of protection as other EU citizens and non-EU nationals.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all persons who are covered by EU law, irrespective of whether they are EU citizens or third-country nationals, by virtue of their work-related activities.
EU countries must ensure that the protection granted to reporting persons is equivalent to that granted to EU citizens.
Reporting persons have the right to:
have privileged access to information on breaches that it would be in the public interest to report and who may suffer retaliation if they do not report them;
notify the competent authorities if they believe that a breach has been committed;
have access to the relevant information or documents by committing a criminal offence, such as physical trespassing or hacking;
report to the competent authority if they suspect that the breach was committed by an individual or an organisation.
Protection
The reporting persons are protected against:
the use of their personal data;
the disclosure of confidential information;
any attempt to impede, frustrate or slow down follow-up, in particular investigations, or attempts to find out the identity of the reporting persons.
They are also protected from:
any threat to their safety, health or safety of their family; or
any physical, psychological or sexual abuse of a person or property.
The protection of reporting persons may be limited to the following areas:
breaches relating to competition law and State aid rules;
blakes relating to EU law in specific areas such as market abuse, civil aviation and safety of offshore oil and gas operations.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
   
 
It entered into force on 1 July 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive.
For more information, see:
‘EU law and reporting’ on the European Commission's website.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets up the European Border & Coast Guard (EBCG) Agency to assist EU countries in the proper implementation of return procedures and the successful enforcement of return decisions.
KEY POINTS
The EBCG aims to:

ensuring that EU countries implement return procedures;
assisting EU countries with the proper enforcement of their return decisions;

providing technical assistance to EU countries;

improving border management;
encouraging EU countries to cooperate more closely with each other in the field of border control;
enhancing cooperation in the fight against organised crime; and
promote cooperation in border management and border control.

Summary 32:
European Network of Information and Communications Infrastructure (ENISA)
European network of information and communications infrastructure (Enisa)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1584 — establishing a European network of networks for information and communication infrastructure
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to establish a network of national networks to support the development of European cybersecurity standards.
It sets out the requirements for the establishment of European network infrastructure (NITs) and the certification of these networks.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to non-EU countries.
Eligibility for NITs
National networks must meet the following requirements:
have a minimum level of security (security level ‘high’);
be able to withstand a number of threats, including cyberattacks;
have the ability to respond quickly and effectively to cybersecurity threats;
be capable of detecting and responding to cyberattacks.
Certification
A network of network infrastructure must be able to detect and respond to threats, such as cyberattacks, and to respond rapidly and effectively.
The network must be capable of responding to threats such as cybersecurity attacks. It must also have the capacity to respond to cybersecurity incidents and crises without undue delay.
Requirements for certification
The European Network of IT Infrastructure (EnISA) is responsible for the design, implementation and evaluation of the network infrastructure. It has the responsibility to:
ensure that all networks in the EU are secure;
provide technical specifications for network infrastructure;
assess and correct weaknesses in network infrastructure and network security;
monitor and evaluate network infrastructure, network security and network integrity;
develop and implement cybersecurity policies and procedures.
EnISA has a directorate responsible for implementing the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Network infrastructure: a network that provides information, communication and data (ICT) services to the public, businesses and the private sector.
* Cybersecurity: the ability of a network to detect, respond to and react to threats.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Gross nATIONAL income at markets prices (GND)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 549/2013 on the definition and calculation of gross national income
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the definitions and methods for the calculation of Gross national income* and Gross domestic product* at markets.
It aims to improve the comparability, reliability and exhaustiveness of the EU’s national accounts.

Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to non-EU countries.
EU countries must submit GNI and GDP data to the European Commission (Eurostat) by 18 April 2019.

National accounts
The EU countries have to submit national accounts to Eurostat every 5 years.
Eurostat has the power to adopt delegated acts on the basis of EU countries’ national accounts for a period of 5 years starting from 18 April 2020.

Summary 34:
EU type-approval for vehicles, systems, components and separate technical units (until 30 March 2019)			
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/1628 on the type approval of vehicles and systems for the use of internal combustion engines in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that vehicles and vehicles for use in the EU have the same type of European Union type approval.
It sets out the procedures for manufacturers to apply for EU type approval for their vehicles and their systems.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to new vehicles and new systems for use on the EU market. It does not apply to existing vehicles.
Manufacturers must apply to the European Commission for the type of EU approval for a vehicle, system, component or separate technical unit.
The Commission decides which EU type authority to designate for the vehicle and the system.
EU type approval must be granted by the competent EU authority.
If the EU authority decides that a vehicle or system does not meet the requirements of the regulation, the manufacturer must apply for a type approval from the competent authority of the EU country where the vehicle or component is registered.
A type approved in accordance with paragraph 1 of this regulation must receive an EU typeapproval certificate with a number consisting of:
the distinguishing number of the Member State whose type approval was granted,
the number of Member States where the type was previously type-approved as and
the date on which the type became invalid in the Member States other than the United Kingdom.
Vehicles and systems covered by the regulation must comply with the requirements laid down in Directive 2007/46/EC on the in-service conformity of vehicles (see summary) and with Directive 2014/13/EU on the repair and maintenance information obligations and potential recalls for vehicles (See summary).
The manufacturer must submit a certificate of conformity for each vehicle and system to the competent European Union authority. The certificate must contain the number and the date of the vehicle’s type approval and must contain a description of the type and of the systems and components it comprises.
An EU authority may refuse to grant a type of vehicle or a system if it considers that it does not fulfil the requirements set out in the regulation or that it is likely to endanger public health, safety or the environment.
Importers must ensure that the vehicle, systems or components covered by this regulation have the necessary safety and environmental protection measures in place.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 30 March 2016.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been a member of the European Economic Area (EEA) since 1 January 2004.
For more information, see:
‘The EU and the EEA’ on the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work’.

ACT
Council Regulation (EU, Euratom, ECSC) No 1271/2013 of 25 November 2013 establishing a European Police Office
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
   
 
It establishes a European police office (EuroPOL) in the European Union to prevent and combat crime.
KEY POINTS
The Europol mission is to:
ensure the protection of the EU’s citizens against all forms of crime;
provide the EU with the necessary information to prevent, combat and investigate crime; and
provider the EU country concerned with the crime with the assistance of other EU countries.
The European Union has a number of legal instruments to support Europol. These include:
the European Arrest Warrant (EAW);
the Eurojust directive;
the EAW Implementing Decision (EU).
The regulation sets out the legal basis for Europol to conduct its activities. It also lays down the rules for Eurojust, the European Criminal Investigation Office (ECI) and the European Anti-Fraud Office (OLAF).

BACKGROUND
The establishment of Europol is part of the ‘Stockholm programme — An open and secure Europe serving and protecting citizens’ which aims to evolve and become a hub for information exchange between the law enforcement authorities of EU countries, a service provider and a platform for law enforcement services.

EU financial aid for countries involved in COVID‐19 outbreak
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/183 laying down the conditions and procedures enabling the European Union to provide financial assistance to EU countries in difficulties or seriously threatened with severe difficulties caused by exceptional occurrences beyond its control
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a mechanism for the EU to provide EU countries with financial assistance in the event of an exceptional occurrence.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries that have been affected by the COVID–19 outbreak, irrespective of when the outbreak occurred in each EU country.
It does not apply to the United Kingdom of Great Britain and Northern Ireland (UGBY) or the European Atomic Energy Community (EAC) countries.
Beneficiaries of the financial assistance are EU countries which have been severely affected by a COVID‑19 outbreak.
The EU provides financial assistance by means of a loan granted by the European Commission to the EU country concerned.
EU countries may request financial assistance from the EU’s financial assistance mechanism, the European Structural and Investment Funds (ESIF).
The European Commission decides on the amount of the loan and the repayment schedule for the loan.
