Summary 37:
Resolution of a failing credit-clearing clearing house (CCP) — rules for the resolution of its insolvency and recovery plans
Resolving a failing Credit-Clearing House (CPS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/2365 on resolution of insolvencies and the recovery plans of failing credit clearing houses (CCPs)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the principles and procedures for resolving insolvent credit trading houses in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries and non-EU countries that have a national law on the resolution or recovery of insolvent Credit Clearing Houses.
It does not apply to the EU’s Member States.
The resolution of a CCP under resolution must be carried out by the competent national authority of the EU country in which the CCP is located.
EU countries must ensure that the resolution authority has the powers to:
impose a recovery plan for the CCP;
ensure the full allocation of uncovered losses to clearing members, and to their clients if those clients are direct creditors of the CCP.
Implementing the resolution plan
The recovery plan must include a framework of indicators based on the risk profile of the CCP and identify the circumstances under which measures in the recovery plan are to be taken.
A resolution authority may suspend any payment or delivery obligations of both counterparties to any contract entered into by a CCP if it considers that the CCP may be failing or likely to fail.
the resolution authority must also suspend the payment of any outstanding contractual obligations of the clearing members.
An early intervention measure must be in place to prevent the CCP from failing or failing to comply with its obligations.
In the event of a failure or failure to meet its obligations, a resolution authority is responsible for the early intervention measures.
Resolution plans must include:
a recovery plan;
a description of how the resolution will be implemented;
an assessment of the risk that any of its clearing members brings to the CCP, which the resolution authorities considers to have the potential to trigger a default procedure;
measures to ensure that all of its members in recovery are paid the full amount of the uncovered losses that they have been entitled to.
Regulatory technical standards for the application of the ‘no creditor worse off’ principle must be set out in the resolution plans.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2014/60/EU on the European System for the Resolution of Clearing House Delegated Regulation (EC) No 648/2012 (see summary) has applied since 12 February 2015.
Directives 2014/40/EU and 2014/50/EU have applied since 1 February 2016.
Amending Directive (EU, Euratom) 2018/852 amends Directive 2014/30/EU to set up a European system for the Recovery of Cleared Houses (ESR) and amends Regulation (EU), Euratom and EU countries’ national laws on the recovery of CCPs.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to improve the functioning of its financial markets, including the creation of a single European clearing house.
For more information, see:
‘Resolution and recovery plan’ (European Commission).

Summary 40:
Reclaimed water for agricultural irrigation
Reused water for agriculture
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1069/2009 on water reuse
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries can reuse treated urban waste water from waste water treatment plants for agricultural purposes.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out minimum requirements for reclamation of treated urban wastewater to be reused, in accordance with Directive 91/271/EEC, for agricultural use.
It applies to all EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that:
the reclamation facility operator complies with the following:
minimum requirements for water quality laid down in Section 2 of Annex I of the regulation;
any additional conditions set by the competent authority in the relevant permit pursuant to points (c) and (d) of Article 6(3), as regards water quality.
The permit must be issued by an EU country’s competent authority and must be valid for at least 5 years. It must be reviewed as necessary, in particular taking into account climate change projections and national climate change adaptation strategies, and at least every 6 years.
Reclamation facility operators must:
ensure that, at the point of compliance, reclaimed water intended for agriculture irrigation meets the following requirements:
a minimum level of water quality;
the minimum levels of environmental protection;
all other conditions set out in the regulation are met;
there is no risk to the environment or to human or animal health.
the permit is valid for 5 years and may be suspended if the reclamation facilities do not comply with the above requirements.
If a facility operator fails to comply with these requirements, the permit is suspended and the water is used for other purposes, such as drinking water.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
Directive 2009/40/EC has applied since 1 July 2009.
Directives 2009/30/EC and 2011/92/EU have applied since 31 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a long history of water scarcity and droughts.
For more information, see:
‘The EU's water crisis’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 41:
Beneficiaries of the right to free movement — EU blue card (EU) (European Commission)
Beneficiés of the rights to free mobility — EU Blue card (European Commision)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2011/95/EU — granting highly qualified third-country nationals the right of free movement within the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that highly qualified non-EU nationals who are beneficiaries of international protection* have the same rights as EU citizens when they move to an EU country.
KEY POINTS
EU blue card
The directive applies to highly qualified nationals who have been granted international protection (i.e. protection from persecution, war or natural disaster) by an EU Member State.
The beneficiaries of this right are entitled to:
the right to live, work and study in the Member State in which they are resident;
a residence permit valid for at least 24 months in that Member State;
access to the labour market and to the services of an employer in the same Member State as those of EU citizens;
the protection of the health and safety of the third country nationals concerned;
equal treatment with EU citizens as regards recognition of professional qualifications, in accordance with applicable Union and national law.
EU countries may reject an application for an EU Blue Card if:
a Member State considers that the applicant’s documents have been fraudulently acquired, falsified or tampered with; or
the third country national concerned is considered to pose a threat to public policy, public security or public health.
A Member State may also refuse to issue an EU blue Card if the applicant has been convicted of a criminal offence or has been found to have committed a serious breach of public order or public security.
An EU country may, however, issue a new EU bluecard if it considers that a Member State has failed to fulfil its obligations under the directive.
If an employer refuses to issue a blue card, the employer may be subject to sanctions. These sanctions must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2012.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘EU blue cards’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  базданска гарада

Summary 42:
European Union Integration Fund (EUIF) (European Commission)
European Union Initiative for Integration — European Union Fund (Eurofin)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/523 establishing the EU Integration Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the European Union (EU)* Integration Fund.
It aims to support integration measures that are tailored to the needs of non-EU nationals, as well as horizontal measures aimed at building EU countries’ capacity to develop integration strategies, to strengthen exchange and cooperation, and to promote contact, constructive dialogue and acceptance between third-country nationals and the receiving society.
KEY POINTS
The EU integration fund is funded by the EU’s general budget. It is managed by the European Commission.
The European Commission manages the fund.
EU countries are responsible for implementing the EU integration funds. The Commission is responsible for managing the fund and for the implementation of the EU investment programmes.
Funds are allocated according to the following criteria:
the number of EU nationals who are eligible to benefit from EU integration measures;
the type of integration measures and horizontal measures that the EU countries are able to develop; and
the degree to which the EU is able to integrate the EU nationals involved in the projects.
Projects must be implemented by EU countries in the following areas:
integration measures:
support for the development of integration strategies;
exchange and cooperation between EU countries and third countries;
interoperability of EU integration policies;
cooperation between the EU and third country nationals;
transport and mobility schemes;
education and training;
health and well-being;
sustainable development;
social protection;
environmental protection.
Programmes must be designed to:
promote the integration of EU citizens in the EU;
support the integration and integration of vulnerable persons;
encourage the exchange and exchange of information and skills;
provide support for the creation and implementation of horizontal integration measures.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 14 March 2021 and had to become law in the Member States by 14 March 2022.
BACKGROUND
EU integration fund
The Fund is part of the general EU investment programme, InvestEU.
For more information, see:
EU Integration Fund — European Commission (European Agency for Migration and Integration).

Summary 43:
Fathers’ right to parental leave — repealing Directive 2010/18/EU (Parental Leave Directive) (European Commission)
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
Directive 2019/24/EU on the right of fathers to take parental leave
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that fathers have the same rights as mothers when it comes to taking parental leave.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that mothers have the right to a minimum period of parental leave of 10 working days that is to be taken on the occasion of the birth of the worker's child.
This minimum period cannot be transferred from one parent to the other.
The minimum period must not be less than 4 months.
However, EU countries may decide to extend the minimum period to 6 months if they consider that the child is under the age of 6.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive entered into force on 2 August 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 2 August 2022.
BACKGROUND
Parental leave is one of the fundamental rights of women and men in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘The right to have a child’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 44:
Protecting the European Union’s financial interests: criminal offences and sanctions in the area of fraud affecting the EU's financial interests (Directive 2016/17/EU)
Protecting The European Union  
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2016/18/EU on the protection of the European Communities’ financial interests
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to protect the EU financial interests by establishing a common definition of fraud falling within the scope of this directive.
The directive applies to all revenues, expenditure and assets covered by, acquired through, or due to:
the EU budget;
the budgets of the EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies established pursuant to the Treaties or budgets directly or indirectly managed and monitored by them;
legal persons having legal personality under the applicable law, except for States or public bodies in the exercise of State authority and for public international organisations.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that legal persons can be held liable where the lack of supervision or control by a legal person has made possible the commission, by a person under its authority, of any of the criminal offences referred to in Article 3, 4 or 5 of the directive;
impose sanctions on legal persons if they are found to have committed or are suspected of committing any of these offences.
Sanctions must be effective and dissuasive. They must be proportionate to the damage or advantage caused by the fraud and must not be less than EUR 10 000 or an advantage of less than € 10 000. EU countries may also provide for sanctions other than criminal sanctions.
National authorities must ensure that the personal data of legal persons is processed by the competent national authorities and that the exchange of such data between EU countries on the one hand and between competent European Union bodies on the other.
Legal persons may not be held criminally liable if they:
have the right to have their personal data processed by national authorities;
have a legal personality in the EU;
are a natural person under the law of an EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2016. EU governments had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
    *   ‘Fraud’ is a term used to describe any fraudulent conduct with respect to the EU budget, including financial operations such as borrowing and lending activities.
For more information, see:
Fraud (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Fraud: any intentional or unintentional act or omission to commit a criminal offence.
* Legal person: an entity having legal person status under EU law.

  И   Д

Summary 45:
Interchange fees for card-based and internet and mobile payments in the European Union (EU) (Directive 2014/40/EU)
INTERFERENCE FEITS FOR CARD-based AND MOBILE PAYMENTS IN THE EU
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1093/2010 laying down uniform requirements for payment card interchange fees
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the interchange fees charged by payment card schemes and payment service providers for transactions carried out with any device on which their payment brand is present in the EU are uniform across the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all debit card transactions, including:
transactions with payment cards issued by a single payment card scheme or with debit card schemes issued by three parties;
the use of a debit card by a consumer in a non-EU country.
It does not apply to transactions for which their scheme is not used.
EU countries must ensure that:
the interchange fee charged by the card issuing and acquiring banks for each debit card transaction is uniform across all EU countries;
payment service providers do not charge a per transaction interchange fee of more than 0.2 % of the value of the transaction.
The interchange fee must be applied to all transactions, irrespective of the country in which the card is issued or the type of card in which it is issued.
In order to ensure uniform requirements, EU countries must collect information regarding the volume and value of all transactions within a card scheme, or of the transactions pertaining to one or more payment card providers. These data must be displayed prominently at the entrance of the shop and at the till.
Merchants and consumers must be able to choose a cheaper card brand on co-badged cards or to steer consumers to the use of such cheaper cards. The interchange fee should not be higher than the amount that the merchant would have to pay if the merchant were to compare the cost of a payment card with those of non-card (cash) payments (taking into account the fee for service paid to acquiring banks, i.e. the merchant service charge and the interchange fee).
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
Directive (EU), Euratom and the European Banking Authority (EBA) have to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU has been working towards an integrated market for electronic payments, with no distinction between national and cross-border payments.
For more information, see:
‘Euratom’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Implementing Decision 2014/420/EU of 30 November 2014 on the harmonisation of interchange fees for credit and debit cards in the Member States and repealing Regulation (EEC) No 552/2008 (OJ L 347, 31.11.2014, pp. 1-2)

SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/849 on the transmission of statistics on the traffic flows on the rail network
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries send the European Commission (Eurostat) reliable, up-to-date and comparable data on the transport of goods and passengers by rail across the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation requires EU countries to transmit the following types of data:
annual statistics on goods and passenger transport — detailed reporting (Annex I);

regional statistics on freight rail traffic flows;
statistics on traffic on the railway network.

goods transported in:
total tonnes

total passenger-km
total freight train movements
total train-km.
Transport of goods by rail:
the movement of goods using railway vehicles between the place of loading or unloading and the place where the goods are unloading;
transport of passengers using passenger trains between the point where the passengers are disembarked.
Railway undertakings that operate entirely or mainly within industrial and similar installations, including harbours, must be excluded from the scope of the regulation.

Summary 49:
Exemption from the visa requirement for nationals of non-EU countries (except the Schengen acquis) (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 539/2001 — the visa exemption from nationals of other EU countries (also referred to herein as ‘the visa exemption’) (codified version)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the conditions for the exemption from visa requirements for nationals from non-European Union (EU) countries.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must notify the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission within 30 days of the non-compliance of a third country with their visa requirement.
The EU countries must inform the European Commission of the reasons for the suspension of the exemption and the measures to be taken by the EU countries to ensure that the exemption is maintained.
Where a third EU country applies a visa requirement to nationals of at least one EU country, the following provisions apply:
the EU country concerned may suspend the exemption for no more than 90 days in any 180-day period, notwithstanding the possibility of having country-specific amendments to those annexes in particular circumstances, for instance as a result of a visa liberalisation process or as the ultimate consequence of a temporary suspension by the third country.
If the EU country suspends the exemption within 90 days, it must notify all EU countries of the reason for its suspension, including the Commission and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC).
The European Commission has the power to adopt implementing acts to extend the exemption. These implementing acts must be adopted in accordance with the examination procedure referred to in Article 11(2) of the regulation.
In the event of a suspension of an exemption, EU countries are required to notify the Commission, the EU Parliament and the Council of their reasons for suspending the exemption, and to inform the EEC of the measures taken to ensure its continuation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 9 January 2002.

Summary 53:
Injunurious pricing — EU countries’ obligations under the EU Shipbuilding Agreement
In injurious pricing – EU countries' obligations under their Shipbuilding Association Agreement
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — injurious price fixing
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It lays down the principles and definitions for the imposition of injurious prices on the EU shipbuilding industry.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
shipbuilding contracts between EU countries and non-EU countries;
contracts for the construction, maintenance, repair and overhaul of vessels;
the sale of vessels for the use of the EU industry; and
contractual penalties.
It applies from the date of entry into force of the shipbuilding agreement between the EU country and the shipbuilder.
EU countries must apply the principles laid down in the regulation.
Importers must ensure that:
the vessel is sold at a price below the normal value of the vessel’s export price;
there is no significant price undercutting by the sale at less than normal value as compared with the price of like vessels; or
the effect of such sale is otherwise to depress prices to a significant degree or prevent price increases, which would otherwise have occurred.
The EU country must notify the European Commission within 30 days of the sale of a vessel for the purpose of initiating an investigation into whether there has been any undercutting of the export price. The Commission then forwards the results of the investigation to the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA).
The Commission may decide to initiate an investigation if there is sufficient evidence of injury, injury, a causal link and that material injury will occur, unless protective action is taken to prevent such injury.
If the Commission concludes that there is no causal link, it must inform the EU countries of the reasons for initiating the investigation.
An EU country may refuse to enter into an agreement if it considers that the vessel has been sold for a price under which it has been undercutting the normal price of other vessels.
Injunctions
The European Commission may impose sanctions on a shipbuilder if:
it considers that an EU country has been the victim of an undercutting;
it believes that the alleged undercutting has resulted in material injury to the Union industry;
or
it suspects that an injury has occurred, or is likely to occur, if the alleged injury is imminent and that protective action has not been taken.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
Injurious pricing (European Commission).

Summary 54:
Cableway safety — EU countries’ national rules
Cabling safety – EU countries' national rules	SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/849 on the safety of cableway installations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out the safety requirements for the construction and operation of cableways in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
cableway installations located in the territory of an EU country;
substances and safety components intended for use on a cableway installation located in another EU country.
EU countries must lay down procedures for authorising the construction, the entry into service and the removal of cables or safety components from cableways located within their territory.
Implementing acts
The implementing acts must be adopted by EU countries by 21 March 2018. They must contain the following information:
the type of safety components and subsystems to be tested and certified;
the design and construction of the cableway;
a description of the risks to human health and the environment;
an analysis and safety report for planned cableways.
The EU countries must notify the competent national authority responsible for notifying and market surveillance of the safety and health of the components and substances covered by the regulation.
National authorities must inform the notifying authority of the following:
any refusal, restriction, suspension or withdrawal of a certificate or approval decision;
any request for information which they have received from market surveillance authorities regarding conformity assessment activities;
on request, conformity assessment results performed within the scope of their notification and any other activity performed, including cross-border activities and subcontracting.
Notified bodies must carry out the conformity assessment and issue the EU declaration of conformity on the basis of the results of the assessment.
If a notified body fails to comply with the regulation’s safety requirements, the EU country must take appropriate steps to ensure that the files of the notified body are either processed by another notified body or kept available for the responsible notifying authorities at their request.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 21 March 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 21 April 2018.
BACKGROUND
Cableways are the main means of transport in the EU by rail, road, air and sea.
For more information, see:
‘Safe cableways’ on the European Commission's website.
KEY TERMS
* Cableway: an installation designed and constructed to carry cables or their attachments.
* Substances: components used for the operation of cables.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, 21.3.2017, pp. 1-18
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom, Euratom) have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 55:
Accessibility of products and services for persons with disabilities (Directive 2017/1564/EU)
Assessment of the adequacy of the accessibility of products, services and information on the European Union’s internal market
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2017/1664 — assessment of accessibility requirements for products, service and information
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that products, including services, are accessible to people with disabilities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
products and services providing access to audiovisual media services, such as websites, online applications, set-top boxes and video conferencing services;
services for the production and distribution of audio and visual media;
products for the manufacture and sale of electronic equipment for the processing (including digital compression) and storage of data, and transmitted, conveyed and received in its entirety by wire, by radio, by optical means or by other electromagnetic means.
It does not apply to products designed and produced in such a way as to maximise their foreseeable use by persons with disability.
Accessibility requirements
Products and services must comply with the accessibility requirements set out in Annex I of the directive.
Products must meet the following minimum accessibility requirements:
the product must be accessible at a distance;
the service must be accompanied where possible in or on the product by accessible information on their accessibility features;
users must be able to use their assistive technologies to use the product;
they must have the ability to communicate with the product or to use its data plate.
Annex II sets out the general accessibility requirements related to all products covered by this directive in accordance with Article 2(1) of the Directive.
EU countries must ensure that economic operators only place on the market products and only provide services that comply with these accessibility requirements.
Distributors must ensure the product is in conformity with these requirements and that the service is provided on individual request.
The European Commission must monitor the implementation of this directive and report to the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee on the progress made by EU countries.
FROM WHEN DO THE DIRECTIVES APPLY?
Directives (EU, EFTA and EFTA countries) have to incorporate them into national law by 31 December 2018.
BACKGROUND
Accessible products
For more information, see:
‘Accessibility on the EU market’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 56:
EU system for supply chain due diligence for minerals and precious metals (TSD)
EU system to combat conflict and illegal mining
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2016/852 — EU system for Supply Chain Due Diligence for minerals, precious metals and gold
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to combat the illegal exploitation of minerals and metals in conflict-affected and high-risk areas.
It sets up a system to prevent the supply of these metals and minerals to armed groups and security forces.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the EU’s imports of tin, tantalum and tungsten, their ores, and gold.
EU countries must ensure that importers of minerals or metals who fall within the scope of the regulation carry out appropriate ex-post checks. These checks must be carried out by EU countries’ competent authorities.
Importers must provide the European Commission with a list of responsible smelters and refiners. The Commission must keep that list up-to-date.
The Commission must draw up a global list of EU importers. This list must be drawn up taking into account global responsible importers and the information provided by the EU countries to the Commission. EU countries must also provide the Commission with information on EU economic operators, including small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs), who are responsible for the supply chain of minerals, metals, ores and gold in the EU.
If the Commission considers that an EU importer does not comply with the regulation, it must inform the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for its decision. The European Parliament or the Council may adopt amendments to the regulation.
In addition, the Commission must provide EU countries with an annual report on the implementation of this regulation. This report must include information on the impact of the EU system on the EU economy and on the economic operators including SMEs, as well as the impact on EU social partners.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The EU system entered into force on 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The EU supply chain system for minerals’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барка болка  данска
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 852/2016 of 1 January 2016 establishing a Union system for Suppliers’ due diligence (‘EU system’) (OJ L 347, 1.1.2016, pp. 1-8)

Summary 57:
Customs debt on import — recast of Regulation (EU) 2013/852
Customs debts on import – recast
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2013 / 852 — recasting Regulation (Euratom) on the EU’s customs debt
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It recasts Regulation (EC) No 852/2012, which had to become law in the EU countries by 30 October 2013.
It aims to make it easier for EU countries to settle their customs debt on imports.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all goods imported into or exported from the EU by one of the following persons:
the person who brought the goods into the customs territory of the EU;
the persons in whose name or on whose behalf the goods were brought into that territory or the person who assumed responsibility for carriage of the goods after they were brought in.
Importation of goods by the customs authorities or in the free zone is subject to a customs duty on import or export. The duty is calculated on the basis of the tariff classification of goods and the value of the imported goods.
The import and export of goods must be accompanied by a customs declaration for release for free circulation or temporary admission with partial relief.
A customs declaration must contain the following information: the goods’ value, the date of entry into the EU, the goods entered the EU and the date the goods left the EU territory or were re-exported.
If the goods are imported by a third countries, the customs declaration is issued by the third country.
the goods are placed under a customs procedure or under a re-export procedure
The goods are put under a storage procedure or placed under an exit summary declaration.
An EU country may refuse to accept a declaration if it considers that the goods do not comply with the customs duty or the import/export procedure. If the declaration is refused, the EU country must pay the amount of the customs or export duty corresponding to the customs debt in full or in part.
EU countries may refuse the payment of a customs debt if they consider that the declaration does not meet the requirements of the law of the country in which it was issued or that it was placed under.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The new regulation entered into force on 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to deal with customs debt.
For more information, see:
EU customs debt (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Customs debt: a debt incurred by the importing or exporting country for the purpose of importing goods into or exporting them to the EU.
* Re-export declaration: a declaration issued by an EU country to a non-EU country to allow the goods to be exported.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
    баркова  
Commission Regulation 2013/884/EU of 29 October 2013 on the recast Regulation of the European Parliament and of the Council on the Union's customs debt and repealing Council Regulation (OJ C 229, 31.10.2013, pp. 1-8)

Summary 58:
Ensuring the enforcement of intellectual property rights in the EU — EU customs legislation (EU customs legislation)
Implementing the EU Customs legislation (Uruguay)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2004 — enforcing intellectual property right in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU customs authorities are able to enforce intellectual property (IP) rights when importing goods from non-EU countries.
It sets out the procedures and procedures for applying EU customs law.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to goods imported into the EU by means of a transit or transit through the territory of an EU country. It does not apply to goods intended for retail sale to the ultimate consumer.
EU countries must apply the procedures laid down in the regulation.
Customs authorities must:
grant a decision granting the right to compensation in the event that goods suspected of infringing an IP right are not detected by the customs authorities and are released or no action is taken to detain them.
The decision must contain the following information:
the container number;
the weight of the containers;
information on movements of containers, particular: the type of loading status; the location of final unloading;information on the status of the goods;actual date of arrival.
Decisions on applications
The competent customs department of the EU country to which an application has been submitted must forward the following decisions to the competent customs departments of the Member State or Member States indicated in the Union application, immediately after their adoption:
decisions granting the application;
decision revoking the decision.
Implementation of the decision
The customs authorities must carry out adequate customs controls and must take proportionate identification measures as provided for in Article 13(1) and Article 72 of Regulation (EEC) No 2913/92 in accordance with risk analysis criteria with a view to preventing acts in breach of EU intellectual property laws.
Inspection and sampling of goods whose release has been suspended or which have been detained
Inspections and sampling must be carried out at the point of entry or exit of the container. The container must be inspected and sampled at the place of final loading.
If the container is found to have infringed an intellectual property law, the customs authority must take the necessary measures to prevent further infringements.
Where the container has been released or detained, the goods must be destroyed or returned to the owner or the person responsible for the intellectual property.
An EU country must notify the European Commission of the measures taken by its customs authorities. The Commission must then inform the European Parliament, the Council, the European Economic and Social Committee and the Committee of the Regions of its measures taken.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 July 2004. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2005.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Ensuing Intellectual Property Rights’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барска гарка
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 550/2004 of 29 July 2004 on the enforcement by the Union customs authorities of intellectual Property rights (OJ L 183, 1.7.2004, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC, EEC, L 183) have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 59:
EU programme for cooperation in the field of customs (EU Customs programme 2021-2027)
EU programme to cooperate with the customs authorities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 establishing a new EU programme to support the cooperation between the EU and its Member States, the European Union Customs Union and the European Commission
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a new European Union programme to assist the Member States and the Commission by providing a framework for actions that aim to:
support the customs union and customs authorities working together and acting as one;
contribute to protecting the financial and economic interests of the Union and its EU countries;
ensure the security of the EU’s external financial interests.
KEY POINTS
The EU Customs programme is designed to support EU countries and the EU Customs Union by:
improving administrative procedures, enhancing administrative capacity and improving the functioning and operations of customs authorities through the development and sharing of best practices;
developing human competency and other capacity-building actions;
providing technical support aimed at improving administrative procedures;
enhancing cooperation between EU countries’ customs authorities.
The programme is implemented through multiannual work programmes as referred to in Article 110(2) of the Financial Regulation.
EU countries are responsible for implementing the programme.
Actions under the programme should ensure coherence in the use of EU resources supporting the customs unions and the authorities. They should also contribute to the protection of EU financial interests against fraud and other irregularities.
Eligible actions
The following actions are eligible for the programme:
human competency training or development of e-learning to improve the acquisition of necessary professional skills and knowledge relating to customs;
technical support aimed to improve administrative procedures and improving administrative capacity;
improve cooperation between customs authorities;
support for the exchange of information and communication activities;
information and communication actions that are related to the objectives of the programme, as well as expenses linked to information technology networks focusing on information processing and exchange, including corporate information technology tools and other technical and administrative assistance needed in connection with the management of the Programme.
Implementation
The Commission is responsible for managing the programme and for the implementation of the work programmes. The work programmes are managed by the Commission and are funded by the EU budget.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND

Equitable opportunities and to the Labour market, Fair working conditions, social inclusion and social protection — European structural funds (ESFs)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — implementing the European Social Fund and the European Pillar of Social Rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the implementation of the European social fund and its EaSI strand.
It aims to enhance the effectiveness of labour markets, promote equal access to quality employment, improve the quality of education and training, promote social inclusion, facilitate access to healthcare for vulnerable persons and contribute to eradicating poverty.
KEY POINTS

the objectives of the ESF+ strand under shared management;
the measures to be implemented by the EU countries;
measures to be coordinated by the European Commission.
The EU countries are responsible for implementing the EU social fund under their own national multiannual investment strategies in support of those priorities.
Eligible actions
EU countries are required to:
invest at least 0.25% of their resources in the social partners and civil society organisations (SINOs) under the social partnership strand;
invest an equivalent amount in the SINOs strand under their national investment strategies;
support social entrepreneurs and small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) in their efforts to develop innovative solutions to tackle social challenges;
provide social protection for vulnerable people;
promote the social inclusion of persons with disabilities;
facilitate access to health care and long-term care;
ensure the integration of people from low-income countries into society;
encourage the development of the social entrepreneurship sector;
increase the participation of women and men in the economy;
improve the capacity of civil society to contribute to economic and social resilience.
Actions covered by the regulation are:
e.g. childcare and education;
health;
education and training;
social entrepreneurship;
transnational cooperation;
science, technology, innovation and entrepreneurship (STEM);
science and technology policy and research;
information, communication and publicity (ICTR).
The European Commission is responsible for:
monitoring the implementation by EU countries of the programmes;
assessing the impact of the actions;
developing and implementing strategies to achieve the objectives of ESF+.
The Commission is also responsible for developing and implementing EU-wide action plans.

Summary 63:
Transparency register for EU countries’ public authorities
Transparency registry for EU governments
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement between the European Union (EU) and the EU countries on a transparency register for public authorities in the field of public administration
Decision 2014/852/EU on the conclusion, on behalf of the EU, of the agreement between the EU and the European Commission on a transnational transparency register
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to ensure that public authorities across the EU are transparent in their dealings with public authorities.
The decision concludes the agreement with the EU’s European Commission.
KEY POINTS
The EU and EU countries agree to implement a coordinated approach with regard to the activities covered by the agreement (‘covered activities’) and to set out those covered activities that they decide to make conditional upon registration in the register.
Activities covered under the agreement are listed in Annex I to the agreement.
Registrants promoting their own interests or the collective interests of their members vis-à-vis any of the signatory institutions must provide an up-to-date estimate of the annual costs related to covered activities according to the grid below.
Intermediaries must declare the estimated total annual revenue generated that is attributable to the covered activities.
Eligibility and code of conduct
The register contains rules and principles to be observed by interest representatives that sign up to the register (the ‘register’). Registrants are required to:
present, if requested, supporting material demonstrating that the information the registration is considered to satisfy the requirements set out in Annex II;
provide a copy of the register;
submit a statement to the management board on the basis of which they are eligible for registration;
ensure that they respect the principles of transparency and respect the confidentiality of the information they provide.
If a registrant does not respect these principles, the registrant may be declared ineligible and may be subject to an investigation by the Secretariat.
A management board is responsible for maintaining the register and for its operation.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 64:
European Union financial framework 2021-2027 — cooperation between the EU institutions
European Union Financial framework 2021 - 2027 – cooperation between EU institutions	SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/2093 on the conclusion of the Agreement between the European Union Institutions and the European Commission on budgetary matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to improve the functioning of the EU’s annual budgetary procedure and to ensure sound financial management, and to implement cooperation and establish a roadmap towards the introduction of new own resources that are sufficient to cover the repayment of the European EU Recovery Instrument (EURI).
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the Union.
KEY POINTS
The European Union has a multiannual financial framework for the years 2021 to 2027.
The EU has a budget of €20.8 billion for the period 2021–2027, which includes €8.5 billion from the EURI and €2 billion from other EU financial instruments, such as the European Investment Bank (EIB), the European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) and the Structural and Investment Funds (SIFs).
In 2021, the EU had to repay €1.2 billion of EURI, which had to be repaid by the end of the year.
To repay the EURI, EU countries had to:
provide the European Parliament and the Council with an estimate of the amount of the ERI that they would need to repay in the following years;
provided the Commission with a forecast of the amounts that would be needed to repay EURI in the previous year;
ensure the contribution of the borrowed funds to the achievements of the objectives of EU programmes and the specific EU programmes.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Both decisions have applied since 15 June 2020.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
EU financial framework (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* EURO: a financial instrument designed to provide the EU with financial support in the event of financial difficulties.

Summary 66:
EU countries that fail to comply with the euro area’s financial rules may be fined for failing to meet their financial obligations
EU countries who fail to meet the financial obligations of the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1466/97 on the imposition of fines by the European Commission on EU countries that do not comply with its financial rules
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a procedure for imposing fines on euro-area countries which do not meet the requirements of the financial rules of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries whose currency is the euro. It does not apply to non-euro area countries.
It applies to:
non-euro-area EU countries with a budget deficit of more than 3% of gross domestic product (GDP) that is greater than 0.2% of the GDP of an EU country that does not meet its medium-term budgetary objective;
non euro area countries whose GDP is less than 2% of GDP that have a deficit of less than 1.5% of GDP.
Imposing a fine
The EU country which fails to meet its financial obligations may be subject to a fine of up to €2.5 million. The amount of the fine must not exceed 0,2 % of GDP of the country concerned.
The fine must be deposited with the Commission. The deposit must be in the form of an interest-bearing deposit.
If the EU country fails to pay the fine within 10 days of adoption of the Council decision establishing that a EU country has failed to take action, the Council may decide to reduce or cancel the fine. If the Council decides to reduce the fine, it may annul, reduce or increase the fine so imposed.
An EU country may also refuse to pay a fine if it has not met the minimum requirements for national budgetary frameworks. The Council may, following a reasoned request by the EU countries concerned addressed to the Commission, recommend that the Council reduce the amount or cancel it.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 1997.
BACKGROUND
 
 			
For more information, see:
‘EU countries’ on the European External Action Service's website.

Summary 67:
Population and housing data — EU countries’ national and EU statistical authorities (NUTS 1, NUTS 2 and NUTs 3)
Population and Housing data – EU countries' national and European Union statistical authorities
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 176/2008 of the European Parliament and of the Council on population and housing statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) has comprehensive data on the population and the housing situation in the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
It requires EU countries to collect data on:
the population of the EU;
the housing situation of EU countries; and
the migration of EU citizens.
National and European statistical authorities must collect the data by:
conducting a national or EU-wide population or housing survey;
using a combination of register-based censuses and sample surveys;
a combination of sample surveys and conventional censuses;
appropriate surveys with rotating samples (rolling censuses).
Each EU country must determine a reference date.
The data must be collected on the basis of:
a national register of registered households;
an EU country’s national statistical database;
data from the national statistical databases;
information provided by EU countries and the European Commission.
Data sources and methodology
EU countries must report on the data sources and methods they use and the quality of the data they collect.
Quality reports
The quality reports must contain:
technical specifications of the topics as required by this regulation as well as of their breakdowns as provided for in Article 5(4);
the establishment of the appropriate technical format for the statistical data and the metadata;
modalities and structure of the quality reports.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The EU countries had to incorporate the regulation into national law by 1 January 2009.

Summary 68:
Rules of Procedure for the European Parliament and of the Council (9th parliamentary term)
Rules of Procedures for the Eastern European Union and the European Economic Area (EEA)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2019/852 — rules of procedure for the 9th parliamentary session of the European Union (Euratom)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND THE RULES OF PROCEDURE?
The decision sets out the rules for the Euratom Parliament and the Council.
The EEA Parliament is the legislative body of the EU and is responsible for implementing the EU’s laws and policies. The EEA and the EEA are governed by the Treaty on European Union.
KEY POINTS
EURatom Parliament
The rules apply to all legislation adopted by the EEU and the EU countries.
Members of the Eureatom Parliament have the right to vote and to express their views. They may not vote against a law or a proposal if they believe that it is contrary to the fundamental rights of EU citizens or to the principles of democracy, human rights or the rule of law.
Political parties are bound by the principle of non-discrimination. They must respect Parliament's dignity and must not harm its reputation.
Duties of the Quaestors
The Quaestsors are responsible for administrative and financial matters directly concerning Members, in accordance with guidelines laid down by the Bureau of Parliamentary Affairs (BPA).
The Chair of the Committee of the Regions and the Vice-Chairs of the Committees of Europe and the Southern Neighbourhoods are appointed by the Presidents of the Member States. The Chair is assisted by a committee of representatives from the European External Action Service (EEAS).
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The new EURatom rules entered into force on 1 July 2019.

European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS) - EU institutions, bodies, offices and agencies responsible for the protection of natural persons’ personal data (2018/1725) - European Data Protection supervisor (2018)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2018/1724 establishing an independent authority responsible for ensuring that the fundamental rights and freedoms of human persons, and in particular their right to data protection, with respect to the processing of personal data, are respected by EU institutions and bodies, bodies and agencies
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European data protection supervisor to ensure that human rights and fundamental freedoms are respected in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The EDPS is responsible for:
ensuring that:
the protection of human rights, fundamental freedoms and the right to privacy are respected;
that personal data are processed in a way that is fair, appropriate and consistent with the law of the EU;
protecting the personal data of EU citizens and EU countries.
It is composed of:
a Director and an Appointing Authority;
a Data Protection Officer (DPO) and a Data Protection Board (DPB).
The director and the DPO are appointed by the European Commission.
The DPO and the EDPS are assisted by the EDPB, which is assisted by EU countries’ national data protection authorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 March 2018.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Data protection supervisor: an independent EU institution, body, office or agency that ensures that the rights of human beings, and the rights to privacy and data protection are respected when processing personal data.
* DPO: a person responsible for protecting personal data and ensuring that their rights are respected.

Summary 71:
Fuel efficiency of tyres — rolling resistance and other essential parameters (RRCs)
Fuel efficiency
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1369 on the labelling of tyres
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to encourage end-users to purchase more fuel‐efficient tyres by providing them with harmonised information on the rolling resistance parameter.
It also aims to increase safety, the protection of health, and the economic and environmental efficiency of road transport.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to tyres for cars (C1 tyres) and vans (C2 tyres) but not for heavy‐duty vehicles (C3 tyres).
It repeals Regulation (EC) No 1222/2009.
EU countries must ensure that all tyres on sale in the EU have a fuel efficiency class of ‘A’ or ‘E’ and that the tyre has a rolling resistance coefficient of at least 9.1 m/kg.
The roll resistance coefficient must be measured by a machine in a candidate laboratory of the EU country where the tyre is placed on the market.
Tyre labels must include the following information:
the rolling resistance class and measured value in dB;
the external rolling noise class and indicated value in km/h;
an indication of whether the tyres are a tyre for use in severe snow conditions;
a description of the type of ice grip tyre.
National authorities must verify the accuracy of the tyre label.
If a tyre does not comply with the requirements of the regulation, the manufacturer or importer must inform the competent national authority of the reason for the failure to comply.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
Directive 2014/35/EU has applied since 1 January 2016.
Directives 2014/36/EU and 2014/37/EU have applied since 31 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Fuel consumption on the European Commission’s website.
* KEY TERMS
* Roll resistance coefficient: a measure of the force applied by a tyre to the road surface. It is used to measure the force exerted by a vehicle to move a given amount of weight on the road.

Summary 72:
Integrated Border Management Fund (IBMF)
IntegratedBorder Management Fund
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2021/444 establishing the Integrated border management fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes the EU’s first integrated border management (IMF) fund.
It aims to improve the efficiency and effectiveness of customs controls in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The objective of the IMF is to:
increase the effectiveness and efficiency of EU customs controls;
improve the security of goods entering and exiting the EU;
reduce the risk of fraud, corruption or any other illegal activity affecting the financial interests of the European Union (EU).
The IMF aims to achieve these objectives by:
providing the EU with adequate and equivalent results of customs control;
encouraging cooperation and interoperability between EU programmes and instruments;
ensuring that customs control equipment is interoperable;
promoting the prevention, detection, correction and investigation of irregularities, including fraud, to the recovery of funds lost, wrongly paid or incorrectly used, and to the imposition of administrative penalties.
The Imf is funded by the European Commission and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCRA).
Implementation
The imf is managed by the Commission, the EBCRA and the EU countries’ competent authorities. It is implemented by the EU Customs Directorate-General (CDFG).
It is funded through the European Customs Agency (ECA) and the Customs Agency of the EU (CAA).
EU countries must cooperate with each other in order to ensure that customs controls are more efficient and more effective.
Annex I to the regulation sets out the objectives of the Imf. These objectives include:
improving the efficiency of the customs control system;
increasing the effectiveness of the border control systems;
enhancing the protection of human rights and fundamental freedoms; and
reducing the risks of fraud and corruption.
In order to achieve its objectives, the EU has adopted a number of implementing measures, including:
the establishment of a monitoring and evaluation framework;
the setting up of a coordination mechanism to ensure the interoperability of the Customs control equipment purchased with the support of EU programmes or instruments, and therefore its efficient use.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2019/852 has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Integrated border management Fund’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 74:
European High Performance Computing Joint Undertaking (EuroHPC) (European High-Performance Computing Joint undertaking)
European High–Performance–Computing Joint Underking (Euro-HPC Joint underking)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/695 on the European High–performance computing joint undertaking
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up EuroHPC, a joint undertaking under the European Union’s Horizon Europe programme, to acquire and own high-end supercomputers.
It aims to develop in the EU an integrated, federated, secure and hyper-connected world by investing in high-performance computing (HPC).

Scope

Participation

FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 31 December 2021.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* High-performing supercomputer: a supercomputer that can solve complex problems in a very short period of time.
* Quantum simulation: a computer that can simulate the behaviour of biological, chemical, biological, radiological, nuclear, chemical and biological systems.

* Hyper-connectivity: the ability of a high-powered supercomputer to operate in a highly interconnected environment, such as the internet or the cloud.

Summary 75:
The EU’s 2030 climate and energy targets
The EU's 2030 climate & energy targets			
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1119 on the EU's 2020 climate targets
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out EU countries’ targets for reducing greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions and removals from land use, land use change and forestry in the EU by 2040.
KEY POINTS
EU countries are required to:
reduce GHG emissions by at least 20 % by 2030 compared to 1990 levels;
removal of GHG from land and land use by 20 % compared to 2010 levels by 2030.
The 2020 EU climate targets are set out in the European Green Deal (see summary).
The 2040 EU climate target is based on an indicative, linear trajectory linking the EU 2040 climate target, when adopted, and the climate-neutrality objective set out by the Paris Agreement.
Each EU country must prepare and submit to the European Commission its long-term strategy with a 30-year perspective and consistent with the EU 2030 climate target.
In addition, each EU country is required to take the necessary measures at EU and national level to enable the EU to achieve the climate neutrality objective, taking into account the importance of promoting both fairness and solidarity among EU countries and cost-effectiveness in achieving this objective.
National authorities are responsible for:
monitoring the progress of EU countries in achieving the 2030 climate targets;
assessing the impact of national measures on the climate neutrality objective;
developing a regulatory framework for carbon removal;
providing the Commission with a roadmap for the EU 2020-2040 climate targets.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 17 September 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
 		  	
For more information, see:
‘The EU 2030 Climate and Energy Targets’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* GHG: greenhouse gases emitted by the burning of fossil fuels, such as oil, gas, coal, steel, paper, paper and wood.

Summary 76:
Medicines and antimicrobials: EU action programme (2020-2027)
Medicines, antimicrobs and antimicrobial resistance:EU action programme
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2020/2093 laying down the multiannual financial framework for the years 2021 to 2027
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the EU’s action programme for the period 2021–2027.
KEY POINTS
The programme aims to:
promote the optimal delivery and use of medicinal products;
improve the prevention and response to health emergencies;
strengthen capabilities in the EU to prevent, prepare for, respond to and manage health crises;
support actions taken in the framework of the mechanisms and structures established under relevant EU legislation.
The objectives of the programme are:
to promote the optimal and efficient use of medicines;
to improve the prevention, response and management of health emergencies.
To improve the protection of human health and to promote equality between men and women.
In line with the goals of the European Union (TFEU) and the EU's competences in the area of public health, the programme should emphasise actions in relation to which there are advantages and efficiency gains from collaboration and cooperation at EU level, and actions that have an impact on the internal market.
It should support actions taken by EU countries, EU countries and non-EU countries in the field of antimicrobiology, in order to reduce the risk of the spread of infectious diseases and to improve the quality of life of the population.
EU countries are responsible for:
monitoring and evaluating the effectiveness of the action;
providing information to the public on the actions taken;
promoting cooperation between EU countries;
contributing to research and development;
encouraging the exchange of best practice and best practice in the fields of medicine, antimicrobial research and clinical trials;
developing the capacity of EU countries to manage and respond to health crises.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND

Summary 78:
The European Institute of Innovation and Technology (EIT) (European Commission)
The European institute of innovation and technology (Europa)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2021/818 setting out the priority fields and the strategy for the European Institute OF INNOLOGY AND TECHNOLOGY
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It establishes the EIT’s objectives, key actions, mode of operation, expected results and impact, as well as an estimate of the resources needed.
KEY POINTS
The EIT aims to:
promote innovation and creativity in the European Union (EU);
facilitate the exchange of best practices and best practices between EU countries’ universities and research institutes (KICs);
contribute to the creation of a knowledge-based economy in the EU;
support research and innovation (R&I) in the fields of innovation, entrepreneurship and knowledge exchange;
provide a forum for the exchange and sharing of best practice and best practice in the field of R&I.
The objectives and key actions are set out in the strategic plan adopted by the European Parliament and of the Council in May 2021. They include:
creativity and entrepreneurship;
research and innovation;
science and technology;
education and training;
internationalisation;
exchange of experience;
cooperation between the KICs and other relevant stakeholders;
promotion of knowledge and innovation in the area of research and development;
enhancing the role of the European Research Council (ERC) and the European Atomic Energy Community (EAC) in R&D;
encouraging the development of new technologies;
creating a culture of innovation in Europe;
developing the capacity of EU countries to foster innovation and entrepreneurship in the areas of science and technology.
Initiatives and activities
EIT activities are based on a four-pronged approach:
facilitating innovation in areas of research, entrepreneurship, knowledge exchange and internationalisation (RIS);
supporting innovation in transport and maritime transport; and
promoting innovation in propulsion and logistics.
RIS activities are used to attract and facilitate integration of potential new partners that add value to the European Innovation Network (EINN) and are fully integrated in KICS’ multiannual strategies and business plans. The EIT also supports the activities of the EICs’ CLCs and RIS hubs, thus leveraging their role as a gateway for accessing a KIC community, and interacting with the co-located partners.

the name of the product;
the geographical indication;

SUMMARY OF:

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the limits on the exploratory fishery* for some fish species in the EU waters.

The regulation applies from 1 May 2020 to 1 May 2021.
The EU countries must notify the European Commission by 1 May 2021 of their intention to fish for the following fish species:
salmon (Salmonidae)
purse seiners fishing in the part of the WCPFC Convention Area referred to in paragraph 1 shall retain on board, tranship and land all bigeye, yellowfin and skipjack tuna caught.
All purse seiners must not use fixed nets, which may not be used to catch or retain European seabass during the period covered by the regulation.
Limitations on exploratories fisheries for toothfish
The European Commission must notify EU countries by 1 July 2021 of its intention to allow exploratory fish to be caught in the following areas:
the Atlantic Ocean;
the Pacific Ocean; and
the Indian Ocean.
EU countries must also notify the Commission by 31 December 2021 of any plans to allow the fishing of the following species in their waters:
sailfish (Sailfish spp.);
dolphin (Dolphin spp.).

EU macro–financial assistance for Ukraine
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2019/849/EU — EU’s emergency macro-financing of Ukraine
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
The decision authorises the EU to provide Ukraine with €1.2 billion in emergency financial assistance.
KEY POINTS
The EU provides Ukraine with a maximum amount of €1,2 billion to support its economic stabilisation and substantive reform agenda.
The financial assistance will be made available by the European Commission in two equal instalments, each of which will consist of a loan. The loans will have a maximum average maturity of 15 years.
It will be used to support the implementation of a policy programme containing strong immediate adjustment and structural reform measures designed to improve the beneficiary‘s balance-of-payments position in the short term and economic resilience in the medium term.
EU assistance is subject to economic policy conditions, to be set out in a memorandum of understanding (MOU).

Summary 82:
Recovery of swordfish (Xiphias gladius) in the Mediterranean Sea (‘Mediterranean swordfish’) (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Recovery efforts to restore the Mediterranean swordfish population are under way under the multiannual recovery plan adopted by the International Commission for the Conservation of Atlantic Tunas (ICCAT).
ACT
Commission Regulation (EC) No 552/2019 of 6 June 2019 laying down general rules for the implementation by the European Union of a Multiannual Recovery Plan (MRP) for swordfish [Official Journal L 347 of 6.6.2019].
SUMMARY
The European Union is party to the International Convention for the conservation of Atlantic Tuna (the ICCAT Convention) and is responsible for managing the stocks of the fish in its waters.
The Mediterranean Sea is one of the world’s most important fishing grounds for the swordfish species, which has been overfished over the last 30 years.
This regulation aims to ensure the sustainable recovery of the Mediterranean Swordfish population.
WHAT DOES THE REGULATION DO?
It lays down the general rules to be followed by EU countries in the implementation of the MRP.
KEY POINTS
The MRP will be implemented from 1 January 2019 to 31 December 2031.
EU countries are responsible for:
allocating the fishing opportunities available to them;
monitoring and monitoring the fishing fleet;
assessing the impact of the fishery on the environment and the social and economic well-being of the populations of the species;
ensuring that the sustainable management of the stocks is achieved.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 8 June 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 8 June 2020.

Summary 84:
European Maritime Fund for the Faroes (EMFAF) — priorities and rules for EU funding (2018-2027)
European Maritime fund for the faroes (EMPFAF — priorities)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2021/1060 on the priorities of the European Maritime Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the priorities and the specific rules for providing EU funding for the EU’s maritime fund (EMF).
KEY POINTS
The EMF supports small-scale coastal fishing, including shellfish gatherers, in the Faroe Islands, Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland.
The objectives of the EMF are to:
promote the sustainable use of the oceans, seas and marine resources for sustainable development;
ensure the protection of marine ecosystems and the sustainable exploitation of marine resources;
support the sustainable management of the marine resources of the faroe islands, Iceland and Norway;
encourage the sustainable development of the fishing sector in the north-east Atlantic and the south-west Atlantic;
provide the EU with technical assistance and financial support to the EU countries involved in the field of fisheries.
EU countries are responsible for managing the EMFAF and its budget.
Eligible entities, activities and costs
The EU countries may grant EU funding to the following entities and activities:
small-scale fishing vessels of an overall length of less than 12 metres and not using towed fishing gear;
fishers on foot;
harvesting and processing of shellfish;
aquaculture and aquaculture activities;
scientific and technical research;
the management of marine protected areas;
marine research and development (R&D) activities.
Fisheries must comply with the following conditions:
the EU country concerned must have a national work plan for the period covered by the EU funding;
an EU country must submit a revised work plan to the European Commission within 6 months of receiving the EU grant proposal;
a European Commission report on the implementation of the EU support.
An EU country may refuse to provide EU funding if the EU country does not fulfil the conditions for the granting of EU funding.
A non-EU country may also refuse to grant EU support if it does not meet the conditions set out in the EU financial regulation.
Non-EU countries may not provide EU funds if they do not:
fishing activities are illegal;
there is a risk that the fishery will be destroyed or damaged; or
the fishery is involved in illegal activities that could have a negative impact on the environment or human health.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2021.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Small-scale fishery: fishing is carried out by marine and inland fishing vessels, including fishing vessels on foot.
* Harvesting: the process of harvesting and processing shellfish by hand.

Summary 85:
Convention on the Conservation and Management of Straddling Fish and Highly Migratory Fish Stocks (IATTC)
Convention of the International Tribunal for the Law of the Sea and the Agreement for the Implementation of that Convention relating to the conservation and management of straddling fish and highly migratory fish stocks (ICTY)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — the Convention on the conservation of species covered by the International Convention for the Conservation of Species of Wild Fauna and Flora
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
The regulation aims to ensure that the IATTC Convention and the agreement for the implementation of that convention are implemented.
It aims to protect the stocks of wild fauna and flora in the western Pacific Ocean and the highly migratory species of fish stocks in the eastern Pacific Ocean.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The closure period for the fishing of tropical tunas is from 29 July to 8 October or from 9 November to 19 January.
The fishing period for bigeye tuna is from 9 October to 8 November.
EU countries must notify the European Commission by 15 June each year of which of the two closure periods are applicable to their vessels. The notification must be valid for a period of not less than three years.
Each EU country must ensure that:
fishing vessels that use hydraulic, mechanical or electrical systems and that fish in the area north of 23°N and south of 30°S, and the area bounded by the coastline at 2°N, 3°N-95°W, south to 15°S-85°W and south to 30°s shall apply at least two of the mitigation measures contained in the table in the Annex to this regulation including at least one from Column A.
Fishing vessels which use a longline vessel and use a sealed well shall be prohibited to retain onboard, tranship, land, store, sell or offer for sale any part or whole carcass of oceanic whitetip sharks (Carcharhinus longimanus).
Fisheries vessels that fish on data buoys shall not use the same measure from Columns A and B.
In addition to the closure referred to in the annex to the regulation, EU countries must:
provide the Commission with data on:
the number of tuna caught by vessels flying their flag;
the total number of fish caught by the vessels flying the flag of the EU country in the period covered by this regulation;
a list of vessels that have been involved in the fishery;
information on the number of vessels participating in the fishing period.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
It has applied since 15 June 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 15 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Convention’ on the ICCTY website.

Summary 86:
Delegated acts — EU institutions’ tools for better law-making
Deleg delegated acts – EU institutions' tools for Better Law-Making
SUMMARY OF:
Common Understanding between the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission on the tools for improving the quality of EU legislation
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE CONVENTION AND OF THE DECISION?
The common understanding aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) is governed by a system of delegated acts.
The decision concludes the common understanding on behalf of the EU.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The European Commission has delegated the power to adopt delegated acts in accordance with Article 290 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Union (TFEU) to the EU institutions:
the European Parliament and the Council;
the Commission;
European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) and the European Committee of the Regions (EECR).
The EU institutions are responsible for:
promoting the most efficient regulatory instruments, such as harmonisation and mutual recognition, in order to avoid overregulation and administrative burdens and fulfil the objectives of the TFEU;
ensuring that EU legislation is based on sound principles of transparency, accountability and fairness;
encouraging the participation of the public in the decision-making process;
supporting the work of EU countries in the implementation of EU law;
providing the public with information on EU legislation;
exchanging best practice and best practice on EU law.
EU countries’ experts are consulted in a timely manner on each draft delegated act prepared by the Commission services. They must also have access to the meetings of Commission expert groups to which EU countries' experts are invited and which concern the preparation of delegated act. The Commission’s experts must also be consulted on any draft delegated acts adopted under the urgency procedure.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The agreement entered into force on 1 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Better law making’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Council Common Understanding of 21 June 2016 on the use of delegated laws by the EU Institutions for better Law-making (OJ L 53, 1.7.2016, pp. 1-2)
