Summary 87:
Citizens’ initiatives — transposition in the European Union (EU) and the United Kingdom (UK)
Citizens' initiatives – transposition within the EU and the UK
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on transposition of proposals for a legal act of the Union for the purpose of implementing the Treaties
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out a procedure for citizens to submit a request inviting the European Commission to transpose a citizens’ initiative in the EU.
It aims to improve the transparency and effectiveness of the EU’s internal market for goods and services.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries. It does not apply to non-EU countries.
Organisers must obtain a certificate of support from the EU countries’ competent authorities. The organisers may only start collecting statements of support through the online collection system once they have obtained the certificate referred to in paragraph 3.
The organisers must submit the initiative to the Commission, accompanied by information regarding any support and funding received for that initiative.
EU countries must verify the organisers’ certificates of support within 3 months of receipt of the request.
Once the organisers have received the certificates, they must notify the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) of all the information they have collected.
If the organisers do not have the necessary certificates, EU countries may ask the organisers to provide them with additional information, such as the name of the contact persons, the sources of support and the funding. If the organisers fail to provide the necessary information, the EU country may ask them to provide it.
In the event of a dispute between the organisers and EU countries, the organisers may ask for a judicial or administrative solution to resolve the dispute.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2008/53/EC has applied since 1 January 2009.
Directives 2008/54/EC and 2008/56/EC have applied since 31 December 2008.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Transposition of initiatives’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Council Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2008 of 29 December 2008 on transposing proposals for legal acts of the European Community for the purposes of implementing Article 225 of the Treaty on the Functioning of the Community (TFEU) (OJ L 183, 31.12.2008, pp. 1-2)

Summary 88:
Pesticides — placing on the market and use of plant protection products (Regulation (EC) No 596/2008)
Pesticide — placing upon the market or use of plants protection products(Regulation(EC) no 596 / 2008)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation on the placing on market and the use of pesticides
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to establish a common framework for the systematic production of EU-wide statistics on pesticides placed on the EU market and their use.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation covers substances listed in Annex I to the regulation, as well as those listed in the annexes II and III.
It applies to all pesticides which are used for plant protection in the EU.
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with a list of the pesticides on sale in their territory.
The Commission must transmit the data to Eurostat within 3 months of the end of the reference period.
Annexes I and II contain information on:
the number of pesticides on the European Union’s (EU) market;
the types of pesticides used in EU countries;
a description of the substances on sale and their intended use;
an analysis of the risks associated with pesticides;
information on the types of products on sale.
National authorities must provide Eurostat with a copy of the data.
Data collection, transmission and processing
Eurostat collects and transmits the data in accordance with the procedures laid down in the regulation. EU countries must send Eurostat a report every 3 years on the quality of the information they have collected and on the results of the statistical analyses they have carried out.

Summary 89:
Active substances and plant protection products — EU rules on their authorisation and use (Regulation (EC) No 178/2002)
ACT
Commission Regulation (EU) No 179/2002 of 20 November 2002 on the authorisation of active substances and plants protection products (Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 552/2004 and (EC), Euratom).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules for the authorising of active substance* and plant-protection products* and their use.
It aims to ensure that the products covered by the regulation are safe for human consumption and the environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all active substances* and products containing active substances. It does not apply to active substances intended for use in food or animal feed.
Authorisation
EU countries must authorise active substances only when it is necessary to control that serious danger to plant health in their territory.
The EU countries must ensure that:
the active substance or plant protection product meets the criteria of a ‘foodstuff’ as defined in Article 2 of Directive 91/414/EEC (see summary);
the product has been tested or studied to determine whether it poses a risk to human health;
it meets the requirements of the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA).
The competent EU country may refuse to authorise an active substance if it considers that it does not meet the criteria for a foodstuff. It must inform the European Commission and the EFSA of its decision.
Applying for an authorisation
An applicant must submit a dossier containing:
a list of the active substances, synergists* and adjuvants;
a description of the product;
an estimate of the risk of human exposure to the active substance, synergist* or adjuvant;
information on the safety of the products; and
a risk assessment of the safety and effectiveness of the plant protection system.
An EU country must examine the application and decide whether to grant or deny authorisation.
If an EU country decides to grant authorisation, the applicant must provide the EU country with all the information necessary to carry out the risk assessment. If the applicant does not provide the necessary information, the EU countries may refuse authorisation to the product.
Derogations
An application for authorisation may be refused if:
it does not comply with the essential safety requirements; or
it is likely to endanger human health or the environment; or or
there is a risk of serious adverse effects on the plant.
A derogation may also be granted if the applicant fails to provide the required information.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
Directive 2001/43/EC has applied since 1 January 2003.
Regulation(EC) 2016/852 has applied from 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Active Substances and Plants Protection’ (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Active substance: a substance which is used in the production of food or animals.
* Safener: a compound which prevents the growth of a plant by preventing its growth.
Adjuvant: a chemical substance which prevents a plant from growing.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Implementing Regulation (Euratom) 2018/552 of 20 December 2018 on the Authorisation of Active Substances, Safeners and Adjuvants (OJ L 347, 21.12.2018, pp. 1-8)

Summary 90:
Interoperable data management (IMI)
Interoperability data management
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on the use of Interoperable Data Management (IMI)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the security and privacy of personal data exchanged by the EU’s internal market surveillance system, i.e. IMI.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the responsibilities of IMI coordinators and the European Data Protection Supervisor (EDPS).
IMI coordinators are responsible for:
monitoring the use by IMI actors and the Commission;
providing information on administrative cooperation or mutual assistance in the internal market.
The EDPS is responsible for ensuring that IMI users’ personal data are protected, processed and used for the purposes for which it was originally collected and should be subject to all relevant safeguards.
Implementation of the regulation
Implementing the regulation requires EU countries to:
register national coordinators for IMI;
ensure that the EU data protection supervisor has access to all personal data that are strictly necessary to carry out its tasks within the responsibilities set out in this regulation;
assist the EDPS in the implementation of the IMI regulation, including by providing technical assistance;
take all necessary security measures to ensure that personal data processed in IMI is protected and used in accordance with Article 17 of Directive 95/46/EC on the security of data.
EU countries must also:
provide EDPS with all relevant information on IMI, including:
the name and contact details of the coordinators;
the use of the system by EU countries and the EU;
information on EU countries’ cooperation with non-EU countries in the field of information society;
data exchange with third countries.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE

Summary 91:
Fisheries in the Mediterranean and the Black Sea: EU-GFCM agreement
Fishery in the Mediterranean and the Bay of Biscay: EU - GFCM Agreement
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 on the EU’s participation in the General Agreement for the Conservation of the Marine Living Resources in the Eastern Mediterranean and in the Black Seas
Decision (EU) 2018/852 on the conclusion of the EU-European Council Agreement on the European Fisheries Cooperation Mechanism
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION AND OF THE AGREEMENT?
The regulation establishes a framework for multilateral cooperation to promote the development, conservation, rational management and best utilisation of living marine resources in the eastern Mediterranean and Black Sea at levels which are considered sustainable and at low risk of collapse.
The decision concludes the agreement on behalf of the European Community (now the EU).
KEY POINTS
The agreement aims to:
promote sustainable and rational management of the marine life in the area covered by the agreement;
reduce the impact of overfishing on the marine environment;
ensure a high level of environmental protection and sustainable use of marine life;
improve the environmental performance of fishing vessels and the marine ecosystem;
provide a legal basis for the EU to participate in the agreement.
EU countries must ensure that:
fisheries are managed in accordance with the rules of the common fisheries policy (CFP);
fishing vessels are permitted to fish within the agreement area, subject to the conditions laid down in this regulation;
the agreement is open to all EU countries, including non-EU countries, to fish in the agreed areas.
Fishing authorisations are issued by EU countries to vessels operating within the agreed area.
Authorised vessels must:
be registered in an EU country;
have a valid fishing licence issued by the EU country in which they are registered;
be accompanied by an EU flag;
carry out the required inspections;
report to the European Commission on the results of the inspections.
An EU country may refuse to issue an authorisation to a vessel if it considers that the vessel does not comply with the requirements of the agreement or if the vessel is not complying with the EU fisheries policy.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
    баба  
It has applied since 20 November 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 20 November 2011.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a member of the General Association of Fisheries Commissions (GACM).

Summary 92:
Unitary patent protection in the EU — EU countries’ national patent systems (EU countries)
EU countries' national patent system (UPS)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1260/2012 on the granting of European patents with unitary effect
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that European patents granted with the same set of claims in respect of all the participating EU countries benefit from the same level of protection.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to patents granted by the European Patent Office (EPO) in the European Union (EU).
The EU countries have the exclusive right to grant EU patents with this type of protection, provided that the patent has been registered in the Register for Unitary Patent Protection (RUPP) and that it has been granted in a participating EU country.
The RUPP is a legal instrument that allows EU countries to grant patent protection to the same type of product or service.
Unitary protection is achieved by attributing unitary protection to European patents in the post-grant phase by virtue of this regulation and in the following measures:
patent proprietors must pay a single annual renewal fee for a European patent.
A European patent granted with a single claim must be protected in all the EU countries where it is registered.
An EU country must notify the European Commission of the measures adopted in accordance with Article 4(2) by the date of application of the regulation or, in the case of an EU country in which the Unified Patent Court does not have exclusive jurisdiction with regard to patent protection, on the date from which the EU country has such exclusive jurisdiction in that EU country
The European Commission must publish a list of EU countries covered by the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
EU patents (European Commission).

Summary 93:
EU Office for the Protection of Intellectual Property Rights (OPTO) (2013-2016) (European Commission Regulation (EU) No 552/2013) (OJ L 183, 22.12.2013, pp. 1-2)
EU Office For the Protection Of Intellectual Property rights (OPIO) (2012-2016)(European Commission)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) 2013/183 laying down the tasks of the European Union’s Office for Protection of intellectual property rights
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Union Office to monitor and report on infringements of the intellectual property (IP) rights of EU citizens and businesses.
It aims to facilitate and support the activities of national authorities, the private sector and the EU institutions in the fight against infringements.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EU Office is responsible for:
monitoring and reporting infringements;
facilitating and supporting the actions of EU countries’ authorities in the enforcement of IP rights;
providing information and data to the European Commission and the European Trade Union Confederation (ETUC) on intellectual property infringements in the EU;
assisting EU countries in the investigation and prosecution of infringements, as well as in the prevention of counterfeiting and piracy;
advising the Commission on issues falling within the scope of this regulation, on the basis of a request from the Commission;
carrying out similar activities necessary in order to enable the Office to fulfil the tasks set out in Article 1.3 of the regulation.
Tasks
The European Commission appoints an EU Office to carry out the tasks laid down in this regulation. The Office is chaired by the Commission and comprises representatives from the public administrations, bodies and organisations in EU countries and private sector representatives.
The Commission may invite EU countries to participate in individual operations or investigations carried out by the competent authorities.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 22 March 2013.

Commercial policy in the field of international trade rules — EU’s rights under international trade agreements (Article XXVIII of the GATT Agreement)
Commercial policy on international trade rights — EU's rights under International trade agreements(Article XXvIII)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — common commercial policy
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down common EU commercial policy in order to ensure the exercise of the European Union's (EU's) rights under the International Trade Agreements (ITAs) and the World Trade Organization (WTO).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all goods and services originating in the EU from non-EU countries.
It does not apply to services originating outside the EU.
EU countries must ensure the application of the regulation within their respective territories.
The European Commission may adopt commercial policy measures suspending concessions or other obligations in the fields of trade in goods or services. These measures may be suspended, modified or repealed by the EU if they:
are contrary to the EU's interests;
are detrimental to the interests of the EU or its economic operators;
have a disproportionate impact on the EU economy or the economic interests of EU countries or EU countries’ economic operators.
In the case of a breach of EU trade rules, the EU may adopt corrective measures. These may include:
suspension, modification and repeal of measures suspending or withdrawing concessions;
rebalancing concessions and other obligations;
restructuring concessions and obligations; or
restoring the level of concessions that the EU has previously enjoyed.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

Summary 95:
Stabilisation mechanism for bananas — EU countries’ rights to apply safeguard measures (Article 106 of the Agreement on Trade in Services between the EU and the EU countries and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA))
Stabilising mechanism for banana — EU country’s rights to use safeguard measures
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — the stabilisation mechanism under the agreement on trade in services between the European Union and the EFTA countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries have the right to use a safeguard mechanism to suspend or increase the rate of customs duty on bananas imported into the EU.
It sets out the conditions for the use of safeguard measures.
KEY POINTS
The stabilisation system for bananas is set up by the agreement concluded between EU countries, the European Commission and EFTA.
EU countries are allowed to apply provisional safeguard measures in critical circumstances.
The measures may take one of the following forms:
a suspension of a further reduction of the tariff on the product concerned provided for in the Tariff Elimination Schedule;
an increase to a level which does not exceed the lesser of:
the most-favoured-nation (‘MFN’) applied rate of import duty on the products concerned at the time the measure is taken; or
the MFN applied rate on the day immediately preceding the date of entry into force of the agreement.
Imposition of provisional safeguards must be based on verifiable facts and not merely on an allegation, a conjecture or a remote possibility.
A safeguard measure may not apply for more than 200 calendar days.
Prior surveillance measures must have a limited period of validity.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Bananas (European Commission).

  барска  гарка

Summary 96:
Creditors’ right to preserve their assets in the event of insolvency
Creditor’s right to safeguard their assets (i.e. the assets of a person or entity)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/849 on the enforcement of judgments, court settlements and authentic instruments in civil matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the judgment, court settlement or authentic instrument issued by a creditor is enforced in the EU country in which the debtor is resident.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Ireland, where it applies to the United Kingdom (1) and the Republic of Ireland (1).
It does not apply to:
non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway and Switzerland, which have jurisdiction to rule on the substance of the matter in accordance with the relevant rules of jurisdiction applicable.
The creditor must obtain a judgment or court settlement before the creditor can issue a preservation order.
If the creditor has not yet obtained a judgment and/or an authentic instrument, the creditor is entitled to issue a Preservation Order.
Conditions for issuing the order
The order must contain the following information:
a description of the subject matter of the case and the electronic version of the form to be used for the declaration pursuant to Article 25.3 of the regulation;
the creditor must have sufficient funds to cover the costs of enforcing the order;
it must be executed by the creditor within 10 working days of the date of the execution of the order and be effective for at least 5 years from the date on which the order was issued.
It must also contain:
the amount of the judgment and the court settlement;
a statement of the identity of the creditor;
an explanation of the reasons for the decision;
information concerning the security of the debtor.
An order may be refused if the creditor:
does not have the necessary funds to execute the order or if the order has not been executed;
is unable to satisfy the conditions or requirements of the preservation order; or
is not enforceable.
A creditor may refuse to enforce the order if the debtor:
has not paid the amount due to the creditor or has not paid a security deposit or an alternative assurance;
has failed to pay the security or the alternative assurance or a mortgage.
In the event that the creditor fails to satisfy these conditions, he or she may ask the court of the country of origin to order that the order be executed.
Where the creditor does not have funds in his or her account or accounts, he/she may make a request for account information. The creditor may also do so in the absence of such a request.
When a creditor refuses to execute an order, the debtor has the right to appeal the decision to the Court of Justice of the European Union (CJEU).
The court of CJEU may, however, refuse to issue an order if it considers that the debtor does not meet the following conditions:
he/she is not a resident in the country where the order is to be executed,
the order has been executed by a person other than the creditor,
he or she has not provided the creditor with sufficient funds for the purpose of executing the order, or
the debtor has not made the necessary payment.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
Directive (EU, Euratom) 2018/829 has applied since 1 July 2018.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has adopted a number of measures to ensure the proper functioning of the internal market. These include:
Implementing Regulation (Euratom) No 849/2017, which sets out the rules for the enforcement by EU countries of judgments and court settlements, judgments and authentic instrument in civil and commercial matters, as well as the conditions for the execution by the courts of the EU countries in which these decisions are to be enforced.
For more information, see:
‘Protecting assets’ on the European Commission's website.

Summary 97:
EU border surveillance — non-discriminatory and non-refoulement measures for asylum seekers, refugees and asylum seekers (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
EU border surveying — no-discrimination, non-recourse measures for the protection of asylum seekers and refugees and refugees
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 562/2006 on border surveillance
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries carry out border surveillance at their external borders to prevent unauthorised border crossings, to counter cross-border criminality and to apprehend or take other measures against those persons who have crossed the border in an irregular manner.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to border surveillance operations carried out by EU countries at the external sea borders in the context of operational cooperation coordinated by the European Agency for the Management of Operational Cooperation at the External Borders of the Member States of the European Union (‘the Agency’).
It does not apply to:
border surveillance carried out in the territorial sea or the contiguous zone of the Schengen area, which are outside the scope of the regulation;
border control operations conducted by the EU countries on the territory of the other EU countries.
EU countries are responsible for:
detection of vessels suspected of being engaged in illegal activities at sea;
interception of persons on board;
search and rescue situations;
returning the vessel or persons to the host EU country or to a neighbouring participating EU country.
The participating units must collect and report information to the International Coordination Centre (ICC), which transmits that information to a national coordination centre of the EU country concerned.
If the vessel is suspected of having committed an illegal act, the participating units may take the following measures:
suspending the vessel’s operation;
restricting its movement;
detaining or seizing the vessel;
taking other measures to prevent the vessel from crossing the border;
refouling the vessel.
Where the vessel has been detained or seized, EU countries may authorise one or more of the measures laid down in paragraphs 1 and 2. These measures may include:
restraining the vessel until the vessel can be brought to the attention of the competent authorities of the host country or the neighbouring EU country;
releasing the vessel to a host country, or to the authorities of a neighbouring country; or
taking measures to return the vessel and its passengers to the country in which they were detained or detained.
In the event that the vessel does not comply with any of these measures, the competent authority of the participating country may take any other measure, including:
taking the vessel into custody;
disposing of the vessel, its passengers or its cargo;
arresting or seizing any persons or cargo on board.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATING APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2019. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Seaworthy vessel: a vessel that is capable of carrying out illegal activities, such as smuggling drugs and human beings, but which is under the jurisdiction of the country where it is registered.
* Unauthorised crossing: crossing a border illegally.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  �   бода
Commission Implementing Regulation(EU) 2018/853 of 1 January 2018 on border surveillance (OJ L 140, 1.2.2018, pp. 1-2)
RELATED DOCUMENTS

Summary 98:
EU asylum, migration and integration and internal security funds — common rules for beneficiaries and the EU’s financial interests
EU asylum and migration and integrated security funds – common principles for beneficiaries, the EU's financial interests and the European External Action Service
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 513/2014 on the European Asylum, Migration and Integration and Internal Security Funds
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the common principles and procedures for the European Union (EU), the European Commission, the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (OLAF) and EU countries’ responsible authorities.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries, EU countries and the Commission.
EU countries are responsible for:
providing asylum and integration services;
ensuring that beneficiaries receive the full amount due promptly.
Responsible authorities must:
ensure that beneficiaries have access to the services they need;
monitor and evaluate the implementation of the asylum, immigration and integration (MIMI) and the Internal Security Fund (ISF) programmes;
provide information to the public on the MIMI and ISF programmes and to the European Parliament and the Council.
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation and the specific regulations.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 16 April 2014.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Asylum, mobility and integration: the migration, integration and border control policies of the EU.
* Internal security fund: the instrument for financial support for police cooperation, preventing and combating crime, and crisis management and repealing Council Decision 2007/125/JHA (see page 93 of this Official Journal).
* Crisis management: the management of the external borders of EU countries.

Summary 99:
European Union financial support for asylum and migration
European Union funding for immigration and asylum
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 966/2012 on the European Union’s financial assistance for the migration and asylum fields
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the objectives of EU financial support and the eligible actions.
It aims to contribute to the efficient management of migration flows and to the implementation, strengthening and development of the common policy on asylum, subsidiary protection and temporary protection and the common immigration policy, while fully respecting the rights and principles enshrined in the Charter of Fundamental Rights of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The objectives of the regulation are to:
promote the efficient migration management and the integration of asylum seekers and non-EU nationals in the EU;
facilitate the transfer of beneficiaries of international protection from one EU country to another.
Eligible actions
EU countries are required to take the following actions:
support the efforts of the European Migration Network (EMN), a network of EU countries’ national contact points, to implement the objectives set out in the regulation;
provide financial support to the European Asylum Support Office (EASO) and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCA);
support EU countries in their efforts to resettle refugees;
support EASO in its activities in cooperation with the United Nations High Commissioner for Refugees (UNHCR).
The EU countries are also required to support the activities of the UNHCR, the European Agency for Refugees and Migration (EACH) and other non-governmental organisations (NGOs) in the field of migration.
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the regulation.
In addition to the objectives and the actions listed above, the regulation sets out a general framework for the implementation of eligible actions, including:
the available financial resources and their distribution;
the principles and mechanism for the establishment of common EU resettlement priorities; and
the financial assistance provided for the actions of the EMN.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 2 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
 
 			
For more information, see:
‘Asylum and migration’ on the ESM's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Refugee: a person who is a national of an EU country and who has been granted international protection (i.e. the right to live and work legally in another EU country).
* Non-EU national: an individual who is an EU national but who is not a resident of that EU country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, Euratom (OJ L 183, 2.12.2012, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, Euratatom) No 392/2012 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 100:
Protecting people and goods from increasingly transnational threats: EU action plan (2014-2021)
Protecting persons and goods against increasingly cross-border threats:EU action plan(2014-2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 515/2014 — enhanced actions at EU level to protect people and businesses from increasingly crossborder threats
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out enhanced EU action plans (EU actions) for the period 2014-2020.
KEY POINTS
EU actions
EU countries are encouraged to use a part of the global resources available for the national programmes to address the strategic EU priorities set out in Annex I to this regulation.
The objectives of the EU actions are to:
preventing and combating trans-border, serious and organised crime including terrorism;
strengthen coordination and cooperation between law enforcement authorities of EU countries and with relevant non-EU countries;
support the work carried out by EU countries’ competent authorities.
EU EU countries are also encouraged to take action in the field of health.
Eligible EU actions under national programmes
The EU actions listed in Annex II to the regulation are listed in the following categories:
national actions;
national programmes under the European External Action Service (EAS);
actions under the EU’s external borders and visa programme;
actions carried out under the Internal Security Fund (ISF).
The European Commission and the EU countries, together with the EAS, must ensure coordination as regards actions in and in relation to third countries, as well as with the European Union Agency (Eurojust) Europol, if applicable. EU actions must be implemented in accordance with Article 6(5) of the regulation. EU countries must also ensure that EU countries have the necessary resources to implement EU actions.
A common indicator for the measurement of the specific objectives is set out by the European Commission. It is based on:
the number of EU actions carried out;
the total number of national actions taken; and
the amount of EU resources used.
Annex III to the Regulation sets out the common indicators for the measure of the objectives. These indicators are based on the number of actions taken by the EU country and the total amount of resources used to implement those actions. The Commission and EU countries may also set up a common indicator to measure the impact of EU EU actions on the objectives identified in Annex III.
In addition, the Commission must draw up a report to the European Parliament and the Council on the implementation of this regulation and on the results achieved.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 1 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Transnational threat: a threat to the safety and security of people, goods and capital of an EU country.
* Countering transnational crime: the fight against terrorism, organised crime and the proliferation of weapons of mass destruction (WMDs).
* Terrorism: the threat posed by a group of armed groups, such as the Taliban, the Islamic State and the Taliban.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2014/515 of 20 July 2014 supplementing Regulation (EEC) No 605/2014 with regard to enhanced EU actions at Union level (OJ L 140, 21.7.2014, pp. 1-2)

Summary 101:
Restrictions on non-sustainable fishing of fish stocks of common interest
Restriction of non-Sustainable Fishing of fish stock of common interests
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — measures to restrict the importation of fish products caught by vessels conducting fisheries on a stock under the control of the country allowing non-soil sustainable fishing
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent non-EU countries from fishing on a fish stock under their control.
It sets out the measures to be taken by EU countries if they suspect that an EU country is fishing on the fish stock.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must notify the European Commission of any country that is suspected of allowing or attempting to fish on the fishery.
The notification must include information on the reasons for the identification of that country as a country allowing sustainable fishing and the possible measures that may be taken in relation to it pursuant to this regulation.
EU fishing vessels or EU fishing equipment must not be used for fishing the stock under its control. EU countries may restrict the access to ports for those vessels, or by preventing EU fishing vessels from being used to fish the stock in question.
This regulation does not apply to fishing in the waters of the Mediterranean or the Black Sea.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the world’s largest fishery for bluefin tuna.
For more information, see:
Bluefin tuna (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Non-EU country: a country that allows or attempts to fish fish stocks that are not sustainably managed.
* Sustainable fishing: fishing that is not sustainable for the long-term survival of the fish stocks.

Summary 102:
Bilateral investment agreements with non-EU countries (until 9 January 2013)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 182/2011 on bilateral investment agreements
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the conditions under which EU countries are empowered to amend or conclude bilateral agreements with third countries.
It aims to ensure that the EU’s exclusive competence with respect to the common commercial policy is respected.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to any agreement with a third country that contains provisions on investment protection.
EU countries must notify the European Commission within 90 days of receiving a notification of a bilateral investment agreement. The notification must include a copy of such an agreement.
Authorisation to open formal negotiations
The EU country concerned must inform the Commission without undue delay of all meetings which will take place under the agreement. It must also provide the Commission with all relevant information.
The Commission must then take a decision on the conclusion of the agreement, including:
the number of companies covered by the agreement;
the nature and scope of the investment protection; and
the terms and conditions of the dispute settlement mechanism.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The new regulation entered into force on 1 January 2013.
BACKGROUND
The European Union has exclusive competence in the area of trade and investment.
For more information, see:
Trade and Investment (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Investment protection: the protection of intellectual property rights (IP rights) of companies operating in the EU.
* Bilateral investment agreement: an agreement between an EU country and another EU country to invest in a business in the territory of the other EU country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 181/2011 of 9 January 2011 laying down conditions for the negotiation or conclusion by EU countries under Article 2(1)(e) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU) (OJ L 183, 9.1.2011, pp. 1-8)

Summary 103:
Tariff preferences for sustainable development and good governance in the European Union (EU)
Tariffs preferences for Sustainable development and Good governance
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2013 on the general arrangement for preferential market access in the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the general arrangements for the EU’s common market access (GSP) and special incentive arrangement (SIA) in the fields of sustainable development, good governance and environmental protection.
KEY POINTS
The GSP and SIA are bilateral agreements between the EU and non-EU countries.
The general arrangement allows the EU to grant tariff preferences to certain products, such as agricultural products, to beneficiary countries of the GSP. The tariff preferences are based on the combined national code (CN) of the beneficiary country.
In order to ensure uniform conditions for the implementation of this regulation, implementing powers are conferred on the Commission. The Commission adopts implementing acts to amend Annex III of the regulation.
Annex IV sets out a list of the products covered by the tariff preferences.
A temporary withdrawal of tariff preferences may be made by the Commission on the basis of the following reasons:
the beneficiary country no longer meets the criteria for granting tariff preferences;
bankruptcies;
profitability;
capacity utilisation;
employment;
imports;
prices.
If the Commission finds that the reasons justifying a temporary withdrawal no longer apply, it may adopt a delegated act to reinstate the preferential tariff preferences provided under the SIA.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The EU is the largest trading partner of the world, with a gross domestic product (GDP) of more than €2.5 trillion.
For more information, see:
‘Tariffs’ and ‘SIA’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

Summary 104:
EU measures to prevent, combat and eradicate the illicit manufacture of and trafficking in firearms, their parts and components and ammunition (UN Firearms Protocol)
EU measures for preventing, combating and eradicating the illicit manufacturing and trafficking within the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2015/849 — measures to combat, combat, eradicate and eliminate the illicit production and trafficking of firearms, parts and essential components and ammunitions (UN firearms protocol)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent and combat the illicit trade in firearms and parts and ammunition, as well as the illicit import and transit of such weapons within the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The UN Firearms Protocol, the purpose of which is to promote, facilitate and strengthen cooperation among parties to the International Criminal Tribunal for the former Yugoslavia (ICTY) and the Organisation for the Prohibition of Chemical Weapons (OPCW), aims to:
prevent and combat illicit production, manufacture, transport and import of firearms and their parts;
combat the illicit trafficking in ammunition;
contribute to the eradication of organised crime.
The EU is a party to the protocol.
EU countries must ensure that:
the export of firearms to a non-EU country is authorised by the competent authorities of the EU country where the exporter is established;
the exporter has a valid export authorisation established in accordance with the form set out in Annex I of the implementing regulation;
an EU country’s competent authorities have the authority to control the import and transshipment of firearms.
Importers must:
provide the EU with the necessary documentation to prove that they have a legitimate right to export firearms;
produce the relevant customs importation documents to confirm the receipt of the dispatched shipment of firearms; and
producer the necessary documents to prove they have the necessary right to import the firearms and the essential components or ammunition.
An EU country may refuse to grant an EU country an authorisation if it considers that the exporting country has not complied with the export requirements.
Exporters may also refuse to export if they consider that the importing country does not comply with the requirements of the exporting EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THE IMPLEMENTING REGULATIONS APPLY?
Implementation of the regulation has applied since 3 July 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 3 July 2016.
BACKGROUND
The United Nations Framework Convention on the Suppliers of Arms and Ammunition (UNFCTA) entered into force on 3 July 2005.
For more information, see:
‘The UN firearms protocol’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission Implementing Regulation(EU) 1549 of 4 July 2015 laying down measures for combating, combating, eradicating and eliminating the illicit supply and trafficking on the territory of the Union and repealing Council Regulation (EC) No 515/97 (OJ L 183, 5.7.2015, pp. 1-8)

Summary 105:
EUROSUR — the European Border Surveillance System (EBSS) (European Agency for the Protection of the Internal Border of the European Union)
EuroOSUR – the European Asylum Support and Migration Agency (EASA)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1052/2013 on the European border surveillance system (EBITS)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up EuroOSUR, a European Union agency responsible for detecting, preventing and combating illegal immigration and cross-border crime and contributing to ensuring the protection and saving the lives of migrants.
It aims to improve the situational awareness and reaction capability of EU countries’ authorities at the external borders of the EU (‘external borders’).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation sets out the role and responsibilities of EuroosUR.
EURosUR is responsible for:
monitoring the EU’s external borders;
providing the EU countries with the infrastructure and tools needed to improve their situational awareness, reaction capability at the border;
cooperating with other EU countries to detect, prevent and combat illegal immigration;
coordinating with non-EU countries to prevent illegal immigration.
EU countries must designate a national coordination centre to coordinate the activities of the agency.
The European situational picture (ESP) is a common European intelligence picture that is used by EU countries and Euroosur. It is composed of the following elements:
the national situational picture;
the events layer;
pre-frontier intelligence picture; and
the operational area of a joint operation, pilot project or rapid intervention coordinated by the Agency.
National coordination centre
EuroosUR operates twenty-four hours a day and seven days a week. It has a staff of 1,000 staff and is based in the EU country in which it is based.
Implementation of the regulation
The EU countries are responsible for implementing the regulation. The EU countries have the power to set up their own national coordination centres.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
European border surveillance (European Commission)
European asylum support and migration (Euroosur Agency).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Commission Regulation (EU, Eurosur) 2013/1052 of 20 December 2013 laying down the operational framework for the European External Borders Surveillance System and repealing Regulation (EC) No 562/2006 (OJ L 295, 6.12.2013, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Eurosur, EurOSUR) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 106:
Eurodac — the fingerprint database for the fight against terrorism and other serious criminal offences
EuroDac —the fingerprint database to fight terrorism and/or other seriouscriminal offences
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — the Eurodac fingerprint database
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the conditions under which EU countries can exchange fingerprint data for the purpose of preventing, detecting or investigating terrorist offences or other serious crimes.
KEY POINTS
EU countries’ designated authorities for law enforcement purposes are responsible for the processing of fingerprint data.
The data are processed by the authorities of the EU countries for the purposes laid down in Article 1(2) of the regulation (see summary).
The EU country of origin must ensure the security of the data before and during transmission to the Central System as well as the security when it receives data from the central system.
Each EU country must notify the European Commission of its designated authorities, of the operating units and of its verifying authority, and notify without delay any amendment to the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES IT APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 20 October 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into their national law by 20 October 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of this regulation on its website.
For more information, see:
‘‘The fingerprint database’’ on the website of the Commission’s Directorate-General for Justice and Home Affairs.

Summary 107:
Protected designations of origin and protected geographical indications — labelling
Protected designs of origin (PDOs and PGI) and traditional specialities guaranteed (TSPs) — the labelling of products linked to a geographical area
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — rules for protected designations and protected geographic indications
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out rules for the protection of trade marks, designations* and geographical indications (PGIs) on foodstuffs.
It aims to ensure fair returns for producers of goods linked to an EU geographical area by:
ensuring uniform protection of the names as an intellectual property right in the territory of the EU;
providing clear information on the value-adding attributes of the product to consumers.
KEY POINTS
Protection
The protection of PDOs and PGIs is based on a set of principles:
the protection of intellectual property rights (IP rights) is guaranteed by the EU countries;
the use of the protected designation of origin or protected geographical indication is permitted as long as it does not infringe on the rights of the producers of the products.
The EU countries must ensure that PDOs or PGIs are used only on products that are produced in the EU and that they do not appear on products which are produced outside the EU.
Labelling
PDOs or PGI’s must be accompanied by a product specification. The product specification must state the name of the producer and the geographical area where the PDO or PGI originates.
If a PDO is used as an ingredient in a product, the product must be labelled as ‘PDO’ or ‘PGI’.
A PDO, PGI or TSP must not be used on a product which is produced in a non-EU country and which is not produced in an EU country.
An EU country may require that a protected PDO be used for a product that is produced outside its territory.
EU countries must set up a quality scheme for PDOs, PGIs and TSPs. The quality scheme must be established by the competent national authority of the country in which the PDOs/PGIs originate. The EU country responsible for the quality scheme is responsible for ensuring that the protected PDOs / PGI are used for the intended purpose.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
Protective designations OF origin and PGI
For more information, see:
‘Protected Designations of Origin’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Protected designation: a trade mark or a geographical indication that designates a trans-border geographical area or in the case of a ‘traditional specialities guarantee’ name.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission Regulation (EC) No 852/2017 of 1 January 2017 laying down rules for protection of trademarks, trade marks and trade indications on foods and agricultural products and amending Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 182/2011 of the European Parliament and of the Council (OJ L 183, 1.1.2017, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) 2018/922 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?

KEY POINTS

Summary 109:
European System of Financial Supervision (ESFS) (European Union)
European Systemof Financial Supervisory (ESF) (Europa)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1092/2010 on the structure of supervision of the financial sector in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the structure and tasks of the European Supervisory Authority (ESAF), the European Banking Authority (EBA) and the European Securities and Markets Authority.
KEY POINTS
The ESFS is responsible for supervising financial institutions operating in the EU.
It is a single supervisory body responsible for the supervision of all financial institutions across the EU, including banks, insurance companies, pension funds and savings and loan funds.
The ESRB is the EU’s central supervisory authority. It has the power to:
make recommendations to the EBA, EBA and the ESA;
make decisions on the supervision and supervision of financial institutions;
provide advice to the other EU supervisory authorities;
act as a mediator between the EU and non-EU countries when a financial institution fails to comply with the requirements of the ESFS;
ensure that all EU countries’ financial institutions comply with all relevant EU rules.
ESAF has a number of tasks, including:
supervision of the banking sector;
supervisory of the securities and market sectors;
monitoring and assessment of systemic risk;
implementing technical standards.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2011.

Summary 110:
Freedom of movement and employment of workers in the European Union (EU) (codified version)
Freedom of mobility and employment in the EU (Codified Version)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2005 on the freedom of movement of workers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that workers from one EU country have the same rights and benefits as workers from another EU country.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to nationals of an EU country and any employer pursuing an activity in the territory of a EU country, and may conclude and perform contracts of employment in accordance with the provisions in force laid down by law, regulation or administrative action, without any discrimination resulting therefrom.
EU countries must send to the European Commission information on problems arising in connection with the movement of the workers and particulars of the state and development of employment. This information must be brought up to date regularly.
An Advisory Committee composed of representatives of the EU countries meets at least twice a year.
The European Coordination Office (ECO) is responsible for:
coordinating the practical measures necessary for vacancy clearance at EU level and for analysing the resulting movements of workers;
contributing to such objectives by implementing, in cooperation with the Technical Committee, joint methods of action at administrative and technical levels;
carrying out, where a special need arises, and in agreement with the specialist services, the bringing together of vacancies and applications for employment for clearance by those specialist services.
Employment and equality of treatment
EU workers have the right to:
exchange their applications for and offers of employment;
be treated equally on grounds of nationality by comparison with nationals of the other EU country who wish to pursue the same activity;
have the right of eligibility for workers’ representative bodies in the undertaking.
A worker who is a national of a Member State and who is employed in another EU State shall enjoy all the rights and rights accorded to national workers in matters of housing, including ownership of the housing he needs.
Workers who are employed in an EU Member State must be treated equally in respect of:
living and working conditions and the state of the labour market;
health and safety;
education and training;
social protection;
personal safety and security;
protection of personal data and personal data;
identification of health and safety equipment;
access to medical and vocational care;
the right to leave and return to the country in which he or she is employed.
National workers must not be treated differently than workers from the other Member State.
Vacancies must be cleared by the competent authorities of the Member State in which they are to be employed. The Member States must notify the ECO of any vacancies that are not cleared by their competent authorities.
Where a vacancy is not cleared, the Commission must set up an Advisory Committee to advise the Member States on how to deal with the situation. The Advisory Committee is composed of the representatives of each Member State, and is assisted by the Commission.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2005.
BACKGROUND
 
  *   *
For more information, see:
‘Freedom of Movement and Employment of Workers’ on the EU’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Regulation (EC, EC, EEC, OJ L 552, 1.7.2005, pp. 1-3)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, Euratom, EEA, EEO, EEE, EIE, EEU, EMEA, EPEA) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 111:
Ozone-depleting substances — EU rules on their disposal, recovery and destruction
Ozone - depletion substances —EU rules on its disposal and recovery
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 596/2004 on the placing on the market of controlled substances for essential laboratory and analytical uses
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure the safe disposal of all substances listed in the Protocol to the International Convention on the Law of the Sea (OCTS) by ensuring that they can be recovered, recovered and destroyed.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
products, equipment or containers containing more than 6.5 kg of substances;
other substances with a boiling point > 20.5 °C;
products and equipment with a fluid charge of 30 kg or more;
operators of refrigeration, air conditioning or heat pump equipment, or fire protection systems, which contain controlled substances.
EU countries must ensure that:
the products and equipment are checked for leakage at least once every 3 months;
the detected leakage is repaired as soon as possible and in any event within 14 days.
The Commission may determine the form and content of the label to be used.
Labelling
Labels must contain the following information: the name of the manufacturer or importer of the product or equipment;the quantity of the substances to be recovered or destroyed;the number of containers containing the substances;the type of equipment used to recover or destroy the substances and the method of recovery;the method of destruction;the amount of the chemicals to be destroyed or recovered.
Annexes to the regulation provide information on:
recovery and destruction of used controlled substances
the methods of recovery and disposal;
methods of destruction.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
Directive (EU) 2017/852 has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU is a party to the OTSS.
For more information, see:
‘Ozone’ on the European Commission’s website.

European Union Environmental Agency
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 1210/90 — setting up the European Environment Agency (EEA)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European Union (EU) environment agency.
It aims to:
ensure the protection of human health and the environment;
promote cooperation between EU countries in the field of environmental protection;
provide information to the public on the environmental situation in the EU and on the activities of the EEA.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The EEA is responsible for protecting human health, the environment and the social aspects of the EU’s internal market.
The regulation provides for the establishment of a European Environment Information and Observation Network (EEOI) to provide the public with information on the environment, including on the effects of human activities on human health. It also aims to promote cooperation between the EU countries and the EEOI.
EU countries are responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
Rules of conduct

the EEA has a management board, which is composed of representatives of EU countries, the European Commission and the European Atomic Energy Community (Euratom).
EAEI has a legal personality. It has a budget of €1.5 billion for the period 2014-2020. It is funded by the general budget of the European Union.
EACEI has the following legal personality:
its directorates are appointed by the Commission for a period of 3 years;
its executive director is appointed for a term of 5 years; and
the directorates have the power to appoint or dismiss staff.
Each EEA directorate is assisted by a scientific committee.
A management board is composed by representatives from each EU country. It meets at least once a year and has a maximum term of 6 years.

Summary 113:
Environmental management systems for small and medium-sized enterprises (EMAS)
Environmental management system for small organisations ( EMAS)SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2008 on the environmental management systems and environmental management system of small organisations
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that small organisations in the European Union (EU) have the necessary environmental and management systems in place to meet the objectives of the EU’s environmental action programme.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU-registered organisations, including small organisations.
It does not apply to:
non-EU countries, such as Iceland, Liechtenstein, Norway, Switzerland and Switzerland, which are not part of the European Economic Area (EEA) or the European Free Trade Association (EFTA).
It applies to EU countries that have not yet joined the EEA or the EFTA, but which wish to do so by 31 December 2020.
The EEA and EFTA are not parties to the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that:
organisations comply with the requirements of the regulation by:
establishing, implementing and maintaining a system of environmental management;
implementing and maintaining an environmental management programme;
ensuring that the organisation has the necessary resources to meet its environmental objectives.
Organisations must establish, implement and maintain a procedure(s) for:
internal communication among the various levels and functions of the organisation;
receiving, documenting and responding to relevant communication from external interested parties;
coordinating and coordinating with other organisations;
providing information to the public on the organisation's environmental objectives;
assessing the environmental impact of its activities;
monitoring and evaluating its environmental performance;
conducting an internal environmental audit;
validating the environmental aspects of its organisation and its management system;
checking that its environmental aspects and management system are in line with EU and international standards.
Regulatory bodies
The European Commission appoints a competent body to carry out the tasks of environmental verifiers. The competent body must:
assess the organisations’ compliance with the regulation;
check that the organisations have implemented the requirements for environmental management and their environmental management programmes;
establish procedures for the accreditation and licensing of the organisations; and
ensure that the competent body has the power to suspend or withdraw the organisations' accreditation or licensing.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 December 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
 * Environmental management system: a system that ensures that an organisation has a set of environmental objectives and that the environment is protected.
* Environment impact: any change in an organisation's operation, structure, administration, processes, activities, products or services that has or can have a significant impact on the environment, the environment or human health.

SUMMARY OF:
Regulating the definition and presentation of spirits and their components
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the labelling and the definition of spirits, their components and their geographical origin.
It aims to ensure that spirit drinks and all of their components are of any origin other than agricultural, within the meaning of Annex I to the Treaty on the Functioning of the European Union (TFEU).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all spirit drinks produced in the EU. It does not apply to spirits produced in non-EU countries.
Labelling
A spirit drink must bear the geographical indication ‘agricultural’ on its label.
The label must contain the following information:
the geographical area of origin;
the method for obtaining the spirit drink and, if appropriate, the authentic and unvarying local methods;
any requirements laid down by EU and/or national and regional provisions;
a name and contact address of the applicant, according to the relevant technical file.
EU countries must ensure that the geographical indications are accompanied by the sales denomination ‘rum’, ‘wine’ or ‘vegetable spirit’ accompanied by any other geographical indication of the EU countries or the Autonomous Region of Madeira as registered in Annex III.
Regulatory procedure
The European Commission is responsible for the regulation’s implementation and for the implementation of the regulation. It is assisted by the European Commission Directorate-General for Food Safety and Health, the European Food Safety Authority and the European Agency for Rural Development.

Summary 115:
Safeguarding civil aviation: common rules for operators and other entities
Securitisation of civil aviation — common rules to protect persons and goods within the European Union
The European Union (EU) has adopted a common set of rules for the protection of passengers and goods in civil aviation.
ACT
Council Regulation (EC) No 552/2004 of 26 February 2004 establishing common rules on the protection and management of persons and good goods in the European Community (civil aviation).
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out common standards for the security of passengers, goods and infrastructure within the EU.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must draw up a national civil aviation security programme.
The national security programme must:
ensure that air carriers and other operators comply with the common basic standards on aviation security;
provide a quality control programme to ensure that the air carrier complies with the requirements of the security programme of the EU country from which it provides services.
Each EU country must designate a competent authority to carry out inspections of airports, operators and entities applying aviation security standards.
National authorities must ensure that airports and operators apply the security standards set out in the national security programmes.
Airports and operators must ensure:
the safety of passengers;
the security of goods;
security of infrastructure;
safety of infrastructure.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 March 2005. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 March 2006.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Civil aviation: any form of air transport in the EU, including cargo and mail.
* Air carrier: an air transport undertaking holding a valid operating licence or equivalent;
operator: a company that provides goods and/or services to or through airports in an EU country.

EU targeted restrictive measures against human rights violations and abuses in the Middle East and North Africa (Mauritius, Syria, Libya, North Korea, Ukraine, Syria and the Democratic Republic of the Congo) and the region of the former Yugoslav Republic of Macedonia (Slovenia, Serbia, Montenegro, Kosovo, Ukraine and the Republic of Moldova)

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to prevent the freezing of funds and economic resources of, and the prohibition to make funds available to, natural or legal persons* and entities* responsible for or providing support for, committing, facilitating, facilitating or otherwise supporting human rights or abuses, as well as those associated with the natural and legal persons and entities covered.

Annexes to Annex I are:
financial commitments;
documents showing evidence of an interest in funds or financial resources;
financial guarantees or financial indemnities;
other non-state actors, subject to Article 1(4) of Decision (CFSP) 2020/1999.

Summary 117:
EU naval operation in the Mediterranean (EUNAVFOR MED IRINI)
European naval operation (EU NAVFOR MEDIRINI)*
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (CFSP) 2020/472 on a European Union military operation in Libya
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION?
It authorises the European Union (EU) to deploy an EU naval mission in Libya to prevent the illicit export of petroleum from Libya, to capacity building and training of the Libyan Coast Guard and Navy, and to disrupt the business model of human smuggling and trafficking networks.
KEY POINTS
The EU’s mission is to:
combat human trafficking and human trafficking networks through information gathering and patrolling carried out by aerial assets above the high seas, in the agreed Area of Interest;
contribute to the training of Libyan coast guard and navy;
provide humanitarian assistance to the Libyan people;
ensure the protection of the lives and property of Libyan nationals;
combat the trafficking of drugs, weapons and human beings.
The operation is managed by the European Maritime Safety Agency (EMSA) and the European Border and Coast Guard Agency (Eurojust).
The European Union Military Committee (EUMC) is responsible for coordinating the operation and providing the operational control and strategic direction of the operation. The EUMC is chaired by the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy (HR).
FROM WHEN DOES THE DECISIONS APPLY?
The decision has applied since 31 March 2020 and had to become law in the EU countries by 31 March 2021.
BACKGROUND
The Council of the EU authorised the EU to deploy a naval mission to the Mediterranean in 2020.
For more information, see:
European Union military operations in Libya (European External Action Service).

Summary 118:
European Border and Coast Guard Agency (EBCG) — FADO system (European Union Agency for the Protection of Human Rights and Fundamental Freedoms) (European Commission).
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/1896 on the European Border and Cart Guard Agency’s use of the Facility for the Analysis of Authentic Documents (FADO) system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a European border and coast guard agency, the EBCG, which is responsible for the operation of the facility for the analysis of authentic documents and for the detection of false documents.
KEY POINTS
   
The European Union (EU), the European Commission and the European Maritime and Fisheries Agency (EMFSA) are responsible for setting up the facility and for its operation.
The ECBG operates the facility on a need-to-know basis.
It has the power to adopt delegated acts on the operation and maintenance of the system.
EU countries and the EU agencies have the right to use the facility to:
identify authentic documents or authentic documents that have been counterfeited;
detect false documents or false versions thereof;
make recommendations on effective ways of detecting specific methods of falsification and other useful related information.
To identify authentic documents, the EU countries and EU agencies must send the Agency information on:
authentic documents;
authenticated documents which have been forged or counterfeited.
In addition, the Agency must provide information on other related official documents, in particular those used in support of applications for official documents issued by EU Member States and, where applicable, third parties, such as third countries, territorial entities, international organisations and other entities subject to international law, and on false versions of those documents. The Agency may also provide the general public with access to specimens of authentic and false documents with pseudonymised data.
Agency staff must be trained in the field of document fraud and have the necessary expertise to detect and prevent the falsification of official documents. They must also be able to identify false documents and to identify the methods used to commit such fraud.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2020.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Authentic document: a document issued by an EU country to a non-EU country.
* False document: an authentic document that has been forged, counterfeited or is likely to be forged.

Summary 119:
Access to, or the pursuit of, regulated professions: proportionality of requirements
Assessment of requirements restricting access to, and the pursuit thereof
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/60/EU on the proportionality and transparency of requirements relating to the regulation of healthcare professions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) has a high level of health and safety standards for the regulated professions in the healthcare sector.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all professions covered by the European Medicines Agency (EMSA) and the European Commission’s Directorate-General for Health and Consumers.
It does not apply to the following professions:
physicians, pharmacists, dentists, optometrists, opticians, optomists and opticians.
The scope of the directive does not include:
professional organisations or bodies, professional ethics, supervision and liability.
Regulation
EU countries must apply the principle of proportionality when determining whether a regulation is proportionate to the public health or public safety objectives.
They must ensure that:
the regulation of a profession does not restrict access to the profession;
it does not discriminate against a profession based on sex, race, colour, religion or sexual orientation;
there is no discrimination based on age, disability, sexual orientation or any other characteristic.
If a regulation does not meet the public interest objectives, EU countries must take into account the objective of ensuring accessibility and high quality of healthcare for citizens, and adequate and safe supply of medicinal products, taking into consideration the margin of discretion referred to in Article 1 of this directive.
Where EU countries consider a regulation to be proportionate, they must inform the Commission of the reasons for such a decision.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Access to healthcare’ on the European Agency for Medicines and Consumers’ website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
Directivity 2014/40/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2014 on the proportional and transparency requirements relating thereto (OJ L 127, 1.1.2015, pp. 1-2)

Summary 120:
Fishing in EU waters of the North Sea and the Baltic Sea (2014-2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the EU’s common fisheries policy (CFP)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for fishing in European Union waters (EU waters) of the north and Baltic seas.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all stocks of species in the North and Baltic Seas (North Sea and Baltic Sea) which are covered by the EU fisheries policy.
It sets out the maximum sustainable yield (MSY) and the maximum biomass (MBSY) that can be harvested by EU countries.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
EU countries must ensure that the MSY and MBSY are maintained at levels that are sustainable for the long-term.
Fisheries must take into account the difficulty of fishing all stocks at MSY at the same time, especially in situations where that leads to a premature closure of the fishery.
Management of by-catch stocks must be managed in a way that minimises the impact of the by-catches on the overall MSY.
In addition, EU countries must take account of the likely catch composition of vessels participating in mixed fisheries.
A management plan must be drawn up by the European Commission and the International Commission for the Law of the Sea (ICES).
The plan must contain a range of FMSY and biomass reference points, i.e. the maximum yield and maximum biomass of the stocks concerned. The ranges must be set at the level of MSY in normal use and, provided that the stock concerned is considered to be in a good state, an upper limit for certain cases.
An EU country must notify the Commission and ICES of the ranges and the capacity ceilings that it has set for its fisheries. The Commission must inform the European Parliament and the Council of its decision to adopt a delegated act.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
    баба  
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Maximum sustainable yield: the total yield of fish stocks over a given period of time.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 1308/2013 of 21 December 2013 laying down the common fishery policy for the EU and its Member States (OJ L 183, 28.12.2013, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 1308 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 122:
Controls on the movement of cash in the EU (Regulation (EU) 2016/794)
Controlling the movements of cash within the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2016/798/EU — controls with respect to cash entering or leaving the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out a system of controls with regard to cash* entering and leaving the EU to complement the legal framework for the prevention of money laundering and terrorist financing.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all cash entering and departing the EU. It does not apply to cash coming from non-EU countries, such as the United Kingdom (1) or the United States (1).
It does not cover cash coming into or going out of the EU in the form of cash couriers.
Controls
EU countries have the power to require the sender or the recipient, or a representative thereof, to make a disclosure declaration, systematically or on a case-by-case basis, in accordance with national procedures.
The declaration must be accompanied by a statement of reasons and should adequately describe the specific factors that have given rise to the action. The declaration must include the name, address and contact details of the sender and the recipient.
Provision of information
The competent authorities and the Financial Intelligence Unit (FIU) must:
provide information to the competent authorities on:
the movements of unaccompanied cash, for example cash in postal packages, courier shipments, unaccompanied luggage or containerised cargo;
the amount of cash that has entered or left the EU, including the value of the cash; and
the name and address of the person or persons responsible for the cash movements.
Imposition of penalties
The legislation sets out the maximum penalties that EU countries can impose on natural persons who are found to have committed or are suspected of committing a criminal offence. These penalties must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 2 December 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 2 December 2017.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to combat money laundering, terrorist financing and other forms of illicit activity.
For more information, see:
‘The fight against money laundering’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Directive 2016/799/EU of 22 November 2016 on controls with reference to cash (OJ L 183, 3.12.2016, pp. 1-8)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
