Summary 123:
Integrated national energy and climate plans for the EU (2014-2020)
Integmented national energy & climate plans (2014 - 2020)
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/20/EU on the energy union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s energy union (EU) has a coherent, integrated and sustainable energy policy.
It sets out the objectives and targets for energy security, energy efficiency, decarbonisation and competitiveness.
KEY POINTS
The energy union should cover five dimensions: energy security; the internal energy market; energy efficiency; decarbonising; and research, innovation and competitiveness (i.e. the development of low-carbon technologies and energy efficiency).
EU countries must set out their policies and measures for each of the five dimensions in an integrated national energy plan. These must include:
the total volume of each type of fuel or energy supplied;
energy efficiency;
decarbonisation;
research and innovation;
competitive energy markets.
The EU countries’ national energy plans must also include the following information:
energy security;
the internal energy markets;
carbon capture and storage (CCS);
the energy efficiency potentials of gas and electricity infrastructure;
cost-effectiveness of the energy system and GHG emission developments.
Each EU country must also provide the European Commission with a report on the progress it has made in achieving its energy security objectives and its climate targets. The report must be published in the Official Journal of the European Union.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The directive entered into force on 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2014.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The Energy Union’ on the European Agency for Energy and Rural Affairs’ website.
* KEY TERMS
* Energy Union: a European Union policy that aims to:
ensure energy security and decarbonise;
improve energy efficiency and competitiveness;
reduce greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions;
increase the efficiency of energy infrastructure; and
improve the competitiveness of the retail energy market.

Release reduction and minimisation in the European Union (EU))
SUMMARY OF:
Implementing Regulation (EC) No 552/2019 on the release of certain hazardous substances into the environment
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to reduce the amount of waste from the production, processing and transport of certain chemicals by:
reducing the release, minimising the release and eliminating the waste resulting from these processes;
ensuring that these substances do not end up in the environment.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all industrial processes, mixtures and articles used in the manufacture, processing or transport of substances listed in Annex III of the regulation.
It does not apply to:
processes used for the manufacture of chemicals listed in the annexes to the regulation;
processing used for chemical separation;
chemical separation for the production of chemicals.
The list of substances covered by the regulation is set out in Annexes I and II.
EU countries must:
implement measures to reduce or minimise the release or release of the substances listed by Annex III;
take measures to minimise or eliminate the releases of these substances;
consider alternative processes, techniques or practices that have similar usefulness but which avoid the formation and release of substances.
Monitoring
The European Commission is responsible for monitoring the implementation of this regulation. It is assisted by the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work (ESHAW) and the European Monitoring Centre for the Prevention of Pollution from Chemicals (EMCP).

Summary 125:
Single-use plastic products on the European Union’s territory
Single-used plastic products upon the territory of the EU
SUMMARY OF:
Directive (EU) 2019/883 on the use of plastic on EU territory
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to reduce the amount of plastic waste generated on EU territories by reducing the number of plastic products that are found on EU soil.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all plastic products placed on the market in the EU. It does not apply to:
food containers;
personal care products;
towels and sanitary products; and
personal hygiene products.
EU countries must ensure that all single-use products listed in Part E of the directive are placed on their territory.
Labelling
Labels must be clearly visible on the packaging of the product and on the product itself. The labelling must also indicate the quantity of plastic used and the purpose for which the product is intended.
The labelling should also indicate:
the number of times the product has been used;
the amount of waste generated from such products, including as litter, in the same year in that EU country.
An EU country may require that the labelling of a product be accompanied by a statement of the type of plastic it is made of and the type and quantity of waste it is intended to generate.
Extended producer responsibility
An extended producer responsibility scheme must be established for producers of certain plastic products to cover the costs of cleaning up litter. The scheme should be effective, proportionate and dissuasive. EU countries may also establish deposit-refund schemes to encourage the recycling of the plastic.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The legislation entered into force on 3 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
The EU has adopted a number of measures to reduce plastic waste on its territory. These include:
a ban on the manufacture, import and sale of plastic bags;
a prohibition on the placing on the EU market of plastic containers containing more than 10 pieces of plastic;
ban on the putting into service of plastic food containers and personal care products. These measures aim to limit plastic waste to a minimum of 10 pieces per person per day.
For more information, see:
‘Plastic waste’ (European Commission).

EU Trade mark enforcement – enforcement of Intellectual property rights
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2008 on the enforcement of EU trade marks
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for enforcing the EU trade mark and collective marks.
It aims to ensure that the EU Trade Marks Office (ETMO) is independent of the European Commission.
KEY POINTS
The regulation lays down rules for the enforcement and enforcement of the rights of the proprietor of an EU trademark, collective mark, guarantee mark or guarantee mark.
The proprietor has the exclusive right to use the EU trademark or collective mark for the purposes of marketing, advertising or other commercial activities. The proprietor is responsible for ensuring that the trademark is used only for the purpose it was originally intended and does not infringe the rights conferred on him under Articles 31(3), 34(5), 35(3) and 35(4) of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU).
The European Commission has the power to adopt implementing acts on the protection of EU trademarks.

Trade mark: a trade mark used for marketing purposes (e.g. mark, certification mark) and guarantee mark (certificate mark).
EU collective mark: an EU collective mark used to mark a product, service, service or product or service that is intended for sale in the EU.

Summary 127:
Accessible formats for the benefit of beneficiary persons
Accessible format for beneficiaries persons
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1564 on the making and dissemination of copies, in accessible formats, of certain works and other protected subject matter
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to make it easier for people who are blind, visually impaired or otherwise print-disabled to access books and other printed material which are protected by copyright and related rights.
It requires contracting parties to provide for exceptions or limitations to copyright or related rights in order to make and disseminate accessible formats of protected works and subject matter, and for the cross-border exchange of those copies.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
authorised entities established in an EU country may distribute, communicate or make available to beneficiary persons or to an authorised entity established in a non-EU country that is a party to the Marrakesh Treaty an accessible format copy of a work or other subject matter made in accordance with national legislation adopted pursuant to Directive (EU).
Authorised entities may only make accessible formats available to beneficiaries if they comply with the following conditions:
the content of the accessible format must be in a language that can be understood by the beneficiary person;
the work or subject matter must be accessible in a way that does not impair the person’s ability to read, write or listen to it;
it must be made available in a format that is easily understood by beneficiaries.
The EU countries must apply the same conditions for the export and import of accessible format copies made in the EU as they apply for access to the EU.
An authorised entity may also make available accessible formats to beneficiaries in a third country under certain conditions. These conditions include:
accessibility of the content;
transparency of the contents;
availability of the information contained in the accessible formats;
exceptions to the conditions for making and distributing accessible formats.
Exceptions and limitations
The contracting parties are responsible for:
providing for exceptions and limitations to the rights of copyright, related rights and other intellectual property rights;
ensuring that the content of accessible formats is made accessible to beneficiaries;
protecting the intellectual integrity of the works or other subjects covered by the protected rights of the contracting parties;
encouraging and facilitating the exchange of information;
promoting access to accessible formats for beneficiary persons;
facilitating the exchange and exchange of materials for educational purposes;
creating, distributing, communicating or making available for educational or non-educational purposes, accessible formats or audio books, audio books and radio broadcasts.
Disputes between contracting parties may be settled by arbitration or by a court of law.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 25 May 2017. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 25 May 2018.
BACKGROUND
Accessibility for beneficiaries (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Accessible format: a format in which a person can read or write in a written language.
* Printed material: a material in a printed form which can be read, written or heard by a person who is blind or visually impaired, or who has a disability.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  бартарска гардка (OJ L 183, 25.5.2017, pp. 1-2)
RELATED DOCUMENTS
                    г   ‘Accessible Format Directive’ (Directive 2001/29/EC of the European Parliament and of the Council of 27 April 2001 on the protection of natural persons with regard to the processing of personal data and on the free movement of such data, and repealing Directive 95/46/EC (General Data Protection Regulation) (Official Journal L 119, 4.4.2001, p. 1)
Directive 2014/33/EU of the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea and the International Convention for the Protection of the Rights of the Persons Abroad (UNCTAD) on the right of persons to access the sea, the air, the land and the sea and repealing Directives 2001/30/EC and 2001/31/EC, and Directive 2005/36/EC on the rights and obligations of persons in the territory of the Member States, the European Economic Area and the European Free Trade Association (EEA) and Directive 2014/34/EU on the freedom of movement of persons within the Community (Official Code of Conduct).

Summary 128:
EU mutual assistance mechanism for traders’ infringements of EU consumer rights
EU mutual aid mechanism for trader’s infringements
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/1128 on mutual assistance between EU countries and the European Commission for the enforcement of EU laws that protect consumers’ interests
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up a mechanism for EU countries to assist each other in enforcing EU consumer protection laws.
It aims to ensure that EU countries’ competent authorities cooperate effectively in the application of EU law that protect consumer interests.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must:
ensure that competent authorities have the means and powers necessary to cooperate effectively with the competent authorities of the EU countries;
provide each other with information on suspected infringements;
assist each other to identify and investigate infringements.
The European Commission has the power to issue an alert to EU countries, the European Court of Justice and the Court of Auditors of the European Union (CADEU) of suspected infringement of EU legislation.
Competent authorities and single liaison offices are responsible for:
coordinating actions;
exchanging officials between competent authorities and the Commission;
ensuring the exchange of information;
promoting cooperation on enforcement priorities.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2018. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Mutual Assistance Mechanism’ on the Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Mutual assistance mechanism: a mechanism where EU countries cooperate to identify, investigate and prosecute traders who have infringed EU legislation on consumer rights.

Summary 129:
In vitro diagnostic medical devices — EU rules on their safety and health (Regulation (EU) 2018/852)
In vitro diagnostics — EU laws on their safe and health
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation(EU) No 852/2018 on the European Union’s rules on the safety and the health of in vitro diagnostic devices
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to harmonise EU rules for the sale, import, export and use of medical devices in the EU.
It sets out rules for manufacturers, distributors and end-users to ensure a high level of safety, health and environmental protection.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all medical devices, whether or not they are intended for human use. It does not apply to medical devices intended for animals or plants.
Manufacturers must ensure that their products comply with the relevant safety and environmental requirements and that they have been tested and certified in accordance with the EU legislation on medical devices.
Importers and distributors must:
ensure that the products they place on the market comply with all the safety requirements;
provide information on their products to the European Commission, the European Medicines Agency (EMA) and the European Agency for Safety and Health at Work (EASW).
The European Commission has the power to:
approve or reject a product if it considers that it does not meet the safety or environmental requirements of EU legislation;
assess whether a product complies with the requirements of the regulation;
take corrective and preventive measures to ensure that the product meets the necessary safety requirements.
make a decision on whether the product is safe and/or meets the requirements set out in the regulation.
EU countries must notify the competent national authorities if they consider that a product does not comply with EU legislation.
The Commission may, on its own initiative or at the duly substantiated request of an EU country, decide on a case-by-case basis whether a specific product, category or group of products falls within the scope of this regulation or not.
Designation and notification procedure
The manufacturer must carry out a conformity assessment procedure to determine whether the device meets the safety, environmental and health requirements. It must submit the results of the assessment to a designated body (known as a notified body) which then forwards the results to the Commission.
A notified body must draw up and keep an EU declaration of conformity for the device covered by the procedure. The CE marking must consist of the initials ‘CE’ taking the following into account:
the manufacturer’ s technical documentation assessment certificates and EU type-examination certificates;
the European Commission's scientific certificate of conformity;
a summary of changes and scientific certificates that must be submitted at least every 5 years.
If the manufacturer fails to comply with these requirements, it must inform the notified body of the reasons for the failure to do so and inform the Commission of the corrective and preventive measures that have been taken.
Notified bodies must also:
conduct a quality management system assessment;
conduct an inspection of the device to check that it meets all the requirements for the CE marking;
check that the manufacturer has taken all the necessary preventive and corrective measures;
carry out a risk assessment to identify any deficiencies in the product;
draw up a risk management system to ensure the safety of the product and the quality of the environment;
monitor the product to ensure compliance with the regulation and the requirements laid down in Annexes I and II;
report to the MDCG and the Commission on the implementation of the regulations;
make publicly available a list of their subsidiaries.
Disputes between manufacturers and distributors are settled by a dispute resolution procedure.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
  бабанскага  и   � 
It has applied since 1 January 2019.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
In Vitro Diagnostic Medical Devices (European Commission).

Summary 130:
Ensuring the safety of electrical appliances and fittings in the European Union (EU) — rules on conformity assessment and withdrawal
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2014/53/EU on the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the sale, putting on the market, putting into service or putting in service of electrical and electronic appliances or fittings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It sets out rules on the conformity assessment* and withdrawal of appliances and fitting* from the EU market.
It aims to ensure that the safety and health of consumers and the environment are protected.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to:
electromagnetic compatibility products;
electrostatic compatibility products.
Manufacturers must carry out a conformity assessment to check that their products meet the essential safety requirements of the directive.
Importers must ensure that:
the product complies with the requirements set out in the directive;
the appliance or fitting meets the requirements laid down in the annexes to the regulation;
it meets the minimum safety requirements.
EU countries must notify the European Commission of any non-compliance with the directive by 31 December 2014.
The Commission must inform the EU countries of the non-compliant products and inform them of the measures to be taken in order to withdraw them from the market. The Commission must also inform the European Parliament, the Council and the European Economic and Social Committee (EESC) of any measures taken by EU countries in respect of noncompliance.
National authorities must notify EU countries if they find that an appliance or a fitting does not comply with the legislation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?

Summary 131:
Cross-border cash-in-transit (CIT) — rules for the professional cross-border transport of euro coins by road by road (Regulation (EC) No 1072/2009) (SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2011/33/EU — rules on the crossing of the European Union (EU) border by road for professional cash handlers
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that professional cash handling companies (CTCs) can transport euro coins across the EU by road.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to all CIT companies operating in the euro area.
It does not apply to:
bank branches;
national central banks;
cash centres located in non-EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that:
CIT vehicles are equipped with a global navigation system (GNSS);
the CIT security staff wear a uniform;
the transport arrangements are comparable to the transport arrangements allowed for domestic CIT transport.
Requirements for a CIT licence
A CIT company must obtain a licence from the EU country in which it is established.
The licence must be valid for a minimum period of 5 years.
An EU country may require that a company obtain a professional weapons licence or authorisation if:
it is established in another EU country;
it has a minimum number of security staff per vehicle; or
it does not have at least 2 security staff.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2012. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2013.

Summary 132:
Food labelling — safe and wholesome foods and labelling of processed foods and processed food products
Food laboratoryelling — safety and wholesomeness of foods
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1333/2008 on food labelling
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the principles and procedures for the provision of food information, taking into account the need to provide sufficient flexibility to respond to future developments and new responsibilities governing food information.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
foods intended for human consumption,
food intended for animal consumption and
food prepared for human use.
It does not apply to food intended for sale in the EU.
Labelling
Foods must bear the following information on their composition:
fat content
collagen/meat protein ratio
total protein content
total carbohydrate content.
The information must be expressed in the form of pictograms or symbols instead of words or numbers.
Nutrition declaration
The nutrition declaration must include:
the energy value and the amounts of nutrients referred to in Article 30(1), (3), (4) and (5) as a percentage of the reference intakes set out in Part B of Annex XIII in relation to per 100 g or per 100 ml.
Annexes to the regulation must also contain:
a list of the ingredients in the food;
an indication of the quantity of each of these ingredients in accordance with Article 31 to 34 of the regulation;
a description of the composition of the food and of the quantities of the nutrients;
the quantity of the carbohydrate, protein, fat and total carbohydrate contained in each food.
National measures for non-prepacked food
The European Commission must draw up national measures on additional mandatory particulars. These must be published in the Official Journal of the European Union (JEU).
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Food labelling: the process by which food is labelled to ensure that consumers can make an informed choice.
* Conformity: the way in which a product is labelled in order to allow consumers to make a decision.

Summary 133:
EU countries may issue two types of euro coin: circulation coins and collector coins.
EU countries can issue 2-euro commemorative coins to celebrate specific subjects.
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 974/98 on the introduction of the euro
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the issuing of euro coins in the European Union (EU).
It aims to ensure that euro coins are legal tender in all EU countries whose currency is the euro.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to euro coins:
circulation coins intended for circulation, which are intended to commemorate a specific subject as specified in Article 1h of the regulation;
collector coins* intended for collection that are not issued with a view to their entry into circulation.
Issuance of circulation coins
The euro coin must comply with the regulation’s technical specifications, including the denominations and technical specifications.
Collector coins may be put on the market above face value if justified by the special quality of the coin, a special packaging or any additional services provided.
The issuances of collector coins are accounted for in the volume of coins to be approved by the European Central Bank, but on an aggregate basis rather than for each individual issue.
Importers of euro collector coins must ensure that they comply with all the technical specifications set out in the regulation.
They must not have a shaped edge with fine scallops or a ‘Spanish flower’ shape.
In order to avoid that fit euro circulation coins are destroyed by one EU country while there may be a need of such coins in another, EU countries should consult each other prior to the destruction of these coins. The decision whether to issue commemorative euro coins with a common design collectively issued by EU countries is taken by the Council.
Exemptions
The European Commission may exempt from the regulation certain types of coins from the requirements of Regulation (EU) No 1082/2013 (see summary).
The Commission may also exempt certain pieces of euro commemorative coin from the requirement to comply with certain technical specifications (e.g. the design of the obverse of a coin must not be different from that of a regular coin).
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
For more information, see:
‘The euro’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Council Regulation (EC, No 975/98) of 3 May 1998 on the regulation of the adoption, on behalf of the European Community, of the single currency (OJ L 291, 7.5.1998, pp. 1-3)

Summary 134:
The European Social Fund (ESF)
The European social fund(ESF)*
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2019/852 — the mission and scope of the European Social Funds
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the ESF’s mission, scope and investment priorities.
It aims to promote social inclusion, combating poverty and any discrimination in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The ESF has a mission to:
promote social inclusion and combating poverty in the EU;
support social innovation and transnational cooperation in order to make policies more responsive to social change;
provide financial support to social partners and non-governmental organisations (NGOs) to achieve these objectives;
contribute to the other thematic objectives policies, including through sectoral and territorial pacts to mobilise for reform at the national, regional and local levels.
Funding
The regulation sets out specific rules concerning the type of activities that may be financed by the ESFs.
The main investment priorities are:
support for social innovation;
transnational cooperation;
community-led local development;
employment and social inclusion;
education and training;
health and well-being;
sustainable urban development.
EU countries may decide to allocate a limited amount not exceeding 10 % of the total ESF resources to young persons residing in sub-regions which experience high youth unemployment levels and which are outside the eligible NUTS level 2 regions.
Operational programmes
The operational programmes are designed to achieve the investment priorities listed in the regulation. The operational programmes may be public, private and/or community-based, and delivered by different types of providers, namely public administrations or non-profit organisations.
They must be designed and implemented in line with the objectives of the investment priority ‘promoting social inclusion’. They must:
contain at least 20 % of total ESFs resources in each EU country for the thematic objective ‘fighting poverty and combating any discrimination’;
include at least 10% of total EU resources for the other three investment priorities ‘supporting social innovation’, ‘sustainable and inclusive local development’ and ‘job creation and training’ (i.e. the creation of jobs, training and apprenticeships for people with disabilities and the training of people with special needs).
The management of the operational programmes is the responsibility of the managing authority of the EU country in which the operational programme is to be implemented. The managing authority is responsible for the overall management of operational programmes.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The Regulation has applied since 1 July 2019.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘European Social Fund’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT

SUMMARY OF:

WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the frequency and reference time for EU countries to provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on the EU’s population.
KEY POINTS
The EU countries must provide Eurostat with the data on:
the total population of the EU countries at the end of the reference period (midnight of 31 December);

Summary 136:
Textile products — labelling and labelling
Textiles — labeling and labeling
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 596/2004 on the labelling of textile products
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It lays down the rules for the marketing of textiles in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all textile products manufactured in the EU. It does not apply to:
fleece wool or virgin wool products;
cotton, cupro or modal products; or
polyester or polypropylene products.
It does not cover:
multi-component textile products (e.g. cotton, polyester, elastolefin, polyamide or nylon) or mixtures of fibre mixtures.
The textiles covered by the regulation must be labelled with the following information:
the fibre composition;
the number of fibres; and
the percentage of non-fibrous matter.
Labelling
EU countries must ensure that the textiles listed in the regulation are labelled in a language easily understood by end-users. The textiles must bear the following names:
textile products for which inclusive labelling is sufficient;
textiles for which the information provided by the EU countries is not sufficient.
Textile product for which information supplied by EU countries must be sufficient. The information must include the fibre composition, the number of fibre fibres and the total percentage by weight of the fibre in the product.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
The EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission published a report on the implementation of the regulation in 2014.
For more information, see:
‘Textiles’ on the European Commission’s website.
KEY TERMS
* Textile: a material consisting of fibre, such as cotton or polyester.
* Multi-component: a textile consists of more than one fibre.

Summary 137:
Small-scale coastal fishing — EU fisheries policy
Small-sized coastal fishing – EU fishing policy
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1380/2013 on the common fisheries policy (CFP)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the European Union’s fisheries policy, the Common Fisheries Policy (CFDP), which aims to promote small-scale fishing in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The CFP aims to:
promote small–scale fishing by:
encouraging the development, competitiveness and sustainability of coastal fishing;
promoting the sustainable use of marine resources;
supporting the sustainable management of marine ecosystems;
providing the EU with scientific and technical assistance;
facilitating the exchange of information and best practice.
The regulation lays down the following rules:
the CFP is managed by the European Commission and the European Fisheries Agency (EFSA).
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the CFP.
EU countries have the power to grant or withhold support to small- and medium-sized fishing enterprises (SMEs) or to support fishery local action groups (LAGs).
Fisheries enterprises are eligible for support if they:
operate in an EU country with a significant coastal fishing segment;
have a significant small– or medium–sized fishing segment in a non-EU country;
fisheries have a significant fishery area in a country other than the EU country in which they are located.
FISHIES ARE FUNDED UNDER THE CFP
The EU countries are responsible for:
setting up a CFP;
establishing and managing a management and control system;
ensuring the proper use of the resources under their control;
monitoring the operation of the fisheries and the management of the environmental impact of the fishery;
assessing the impact of measures financed under direct management;
exercising the right of the SMEs to participate in the decision-making process of the Commission and EFSA.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The new regulation has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
The Common fisheries policy is part of the Europe 2020 strategy.
For more information, see:
‘Sustainable fishing’ on the EU's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Small-scale fishery: a fishery is defined as a fishing activity that takes place on the coast of a country or in the territory of another EU country.

Summary 138:
Insurance-based investment products (PRIIPs)
Insurance–based investment securities (PRIs)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2013 on the marketing of investment products for retail investors
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that retail investors have access to investment products designed to provide them with a high level of protection.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all insurance-based investments (PRIPs) sold in the EU.
PRIIP manufacturers must provide retail investors with a key information document to enable them to understand and compare the key features and risks of the PRIIP. This document must contain information such as:
the product’s investment objectives;
the investment targets;
a description of how the investment targets are achieved, including the description of the financial instruments used;
financial jargon and terminology which is not immediately clear to retail investors.
EU countries must ensure that the key information documents are published in a language easily understood by retail investors and that they are accompanied by a copy of the regulation.
Manufacturers must also ensure that their PRIIPs comply with all the relevant EU and national legislation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive (EU) 2017/852 has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
The EU has a single market for investment products.
For more information, see:
Investment products (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Insurance-based product: an investment product that provides investors with protection against the risk of losing their investment.
* Key information document: a document that contains information on the investment objectives and the financial instrument used to achieve those objectives.

Summary 139:
Single Resolution Mechanism (SRM) for failing banks in the EU (SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1024/2013 on the establishment of a single resolution mechanism
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up an EU-wide mechanism to resolve the failure of a failing bank.
It aims to ensure that the resolution of failing banks is as transparent as possible.
KEY POINTS
The regulation establishes the Single Resolution Board (SRB).
The SRB is composed of the European Commission, the European Central Bank (ECB) and the European Bank for Reconstruction and Development (EBRD).
It is responsible for appointing the Chair, Vice-Chair and four members of the Board.
The resolution of a failed bank is carried out by the national resolution authorities of the EU country in which the bank is located.
National resolution authorities must adopt a resolution plan for the failed bank. The resolution plan must:
set out the objectives and principles of the resolution plan;
identify the assets and liabilities to be transferred to a bridge institution;
define the rights and assets transferred from the institution under resolution or provided by other sources.
A national resolution authority may decide to take control of the failing institution or to exercise the write-down or conversion power of relevant capital instruments.
An EU country’s competent national authority must notify the SRM of its decision to take over the failing bank or to authorise it to exercise that power.
Implementation of the regulation is governed by the rules laid down in the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Failing bank: a bank that has failed or is in danger of failing.
* Resolution authority: a national authority that takes over the assets or liabilities of an insolvent or failing bank and then transfers them to another EU country.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 768/2013 of 1 January 2013 establishing a single Resolution Mechanic for failing financial institutions in the European Union (SEM) (OJ L 183, 1.2.2013, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments and corrections to the Regulation have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 140:
Noise-related operating restrictions — air traffic management (noise management)
Noise - noise management
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 691/2010 on the implementation of noise-related operational restrictions
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the safety and environmental performance of civil aviation by:
ensuring that aircraft operating in the EU airspace do not emit excessive amounts of noise;
reducing the impact of noise on human health and the environment.
It repeals and repeals Directive 2002/30/EC.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all aircraft engaged in civil aviation. It does not apply to:
military aircraft;
aircraft operating in non-military airspace;
civil aircraft operating on military or non-commercial routes;
commercial aircraft operating within the territory of the European Union (EU).
It applies to aircraft flying between EU countries and airports in the European Economic Area (EEA) and the European Free Trade Association (EFTA), as well as non-EU countries outside the EEA and non-EFTA countries.
The rules on aircraft noise management are laid down in Annex I to the regulation.
Operational restrictions
The European Commission may impose operating restrictions on aircraft flying within the EEAA or EFTA if it considers that:
there is a risk to human health or the environment;
the noise is likely to adversely affect the health or safety of persons, animals, plants or other natural or man-made structures; or
the environment; or natural resources;
there has been a significant increase in the noise level of an area; or the number of people likely to be affected by aircraft noise; or a significant decrease in the level of noise levels; or an increase in noise levels.
EU countries may impose additional operating restrictions if:
noise levels exceed the limits set out in Annex II of the regulation; or are likely to have exceeded those limits;
are likely to result in adverse health or environmental effects; or will result in an adverse effect on the health and safety of people;
have been found to have a significant impact on the environment or the health of animals; or plants;
is likely to increase the risk of adverse health effects on animals, plant or human beings;
has been found likely to affect the environment, including the environment indicators, such as air quality, noise levels and noise abatement operational measures, and the extent to which those measures are regulated under environment indicators.
Implementing measures
The Commission may adopt delegated acts allowing the EU countries to impose more restrictive operating restrictions. These may include:
restrictions on the use of airports;
restriction of the number and duration of flights;
imposition of restrictions on the number or duration of aircraft operations;
temporary or permanent withdrawal of marginally compliant aircraft.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2002/49/EC has applied since 13 June 2016 and had to become law in EU countries by 13 June 2017.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
‘Noise management’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
   
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 608/2010 of 13 June 2010 on the operation of aircraft in the field of noise operating restrictions (OJ L 136, 13.6.2010, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 608/ 2010 have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 141:
Cross-border insolvency — rules for the application of the law of insolvencies (Regulation (EU) 2019/852)
Cross-Border insolvencations — rules concerning the application, recognition and disposal of claims
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2009/33/EC on the application by EU countries of the laws of the Member States of the European Union on the recognition and dismissal of claims in cross-border proceedings
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that EU countries recognise and dispose of claims arising out of the insolvent activities of a debtor.
It sets out the rules for insolvent proceedings in EU countries.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The directive applies to the following types of insolvent actions:
main or secondary proceedings;
administrative actions;
contracts relating to immoveable property; and
avoidance actions.
EU countries must recognise and dismiss claims arising from the main and secondary proceedings, as well as actions deriving directly from them and closely linked with them.
Right to request the opening of proceedings
The debtor has the right to request that proceedings be opened in the EU country in which the debtor has its centre of its main interests. The debtor may also request to open proceedings in another EU country if the main proceedings have been opened in that EU country.
Procedure for lodging claims
Creditors are required to lodge their claims in order to have their claims taken into account in the proceedings. The creditor may also lodge claims in other EU countries if:
the creditor is not a party to the proceedings; or
the debtor has not been able to pay its claims.
The creditor may lodge claims before the court of an EU country where the proceedings are opened. If the creditor has not paid his or her claims, he or she must lodge them in the country where they are to be opened.
Where the creditor lodges claims, the creditor is entitled to have them heard by the court in the other EU country, as long as no proceedings referred to in Article 3(2) of the directive are opened there.
Jurisdiction
The defendant has jurisdiction over actions arising directly from the proceedings and is closely linked to them, such as avoidance actions. The defendant may also have jurisdiction over other actions that are directly linked with the proceedings or that are closely related to them. In addition, the defendant may have jurisdiction to take other actions which are directly related to the actions arising from these proceedings.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
DirectIVE 2009/34/EC entered into force on 1 January 2010.
Regulation(EU) 2018/853 amends the directive to take account of the EU countries’ national laws on the jurisdiction, recognition, dismissal, disposal, disposal and recovery of claims. It repeals Regulation (EEC) No 852/2009.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The law of bankruptcy’ on the European Commission’s website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
 
Directives 2009/35/EC and 2010/9/EU of the Commission and the European Parliament and of the Council of 16 December 2009 on the implementation by Member States, of the Law of the Union and on the Recognition and Enforcement of Claims in Cross-Border Insolvency (OJ L 183, 28.12.2009, pp. 1-2)
Directions 2009/37/EC, 2010/10/EU and 2011/11/EU have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 142:
Limitation of liability for damage caused to passengers in maritime accidents
Limiting the liability of passengers in marine accidents
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2008 on the limitation of liability in the event of a maritime accident
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the liability rules for passengers who are injured or killed on board ships of the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the carriage of passengers by sea within a single EU country on ships of Classes A and B under Article 4 of Directive 98/18/EC, and lays down certain supplementary requirements.
It applies from the date of the entry into force of the Athens Convention for the Protection of Civil Aviation by the EU (see summary) and from 31 December 2012.
Limits of liability
The limit of liability is 250 000 units of account per passenger on each distinct occasion.
The liability of the carrier and the performing carrier is the sum of the total amount which could be awarded against either the carrier or the performing carriers under this convention, but none of the persons mentioned shall be liable for a sum in excess of the limit applicable to him.
Liability for death and personal injury
The carrier is liable for the death or injury of a passenger on board a ship of Class A or B. The carrier is also liable for any loss suffered as a result of the loss of or damage to luggage other than cabin luggage, unless the carrier proves that the incident which caused the loss occurred without the fault or neglect of it.
Insurance
Insurers must provide passengers with a minimum amount of financial security. The amount of security must not be less than 250,000 units per passenger. If security is furnished in several forms, these should be listed in the regulation’s annexes.
Implementing Regulation (EU) 2017/852 amends the regulation to:
increase the maximum amount of liability of insurance companies;
limit liability of a provider of compulsory financial security under the Convention as modified by Article 7(1) and Article 8 of the convention as revised by the Protocol on the Protection for Civil Aviation;
impose a limit on the amount of insurance that can be recovered from a carrier and a performing carrier.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 December 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 31 December 2011.
BACKGROUND
The Convention on Civil Aviation (Athens Convention) is the main legal instrument for the protection of civil aviation in the EU.
For more information, see:
The Athens Convention (European Maritime Safety Agency).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барска гарка
Commission Regulation (Eec) No 550/2008 of 31 October 2008 laying down the limits of liability (OJ L 347, 31.10.2008, pp. 1-2)

Summary 143:
European rail network for competitive freight — EU rules on infrastructure management and freight rail capacity
European railway network for competing freight — European rules on Infrastructure management
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2014 on the establishment of a European rail network made up of freight corridors
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Union (EU) has a high level of rail infrastructure for freight transport.
It sets out rules on the management of freight rail infrastructure and the capacity for freight trains crossing at least one border along the EU’s internal rail network.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries’ rail infrastructure. It does not apply to existing rail infrastructure in non-EU countries.
EU countries must ensure that:
the infrastructure managers responsible for managing the freight corridor have the necessary capacity to carry out their tasks;
the freight corridor is managed according to the general objectives set out in the regulation;
traffic management is managed by a management board.
The management board is responsible for:
setting out the objectives for the freight corridors;
providing a quality service to freight undertakings within the framework of an international rail corridor;
coordinating traffic management;
ensuring the safety of freight trains;
implementing measures to improve the capacity of the freight rail network;
monitoring the performance of the rail network and the quality of the service provided by the infrastructure managers;
assessing the impact of the investment plan and the measures taken by the management board on the freight network and on the capacity to operate.
Management of freight freight infrastructure
Each EU country must designate or set up a joint body for applicants to request and to receive answers, in a single place and a single operation, regarding the infrastructure capacity to be provided by infrastructure managers.
A management board for a freight corridor must draw up priority rules based on the objectives set by the EU country concerned. These priority rules must be drawn up in a way that ensures that the freight railway network meets the objectives of the regulation. The management board must also draw up a management plan for the future development of the railway network. The investment plan must include a detailed programme of measures to be taken in order to achieve the objectives.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
 * Freight corridor: a network of rail tracks that carries freight from one EU country to another.
* Infrastructure manager: a person responsible for the management and operation of the infrastructure of a freight rail corridor.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Regulation (Eec) No 550/2014 of 1 January 2014 laying down the framework for the establishment, on behalf of the Union, of European rail networks made up by freight corridors (OJ L 183, 1.1.2014, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EC) no 550/ 2014 have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Regulations (Euratom) 2015/849/Euratox of the Commission of 1 February 2015 establishing the European Railways Agency (ERA) and Regulation (EFTA) 2016/869 of the Parliament and the Council and amending Council Directive 2001/14/EC and Council Directives 2001/16/EC, 2001/17/EC of the EEC and 2001/18/EC on the allocation of railway infrastructures and repealing Decision 1999/468/EC ( Official Journal L 338, 29.4.2015, pp 3-5)

Road transport operators — good repute and professional competence (EU rules on road transport operators) — good reputation and professional competences
Road Transport operators — bad repute AND professional competence
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2010 — good-repute requirements for road haulage and road passenger transport operators
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the quality of road transport services by setting out minimum standards for good-reputation requirements for undertakings and their managers.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must ensure that:
road transport operators have the necessary professional competence to operate a road transport operation;
they have the appropriate authorisation to operate road transport operations;

The regulation sets out the conditions for the issuing of licences and certificates of professional competence.

National electronic registers
The EU countries must set up national electronic registers for road transport companies. These allow the client of a transport undertaking to verify whether that undertaking is in possession of the necessary authorisation and to check whether it has the necessary documents to operate.

Summary 145:
Beneficiaries’ rights in the event of an accident or invalidity in the Member State in which they are resident — access to benefits in another Member State (Regulation (EC) No 883/2004) (OJ L 347, 30.12.2004, pp. 1-8)
Beneficies recipients in the European Union (EU) are entitled to receive benefits in kind when they are injured or unable to work.
ACT
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 496/2004 of 30 November 2004 on the access to social security benefits by persons in the territory of a debtor Member State.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THE REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rights of persons who have been injured or cannot work in another EU country.
It aims to ensure that people who have received social welfare assistance in one EU country during a period in which he was entitled to benefits under the legislation of another EU State are able to reclaim the benefits due to them.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all persons covered by the social security system in the EU.
Claimants must submit a claim to the institution of the EU country to which they were last compulsorily subject before the accident.
The claimant must provide the relevant information, such as the date of the accident, the place of residence and the amount of benefits paid.
If the claimant fails to provide the information, the institution designated by the competent authority of that EU country of residence may forward the claim to another institution. If the claimant does not provide all the information required, the first institution may refuse the claim on grounds of invalidity or the aggravation of such invalidity.
In the event that the claimant is unable to provide all of the information necessary to make a claim, the creditor institution may deduct the undue amount from the arrears or on-going payments owed to the person concerned regardless of the national social security branch under which the benefit is paid. The creditor institution is responsible for the payment of the benefits to the claimant under type A legislation.
Where the claimant has not provided all the necessary information, a claim must be submitted to the national institution of which he or she was last compulsorsily subject. The claimant must submit the claim within 18 months of the date on which the accident occurred.
When a claimant is not able to work, the claimant must be able to claim benefits under type B legislation. In the case of type C legislation, where the claimant was compulsorsy to work at the time when the accident took place, the person must be entitled to claim the benefits paid under type D legislation. However, in type E legislation, a claimant may not claim benefits if:
the claimant was not able or could not work at that time;
the person was not entitled to work before that period; or
the claim was made before the period of incapacity or the person was no longer able or capable to work;
there was a period of time during which the persons concerned were, because of their residence in the country of creditor EU State, eligible to receive benefit in kind in that territory at the expense of the debtor EU State. If a claimant was unable or could no longer work, he/she must claim benefits in type B or type C.
Creditors may charge interest on late payments and down payments on outstanding claims. They may also refuse to pay the full costs of benefits cost of treatment incurred by the insured person up to the amount by which the notional cost exceeds the actual cost.
Cooperation between authorities and institutions
EU countries must cooperate with each other in order to assess all relevant information and to consult the reports and any other documents relating to the accident or the invalidity of the claimant.
Each EU country must notify the claimant of the decision it has taken in accordance with the applicable legislation. EU countries must also cooperate with the European Commission to ensure the application of the basic regulation and the implementing regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2014/33/EU of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 November 2014 on the application by EU countries of Regulation (EC, No 882/2004, p. 1)
Regulation 2014/34/EU has applied since 30 November 2014 and had to become law in EU countries by 30 November 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the regulation. The report concludes that the regulation has been very successful in improving the functioning of the system of social security.
For more information, see:
‘Claims for social security assistance’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Commission Implementing Regulation (Euratom) no 496 / 2004 of 29 November 2004 concerning the access by EU citizens in the territories of a creditor Member State to benefit from the benefits of that Member State’s social security legislation
Commission Decision 2014/35/EC of 30 October 2014 on



Summary 146:
Statistics on public health (ECHI)
Statistics on Public health
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 on the collection of comparable data and information on health in the European Union
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the European Commission (Eurostat) collects comparable and comparable data on the health of the population in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the criteria for the calculation of ECHI, the European Community’s (EU) national health statistics.
The criteria are set out in Annexes I to V of the regulation.
These criteria include:
the characteristics, definitions and classifications of the subjects covered by the regulation;
the breakdown of characteristics, the reference periods, intervals and time limits for data provision;
information about the sources and compilations used, population covered and information about any national specificity essential for the interpretation and compilation of comparable statistics and indicators.
Eurostat is responsible for the collection, dissemination, accessibility and documentation of the statistical information, in accordance with the principles of comparability, reliability and statistical confidentiality laid down in the regulation and in Regulation (EC, No 322/97 and with Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 45/2001.
EU countries are required to provide Eurostat with comparable data, with appropriate indicators, on the basis of national data. These indicators should be comparable, when relevant and possible, with the developments and achievements resulting from Community action in the field of public health.
National authorities are responsible for ensuring that the data collected are comparable, reliable and statistical in order to provide the public with comparable and reliable information on the state of health in their respective countries.
In addition, Eurostat must take the necessary steps to improve the quality of the data and to ensure the confidentiality of the information.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
 
 			
For more information, see:
‘Health statistics’ on Eurostat's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Health: any physical or psychosocial health problems that affect the health or well-being of a person.

Summary 147:
Data on job vacancies in the European Union (EU) (Official Journal C 202 of 20.12.2000)
Data on jobs vacancies in Europe (Official J C 202)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2000 on the production of job vacancy statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out minimum requirements for EU countries to collect and transmit data on the number of jobs available in the EU.
It aims to improve the quality of EU statistics and to provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with the necessary information to monitor and analyse the level and structure of labour demand.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all economic activities defined by the common classification system for economic activities in the Community (NACE) in force, except for the activities of households as employers and the activities in extraterritorial organisations and bodies.
EU countries must produce the data using business surveys.
The data must be transmitted to Eurostat, the EU’s statistical office, in the form of a standardised format.
National authorities must ensure that the data are transmitted in a format that is easily understood by the general public.
Data must be collected and transmitted within 3 months of the end of the reference quarter. The date of the first reference quarter must also be determined in accordance with the regulatory procedure with scrutiny referred to in Article 9(2) of the regulation.
Eurostat must transmit the data to the Commission, which then forwards them to the European Central Bank (ECB).
The Commission must review the data every 3 years. The Commission must also review the quality and the reliability of the data.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 20 November 2000. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 20 November 2002.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Employment statistics: data on vacancies broken down, inter alia, by economic activity.
* Business surveys: surveys conducted by businesses to measure the size and scope of their activities and the number and type of employees they employ or intend to employ.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Implementing Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2000 of 20 October 2000 laying down minimum standards for the collection and transmission of job vacancies statistics (OJ C 202, 20.11.2000, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, Eurostat) No 540/2000 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 148:
Compressed (gaseous) hydrogen systems and components for motor vehicles
Composed (gasified) hydrogen components and systems for motor cars
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 552/2009 — type-approval requirements for hydrogen components, other than containers, designed to use compressed (gasoline) hydrogen
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the requirements for the EU’s typeapproval of hydrogen systems* and components* designed to be used in motor vehicles.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to:
hydrogen systems* (including containers) and components designed to carry out the transport of hydrogen;
containers designed to contain hydrogen; and
rogen components, such as fuel tanks.
It does not apply to hydrogen components that are not designed to transport hydrogen.
EU countries must apply the requirements laid down in the regulation to manufacturers of hydrogen components.
Manufacturers must ensure that:
the hydrogen system is installed in such a way that it is protected against damage so far as is reasonably practicable;
damage due to moving vehicle components, impacts or unloading is not located directly on or within the container;
the vehicle interface system and the exhaust system are adequately protected against heat;
overpressure protection systems and heat exchanger failure detection systems are in place.
National authorities must refuse to grant rogen components if they fail to meet the requirements.
The EU countries must also ensure that the hydrogen system and components are tested in accordance with the test procedures set out in Annex V with regard to their type.
Testing procedures
The test procedures for hydrogen containers must be carried out under specified temperature and pressure conditions. The test must include:
a burst test;
an ambient temperature pressure cycle test.
If a container is overfilled, the manufacturer must provide evidence that the system prevents overfilling of the container, and that the level of hydrogen during the filling procedure never causes the opening of the pressure relief devices. If the refuelling line is not secured by a non-return valve or a valve with the same function which is mounted directly on the container. The manufacturer must keep the results of the test throughout the service life of the containers.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 24 February 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 24 February 2012.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
‘Compressed hydrogen’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Hydrogen system: a system that carries out the transportation of hydrogen by means of an internal combustion engine or an external combustion engine.

Summary 150:
Legal cooperation in civil matters between EU countries and non-EU countries
Law enforcement and judicial cooperation
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1049/2001 on the legal cooperation between Member States and third countries relating to areas of civil justice
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes a procedure for the European Union (EU) Member States to notify the European Commission of their intention to conclude an agreement on legal cooperation with non-European Union countries.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all civil matters covered by Article 4(3) of the Treaty on the Functioning of the EU (see summary).
It does not apply to criminal matters.
The EU Member States must notify the Commission of its intentions to open or continue formal negotiations on an agreement as well as to conclude the agreement. The notification must be given by letter or by electronic means.
An EU Member State must provide the Commission with the following information:
the nature and scope of the agreement;
the reasons for its intention to enter into negotiations;
a description of the legal basis for the agreement and the conditions for its conclusion.
If the Commission considers that the agreement meets all of the conditions set out in the regulation, it must authorise the Member State to open formal negotiations. If the agreement does not meet all the conditions, the Commission must give a reasoned decision on the application for the authorisation to open negotiations on that agreement.
A Member State may refuse to authorise negotiations if it considers the agreement to be ineffective or does not undermine the proper functioning of the system established by that law.
In the event of a Member State’s refusal to allow the opening of formal negotiations, the EU country must submit a report to the European Parliament, the Council and the Commission on the basis of the information provided. The Commission must then decide whether the agreement should be concluded or whether it should be re-negotiated.
When the Commission decides to conclude a legal agreement, the agreement must be in line with the principles of the European Court of Justice (ECJ) and the European Convention on the Law of the Union (ECLU).
The Member State concerned must inform the Commission within 90 days of receiving the notification of the intention to open the negotiations. It must also provide any other additional information it may wish to provide to the Commission.
Where the Commission determines that the Agreement does not comply with the requirements of the Regulation, it may decide to terminate the agreement without prejudice to the rights of the parties or to the interests of the Community as a whole.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 30 May 2001.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Legal cooperation: the cooperation of the judicial authorities of an EU country with a third country.
* Criminal matters: the law of the country in which a criminal case is brought.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  �  			
Commission Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1109/2001 of 29 May 2001 on the law enforcement and civil cooperation in matters of civil matters relating to Areas of Civil Justice (OJ L 127, 30.5.2001, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 1109 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 151:
European Union statistics on migration and asylum (2008-2013)
European Union (EU) statistics on immigration and asylum
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 311/76 — common rules for the collection and compilation of Community statistics on Migration and Asylum
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out common EU-wide rules for EU countries to collect and compile statistics on:
immigration to and emigration from the EU territories, including flows from the territory of one EU State to that of another EU State;
the number of residence permits issued to persons who are third-country nationals, disaggregated as follows:
permits issued during the reference period whereby the person is being granted permission to reside for a period of no more than 6 months;
permits issued for the prevention of illegal entry and stay.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must provide the European Commission (Eurostat) with data on the following:
the total number of immigrants and asylum seekers in the EU in the reference year 2008;
total number of non-EU nationals who have entered the EU;
number of asylum seekers who have left the EU.
The EU countries must also provide Eurostat with information on the number of requests for information made by EU countries for information on migration.
Data sources and quality standards
Eurostat must ensure that the data it receives from EU countries are up-to-date and of a high quality. It must also ensure the quality of the statistics it receives is comparable across EU countries. EU countries have the right to use different quality standards depending on the data they receive.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGULATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2008.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission publishes a report on the EU’s migration statistics every year.
For more information, see:
EU migration statistics (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Migration: flows from one EU country to another EU country.
* Emigration: flows between EU countries in which a person lives or works.

Summary 152:
The public-interest function of statutory auditors and audit firms (PSA) (European Commission)
The public interest function of PSA’s (European Parliament and Council)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 — the public interest functions of statutory auditor and audit firm
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that statutory audit* functions are carried out in a way that is fair and transparent.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to statutory audit of:
public-interest entities;
systemically important financial institutions; and
other entities.
It does not apply to the audit of the entities listed in Directive 2006/43/EC (see summary) or Directive 2011/61/EU.
Tenders for statutory audit services
The tender documents must contain the following information:
the name and address of the statutory auditor or the audit firm;
the nature and scope of the work to be performed;
a description of the services to be provided and the fees to be charged.
EU countries must ensure that the tender documents are published in the Official Journal of the European Union (OLJ).
The competent authorities of the EU country in which the tender is to be conducted must be notified of the tender and of the terms and conditions for the tender.
The European Securities and Markets Authority (ESMA) is responsible for monitoring market quality and competition in the market for statutory audits and audits of systemically important banks. The ESMA must also monitor the independence and impartiality of the competent authorities in the EU countries where the tender for the statutory audit is to take place.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2018.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Public-interest functions: the role of a statutory auditor is to provide independent, impartial and objective analysis of the financial statements of a public or private entity.
* Audit firms: audit firms that provide independent and impartial analysis of financial statements for a public, private or other entity. They may also provide independent audit services for other entities, such as pension funds.

Summary 153:
Conservation of marine biological resources and the management of fisheries targeting them — Common Fisheries Policy (CFP) (European Commission).
Conservation and management of marine biology and the fishing of fish targeting them – Common Fisheries Programme (CDP)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 on the common fisheries policy of the European Union (EU)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the common framework for the conservation of the marine resources of the EU.
KEY POINTS
The CFP is the EU’s fisheries policy. It aims to protect the marine ecosystems of the Mediterranean, the Black Sea, the Atlantic Ocean and the Indian Ocean.
It aims to ensure that fish stocks and fishery activities are managed in a way that is compatible with the United Nations Convention on the Law of the Sea (UNCLOS).
The scope of the CFP includes:
fishing opportunities;
management of fisheries;
fisheries and aquaculture research and innovation programmes.
EU countries must:
ensure that fishing opportunities are allocated according to the needs of the fishery;
encourage small-scale, artisanal or coastal fishermen to participate in fishing activities;
promote the sustainable use of fishing resources;
support the sustainable development of the fishing industry.
The allocation of fishing opportunities to EU countries must ensure relative stability of fishing activities of each EU country for each fish stock or fishery.
Each EU country must ensure that the fishing opportunities allocated to it are proportionate to the fishing needs of its fishing population.
Fisheries must be managed in accordance with the rules adopted in the area of data collection, collect biological, environmental, technical, and socio-economic data necessary for fisheries management, manage those data and make them available to end–users, including bodies designated by the Commission.
In close cooperation with the Commission, EU countries shall coordinate their data collection activities with other EU countries in the same region, and shall make every effort to coordinate their actions with third countries having sovereignty or jurisdiction over waters in that region.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Fishing opportunities: opportunities to catch fish in the waters of an EU country.
* Fishing activities: fishing activities that take place on the fishing grounds of a fishing vessel.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Commission Regulation (EC, No 1223/2009) of 1 January 2009 on the Common Fisheries policy of The European Union and repealing Council Regulation (EEC, Euratom) No 104/2000 (OJ L 183, 1.2.2009, pp. 1-8)

Summary 155:
Securities settlement systems (CSDs) — rules for their establishment and operation
Securities settlements systems (SSDs) – rules for its establishment and operations
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 648/2012 on the establishment, operation and supervision of securities settlement systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for the establishment and regulation of the securities settlement system (CSD) in the European Union (EU).
It aims to improve the safety, efficiency and transparency of the EU’s securities markets and to ensure that the activities of CSDs are transparent and transparent.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all CSDs established in the EU. It does not apply to:
non-EU countries that do not have a CSD established in their territory;
EU countries which have not yet established a competent CSD.
It applies to the EU countries that have an existing CSD or that intend to establish one.
Requirements for participation
To be eligible for the regulation, CSDs must:
provide services ancillary to their core services, such as:
banking-type banking-type services directly related to core services listed in Sections A and B;
providing cash accounts to, and accepting deposits from, participants in a securities settlement* system and holders of securities accounts;
Providing cash credit for reimbursement no later than the following business day, cash lending to pre-existing securities accounts and cash lending for pre-established securities accounts.
Provide ancredits to participants in the following the completion of a securities transactions;
inform and seek approval from its competent authority of any decision to transfer ownership rights which give rise to a change in the identity of the persons exercising control over the operation of the CSD;
establish an interoperable link between its core and non-core services, i.e.
An EU country must designate a single competent authority to be responsible for cooperation with other EU countries’ competent CSDs.
The competent authorities of EU countries must ensure that:
a CSD has the necessary legal capacity to operate in the host EU country in which it is established;
the competent authority has the authority necessary to authorise it to do so;
a competent authority is responsible for supervising the operations of the host CSD in its territory and must inform the competent authority in that EU country of any changes in its ownership rights.
A CSD must be authorised to provide services that contribute to enhancing the safety and efficiency of the financial markets and do not create undue risks to its core services. These services must be provided by a centralised centralised CSD that is authorised by an EU country and that is subject to the supervision of the competent authorities in that country. The competent authorities must ensure compliance with the regulation and the relevant national laws and regulations of that country with respect to the operation and operation of its CSD and that the relevant authorities have the authority to issue authorisations for the operation or operation of their CSD under the regulation.
Regulatory technical standards
To ensure that CSDs comply with the regulatory technical standards, the regulation sets out a set of technical standards to be adopted by the European Commission. These technical standards must be aligned with the relevant regulatory and implementing technical standards adopted in accordance with Article 47(8) of the TFEU.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGUMENT APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2014.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Securities settlement system: a system used to settle securities transactions.
* Non-EU country: any EU country that has a competent competent authority responsible for the management of a competent securities settlement service.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
  The regulation establishes the framework for the creation, operation, supervision and supervision (CSDS) of securities settlements systems in the Member States and amends Directive 2013/36/EU (see summary) and Regulation (EC) No 1095/2010 (see amending act(s) No 552/2010).
  It repeals Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 852/2008 (see consolidated version).
Successive amendments to the regulation have been incorporated into the basic text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
