Summary 156:
Cohesion Fund — investment priorities and common output indicators (2014-2023) (European Commission) (Europa Commission).
Cohesion fund — investment priority and common outputs (Europas Commission). This European Commission regulation establishes a European Union (EU) Cohesion Fund to support projects in the fields of environment and trans-European networks in the area of transport infrastructure.
ACT
Commission Regulation (EC) No 1084/2006 of 11 July 2006 establishing a Cohesion fund.
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
The European Union’s (EU's) cohesion fund aims to support activities in the areas of environment, energy, transport infrastructure and social cohesion.
KEY POINTS
The EU’S cohesion fund, established in 2013, aims to provide a financial contribution to projects in these fields in order to contribute to the investment priorities within the thematic objectives set out in the framework of the European Pillar of Social Rights (EPSR).
The objectives of the EPSR are:
investment in the environment, including areas related to sustainable development and energy which present environmental benefits;
transport infrastructure, in compliance with the guidelines adopted by the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2013 on EU guidelines for the development of the trans‐European transport network and repealing Decision No 661/2010/EU (OJ L 348, 20.12.2013, pp. 1-2);
social cohesion, including social cohesion, in accordance with the principles of social cohesion and cohesion, which present social benefits.
The EPPR supports projects of common interest, such as:
environmental, energy and transport infrastructure;
sustainable development, including energy and social development;
environmentally sound urban planning;
economic development, particularly in urban areas;
health and well-being;
science and technology (i.e. research, development and technological development);
education and training;
cultural and leisure activities; and
technical assistance.
In order to achieve the objectives, the EPPF supports projects that:
contribute to the sustainable development of Europe;
promote the social cohesion of the EU;
support social cohesion;
provide technical assistance to social cohesion projects;
improve the quality of life of people in the EU.
EU countries are responsible for the implementation of their own operational programmes. The European Commission is responsible for implementing the operational programme.
FROM WHEN DO THE REGULATIONS APPLY?
Directive 2014/2023/EU has applied since 1 January 2014 and had to become law in EU countries by 1 January 2015.
BACKGROUND

The European Regional Development Fund (ERDF) (European Commission)
The European regional development fund (European Parliament and Council)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 1303/2013 on the European Regional development Fund
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets up an EU-wide financial instrument to support regional development in the European Union (EU).
It aims to reduce disparities between the levels of development of the various regions and to reduce the backwardness of the least favoured regions, among which particular attention is paid to regions which suffer from severe and permanent natural or demographic handicaps such as the northernmost regions with very low population density and island, cross-border and mountain regions.
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the objectives of the ERDF.
The objectives are to:
promote sustainable development by:
improving the quality of life of people in the most disadvantaged regions;
increasing the productivity of the labour market;
reducing poverty and social exclusion in the least disadvantaged regions, particularly in the north and the north-east of the EU;
promoting the competitiveness of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) and SMEs in the south-east and north-eastern EU.
To achieve these objectives, the EU-funded ERDF supports the following projects:
investments in airport infrastructure to promote environmentally sustainable air transport when, inter alia, enhancing regional mobility by connecting secondary and tertiary nodes to trans-European transport network (TEN-T) infrastructure, including through multimodal nodes.
Investments in research and innovation in the areas of health, education, social protection and the environment. These projects are supported by Horizon 2020 - the Framework Programme for Research and Innovation (FPRI).
The ERDF is funded by the European Investment Bank (EIB) and the European Social Fund (ESF). The EIB is responsible for managing the EIB’s financial resources.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 31 December 2013 and had to become law in the EU countries by 31 December 2015.
BACKGROUND
 
  * KEY TERMS
* Regional development fund: a European Union financial instrument that supports regional development by providing financial support to the most deprived regions of the country.
* Severe and permanent handicaps: a natural or permanent handicap such as poverty, unemployment or social exclusion.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 			
Council Regulation (EU), Euratom, Euratom and Euratom of 30 November 2013 on the establishment of the European regional Development Fund and repealing Council Regulation (EC) No 1084/2006 (OJ L 165, 31.12.2013, pp. 1-3)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom, Eib) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 159:
EU smart grids — common rules for electricity and gas networks
EU smart networks — common guidelines for electricity, gas and water networks
SUMMARY OF:
Delegated Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — common procedures for the approval of projects of common interest
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
 
It sets out the common procedures to be followed by EU countries when granting permits for energy and gas projects.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all energy projects in the EU, including:
electricity;
gas;
water.
EU countries must ensure that:
energy projects are subject to a cost-benefit analysis to determine whether they meet the essential requirements of the EU’s energy policy objectives;
the project is located on the territory of an EU country, i.e. not in another EU country;
it is located in an area that is under the jurisdiction of a non-EU country.
The EU countries must:
ensure that energy projects are located on their territory;
provide the European Commission with a list of projects that are eligible for a permit;
allow the public to participate in the decision-making process.
Regulatory authorities must apply the common procedure for the granting of permits to energy projects and water projects in accordance with EU law.
National authorities must publish a manual for the permit granting process in order to ensure transparency and public participation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
It has applied since 16 May 2015. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 16 May 2016.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has adopted a number of energy policy directives in recent years.
For more information, see:
Smart grids (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Energy projects: projects that provide electricity, water or other forms of energy to the end user.
* Water projects: water, wastewater or other natural or man-made substances that can be pumped into the ground or into the air.
Electricity projects: energy projects that supply the end consumer with electricity or other form of energy, such as photovoltaic (PV) panels, photogeneration (PIGs) and micro-photovoltaics (MPGs).
* Gas projects: gas projects that produce or emit greenhouse gases or other pollutants.

Summary 160:
Online dispute resolution (ODR) platform for traders and consumers (ODR platform)
The online dispute resolution platform (ODD platform) is designed to allow traders to submit complaints against consumers.
ACT
Council Regulation (EU) No 524/2013 of 21 May 2013 on online dispute resolutions (ODRs)
SUMMARY
WHAT DOES THIS REGULATION DO?
It sets out the rules for the use of the Online Dispute Resolution (ODRS) platform by traders to resolve disputes with consumers. It aims to ensure that traders can submit complaints to the ODR platform without having to go to court.
KEY POINTS
Directive 2013/11/EU lays down the principles and procedures for the operation of ODR platforms.
The regulation applies to all online sales or service contracts, and online marketplaces established within the EU, and to all disputes that are initiated by a trader against a consumer.
It does not apply to disputes where the trader is a natural person who is acting outside their trade, business, craft or profession.
ODR platforms must be set up in the EU country where the consumer is habitually resident.
EU countries must designate an ODR contact point for each EU country. The contact point must be located in the centre of the European Consumer Centres Network (ECCN).
The contact point is responsible for the development, operation and maintenance of the platform and for ensuring that the privacy of its users is respected from the design stage (privacy by design) and that the platform is accessible and usable by all, including vulnerable users (‘design for all’), as far as possible.
Each EU country must designate a competent authority to handle disputes where a trader has submitted a complaint to ODR. The competent authority must:
ensure that the complaint has been submitted by a consumer and that it has been resolved by the trader;
provide the competent authority with all the necessary information to resolve the dispute;
assist the consumer in the negotiation of a dispute and in the settlement of the dispute.
Disputes must be resolved by a dispute resolution entity (i.e. an ADR entity) that is listed in the directive’s annexes.
FROM WHEN DOES THE DIRECTIVE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 8 July 2013. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 8 July 2014.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Online dispute resolution: a procedure that allows consumers to submit a complaint against a trader.
* Dispute resolution entity: an entity that resolves a dispute between consumers and traders by means of an arbitration procedure.
REFERENCES

Entry into force - Date of expiry
Deadline for transposition in the Member States
Official Journal
Regulation (Euratom) No 496/2013

Summary 161:
Direct payments to natural and legal persons in the EU — basic payment scheme for young farmers (2013-2020)
Direct payments for natural and legally situated farmers in the European Union (EU)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 73/2009 on the basic payment system under the Common Agricultural Policy (CAP)
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out minimum requirements for natural or legal persons to be eligible for direct payments under the common agricultural policy (CAP).
KEY POINTS
EU countries must grant direct payments to the following persons:
natural persons, who operate airports, railway services, waterworks, real estate services, permanent sport and recreational grounds.
Legal persons, whose agricultural areas are mainly areas naturally kept in a state suitable for grazing or cultivation and who do not carry out a significant amount of agricultural activity.
Natural persons, such as farmers, who are not natural persons, but who are legal persons and who have a legitimate interest in the production of agricultural products.
They must be natural persons or natural persons who are legally situated in the territory of an EU country.
The general rules for granting direct payments are set out in the annex to the regulation.
National authorities must ensure that:
the beneficiaries of direct payments have the necessary means to meet the minimum requirements;
the recipients of the direct payments do not have a significant economic impact on the EU country in which they live or work;
they have sufficient resources to cover the costs of their own subsistence farming activities;
their income is sufficient to cover their basic living expenses; and
they do not engage in activities that would have a disproportionate impact on other EU countries’ economic interests.
A minimum amount of €1.5 million per hectare of eligible land is set out for each EU country based on the total number of young farmers applying for that payment in 2015.
Annex II sets out the annual national ceiling for the basic payments. This ceiling is calculated by dividing the number of hectares eligible in 2015 by the number young farmers who applied for the payment that year.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation has applied since 1 January 2014. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2016.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘Direct payments’ on the European Commission’s website.

Summary 162:
EU support programme for the smaller Aegean islands
UAE support programme
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 552/2011 — EU support programme to the smaller islands in the Aegea
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
 
It sets out the conditions for the European Union (EU) to provide financial assistance to the small islands of the eastern Mediterranean (Aegean Sea) under the EU’s common agricultural policy (CAP).
KEY POINTS
The programme is designed to support the sustainable development of the islands by:
providing them with agricultural products;
encouraging the development of small and medium-sized enterprises (SMEs) in the area;
promoting trade in agricultural products between the islands and the mainland.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the programme.
EU countries must ensure that the measures are compatible with EU law and that they are consistent with the common customs tariff.
Measures to support local agricultural production
The measures are designed to:
support the production and processing of local agricultural products,
promote the diversification of the production of local products, and
improve the quality of the local products.
These measures are based on:
a forecast supply balance;
a market surveillance system;
the development of local markets;
agriculture-related research and development (R&D) activities;
exchange of information on the production, processing and distribution of products; and
exports of products to the EU and non-EU countries.
Implementing powers
The Commission has the power to adopt delegated acts on the basis of a proposal from the European Parliament or the Council.
Delegated acts are adopted by the Commission on behalf of the EU.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The regulation entered into force on 21 March 2011. EU countries had to incorporate it into their national law by 21 March 2013.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
‘The smaller islands’ on the European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Agricultural policy: a policy that aims to improve the quality and quantity of agricultural products and to promote trade in those products with the EU countries. It aims to provide the islands with a high level of agricultural production and to develop the local economy.

Summary 164:
EU countries’ judicial cooperation in civil matters — documents
EU countries' judicial cooperation on documents
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 596/2004 — EU countries' cooperation in judicial matters
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to establish a European Union (EU) area of judicial cooperation between EU countries in the field of civil matters.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all EU countries, except Denmark, which does not take part in the regulation and is not bound by it or subject to its application.
The EU countries are obliged to cooperate with each other in the following areas:
transmission of documents and all papers that are transmitted to, from, or through an EU country’s judicial authorities;
the exchange of information between the EU countries and the judicial authorities in the EU country in which they have jurisdiction;
procedures for the exchange of documents;
exchanges of information on the legal status of the documents and of the papers transmitted.
It does not apply to:
obligations for the receipt of a document;
refusal to accept a document if it is not in one of the languages provided for in Article 8 of the regulation; or
the refusal of a person to receive a document in another EU country.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?

Summary 165:
Medicines for human use: advanced therapy medicinal products (TRM) (European Medicines Agency) (Euratom) (EU) Regulation (EC) No 552/2008
Medicines to human use — advanced therapy therapeutic products (ETM)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 550/2008 on the regulation of medicinal products for human uses
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that EU countries have a uniform framework for the manufacture and marketing of advanced therapy medicines.
It lays down the essential requirements for the authorisation of these medicinal products.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to any of the following medicinal products:
a gene therapy medicinal product as defined in Part IV of Annex I to Directive 2001/83/EC,
a somatic cell therapy therapeutic product for the treatment of diseases and dysfunctions of the human body;
a tissue engineered medicinal product for use in the treatment or prevention of diseases;
an investigational medicinal product (i.e. a product that has been tested and tested to determine whether it has the potential to treat a disease or to prevent a disease).
Manufacturers must ensure that:
the products are manufactured in accordance with the principles of good manufacturing practice, as set out in Directive 2003/94/EC (see summary);
they comply with the requirements of the European Commission’s Directorate-General for Medicines and Health (DGH).
EU countries must designate a competent authority to authorise and monitor the manufacture of these products and to monitor their safety and efficacy.
The competent authority is responsible for:
checking that the products are safe and that they meet the essential safety requirements;
monitoring the quality and efficacy of the products;
checking the safety and effectiveness of the medical devices that form part of a combined advanced therapy medical product;
assessing the efficacy and safety of the combined medical devices.
Authorisation
An EU country may authorise a medicinal product if:
it has been shown that the medicinal product is safe and effective;
the medical device meets all essential safety, efficacy and efficacy requirements; and
it meets the essential quality and safety requirements laid down in Directive 93/42/EEC.
Importers must ensure:
that the medicinal products they place on the market have been authorised by the competent authority;
that they have been tested, tested and certified;
they have carried out the necessary clinical trials to demonstrate that the medical device is safe, effective and has the necessary safety and quality requirements.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 30 December 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 30 December 2012.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Genome therapy: the use of genetically modified human cells or tissues to treat diseases or disorders.
* Stem cells: the stem cells and tissues of human embryos or animal cells that have been genetically modified to treat disease or disorders of the body.

Summary 166:
EU type approval of motor vehicles and their trailers — rules for in-service conformity, durability of pollution control devices, on-board diagnostic (OBD) systems, measurement of fuel consumption and accessibility of vehicle repair and maintenance information.
EU type approvals of motor vehicles
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2015/852 — type approval requirements for motor vehicles
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
   
It sets out rules for the type approval* of motor cars and their their trailers in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all vehicles registered in the EU, whether or not they are powered by a petrol engine or a diesel engine.
It does not apply to certain types of trucks, such as buses and light commercial vehicles.
Type-approval requirements
EU countries must ensure that:
motor vehicles comply with the emission limits set out in the regulation;
the vehicle has a type approval that is valid for at least 5 years;
it complies with the requirements of the EU type approval system.
Manufacturers’ type approval obligations
The manufacturer of a motor vehicle is responsible for ensuring that its type approval is granted by the competent authority of an EU country.
If a vehicle does not meet the requirements for type approval, EU countries must prohibit its sale or installation on a vehicle if:
it is not of a type in respect of which type approval has been granted in compliance with this regulation and its implementing measures.
National authorities may also prohibit the sale, installation or use of a replacement pollution control device if it does not comply with type approval.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
It has applied since 1 January 2016. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2017.
BACKGROUND

Summary 167:
Scientific assessment of substances on the EU market
Scientific assessments of substances upon the European Union (EU) market
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 178/2002 — the European Food Safety Authority
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the procedures for the European Commission to issue authorisations for the placing on the market of substances that pose a risk to human health.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to substances that have been authorised to be placed on EU markets.
It applies to all foodstuffs and food categories, including foodstuff intended for human consumption.
Authorisations are granted by the Commission on the basis of an independent scientific assessment, of the highest possible standard, of risks that they pose.
Risk assessment
The Commission is assisted by the Standing Committee on the Authority’s operation, which is composed of representatives of EU countries’ organisations operating in the fields covered by the regulation.
EU countries must provide the Commission with all relevant scientific data and other information concerning the substances on which they wish to place an authorisation. This information must be published in the Official Journal of the EU.
Applicants for the authorisation to place a substance on the European market must submit a risk assessment report to the Commission. The Commission then decides whether to grant or reject the application based on the risk assessment.
The EU countries must inform the applicant and the Commission of the reasons for their decision.
Implementation of the regulation
The European Commission publishes a list of substances which have been placed on the Community list. The list is updated every 3 years.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGULE APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 1 January 2004.
BACKGROUND
 			
The Regulation (EU, Euratom) has been amended several times, most recently in 2014.
For more information, see:
EU food safety (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Risk assessment: a scientific assessment of a substance to determine whether it is safe to use it in or on specific foods.
* Authorisation: an EU country to place on its market a substance that poses a risk of serious adverse effects on human health or to the environment.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  б  х 
Commission Regulation (EC), Euratom, of 14 January 2004 on the application for authorisation for the place on the Market of substances (OJ L 183, 14.1.2004, pp. 1-8)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom, Euratox) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 168:
EU rules on accreditation, market surveillance, controls of products from non-EU countries and the CE marking (until 31 December 2020)
EU rules about accreditation*, market surveillance and controls of product from non–EU countries
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) 2017/852 laying down rules for accreditation and market surveillance for products from outside the EU
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out EU rules on the accreditation of products and the market surveillance of products which are intended to be sold in the EU.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to all products intended for sale in the European Union (EU).
It does not apply to:
medical devices, pharmaceuticals, motor vehicles and aviation;
medical products for human and veterinary use;
motor vehicles for human or veterinary use.
EU countries must ensure that national accreditation bodies carry out the necessary activities to ensure that products comply with the requirements set out in the relevant EU harmonisation legislation and do not endanger health, safety or any other aspect of public interest protection.
National accreditation authorities must:
ensure that products that have been accredited by an accreditation body do not present a risk to public health or safety;
provide the European Commission with information on products presenting a risk;
exchange information with national market surveillance authorities and exchange information with them relating to products which they suspect are a risk.
The Commission may, after consulting the Committee set up by Article 5 of Directive 98/34/EC, request the body recognised under Article 14 to contribute to the development, maintenance and implementation of accreditation in the Community.
A European Commission representative may also be appointed to assist the European Agency for Medicines and Veterinary Medicines (EMAV) in the implementation of the regulation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2009/33/EC has applied since 1 December 2017.
Directives 2001/95/EC and 2001/98/EC have applied since 31 December 2000.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission is responsible for implementing the EU’s harmonisation rules.
For more information, see:
EU accreditation (European Commission).
KEY TERMS
* Accreditation: the process by which a product is authorised to be placed on the EU market.
* Market surveillance: the activities carried out and measures taken by public authorities to ensure the products are safe and that they do not pose a risk for public health and safety.
MAIN DOCUMENT
Commission Regulation (EU, Euratom) 2017 / 852 of 27 November 2017 laying down guidelines for the application of Regulation (EC) No 596/2004 as regards accreditation for products intended to come into the EU and for market surveillance
RELATED DOCUMENTS
Commission Directive 2001/96/EC of 31 December 2001 laying down a framework for the harmonisation of the laws of the Member States relating to the establishment of the European Community (OJ L 347, 31.12.2001, pp. 1-8)
DirectIVE 2001/94/EC on the establishment and functioning of the Community market surveillance framework and repealing Directives 95/46/EC (Euratom) and 96/53/EEC (Directive 96/54/EC)

Summary 169:
EU-Iceland agreement on extradition
EU-Ireland agreement on extraditable cooperation
SUMMARY OF:
Agreement on extradition between the EU and its Member States, the Kingdom of Norway and the Republic of Iceland, on behalf of the European Union
Decision (EU) 2017/852 concluding the Agreement on extradition of persons between the European Economic Area (EEA) and Iceland
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE AGREEMENT AND OF THE DECISION?
The agreement aims to ensure that EU citizens who are the subject of an EU arrest warrant or an arrest warrant for a non-EU country are extradited to the EU.
The decision concludes the agreement between the EEA and the EU countries, Norway and Iceland.
KEY POINTS
EU countries must inform each other of their competent authorities when an EU country issues a European arrest warrant (EAW) for an EU citizen. The EAW may be issued for the purposes of prosecution or for execution of a custodial sentence or detention order.
An EU country may issue an EAW for an individual who is a national of another EU country. The EU country must inform the EU country which issued the EAW.
If two or more EU countries have issued EAWs for the same person, the decision as to which of the arrest warrants shall be executed shall be taken by the executing judicial authority with due consideration of all the circumstances and especially the relative seriousness and place of the offences, the respective dates of the warrant and whether the warrant has been executed for the purpose of prosecution.
A judicial authority may refuse to execute an execution warrant if it considers that:
the person is a flight risk or a danger to the safety of the EU; or
the arrest warrant has not been executed in accordance with the law of the country in which the person is resident.
In the event of a refusal to execute the arrest warrant, the person must be informed of the reasons for the refusal and of the legal remedies available to him or her. If the person has been convicted of a criminal offence, he or she must be given the opportunity to appeal against the decision.
Where a judicial authority considers that the person cannot be brought before a court, it may postpone or conditional surrender the person. The person may also be refused to surrender if:
he or she has committed a serious criminal offence or is suspected of having committed a significant number of criminal offences;
the country in question has not applied the extradition system applicable before the entry into force of the agreement.
FROM WHEN DO THE DECISIONS APPLY?
Both decisions entered into force on 1 July 2017.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
Arrest warrants and extradition (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENTS
 
Council Decision (EU), Euratom, Euratom and Euratom — conclusion of the Agreement for extradition of individuals between the Economic Area and Iceland (OJ L 183, 1.7.2017, pp. 1-2)
Council Decision (Euratom) 2017 / 852 of 1 June 2017 on the conclusion, by the EU, of an Agreement for extraditable Cooperation between the Eastern Economic Area of the Union and the European Atomic Energy Community (EURatom) and the Council of the Member States of the one part, and Iceland, of the other part, on the basis of Article 38(1) of the Treaty on European Union (TFEU) and Article 39(2) of Article 40(3) of Regulation (EC) No 552/2008 (O J L 183/2008, p. 1)
Agreements on extradition (EEC, EFTA) and on the mutual recognition of extradition requests (TFU) (Oj L 183 / 1.6.2017)

Summary 170:
Compensation for railway passengers — carriage charge and baggage registration (Article 5(2) of Regulation (EC) No 552/2008)
Compensation to railway passengers – carriage charge AND baggage registration
SUMMARY OF:
Directive 2008/36/EC — compensation for passengers on the European Union (EU) railway system
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DIRECTIVE?
It aims to ensure that passengers travelling on the EU’s railway system are compensated for any loss or damage caused by the carriage of their goods.
KEY POINTS
The directive applies to all passengers travelling by rail in the EU.
It does not apply to:
travel information and reservation undertakings;
ticket offices or selling machines;
on board trains.
The regulation applies to the carriage charge for services provided under public service contracts. The carriage charge must cover the entire route up to delivery.
EU countries must ensure that:
the carriage charge does not exceed the amount of the baggage registration voucher issued to the passenger;
the passenger is entitled to a refund of the cost of the carriage.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS DIRECTIVE APPLY?
Decision 2008/38/EC has applied since 3 December 2008.
Decisions 2009/40/EC and 2009/42/EC have applied since 4 December 2009.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:

* KEY TERMS
* Carriage charge: a charge charged by the railway company to transport goods or passengers on its territory.

Summary 171:
European Union statistics in the field of education and lifelong learning (EU-SILC)
European Union (EU)-SILCs
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1177/2003 establishing a common framework for the systematic production of European Union statistics on education and training systems
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It establishes an EU-wide system for the production of EU statistics on the education and learning systems of EU countries.
KEY POINTS
EU countries are required to collect data on the quality, scope and reliability of their national education systems.
The data are collected by Eurostat, the European Union’s statistical office.
Data must be collected every 5 years.
Each EU country must send Eurostat a request for information on its national education system.
Eurostat forwards the data to the European Commission (Eurostat).
The Commission then forwards the information to Eurostat in accordance with Regulation (EU) 2015/849 on the European system of national statistics on income and living conditions and the European System of Income and Living Conditions (ESILC).
Data are collected on a voluntary basis by EU countries and Eurostat may institute pilot studies to assess the quality of the data collected.
When a country does not send the data within 5 years, Eurostat must inform the European Parliament and the Council of the reasons for the failure to do so.
If a country fails to provide the necessary data, the Commission must inform Eurostat of the reason and inform the EU countries of the measures taken to remedy the situation.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
The regulation entered into force on 1 January 2005.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
EU statistics (European Commission).
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барска  гарга
Commission Implementing Regulation (EC, Euratom) No 1079/2003 of 7 November 2003 establishing a Common Framework for the Systematic Production of European Statistics in the Field of Education and Training Systems (Euratom) (OJ L 291, 12.12.2003, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (EEC, Euroatom) have been incorporated in the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 172:
Energy statistics — EU countries’ data
Energy statistics – EU countries' data
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2008 on energy statistics
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to improve the quality of energy statistics in the European Union (EU).
KEY POINTS
The regulation sets out the rules for the production, trade, stocks, transformation, consumption, and structural characteristics of the EU’s energy system.
EU countries must report to the European Commission (Eurostat) each year on:
energy production and trade;
energy stocks;
transformation and consumption;
structural characteristics of energy system;
installed capacities for electricity generation or production capacities for oil products;
total energy consumption.
It also sets out a set of annual nuclear statistics which are to be reported and disseminated from 2009 onwards, that year being the first reported period, without prejudice to confidentiality, where it is necessary, and avoiding any duplication of data collection, while at the same time keeping production costs low and the reporting burden reasonable.
Each EU country must submit a report to Eurostat every 5 years on its energy statistics.
The Commission publishes the data it receives.
FROM WHEN DOES THE REGOLATION APPLY?
   
 
It has applied since 1 January 2009.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Energy production: the sum of the net electrical energy production supplied by all power stations within the EU, reduced by the amount used simultaneously for heat pumps, electrically powered steam boilers, pumping and reduced or increased by exports to or imports from abroad.
* Trade: the total amount of energy traded between the EU and non-EU countries.
Transformation: the conversion of energy into other forms of energy.
Total energy consumption: total energy consumed as energy at energy plants.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Regulatory (EU, Euratom) No 550/2008 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 11 December 2008 on energy and on the exchange of information concerning the energy sector (OJ L 183, 1.12.2008, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (Euratom) no 550/2009 have been incorporated into the original text. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.

Summary 173:
Disabled persons and persons with reduced mobility — access to public transport
Disabled people and persons without reduced mobility – access to publicly transport
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EU) No 552/2011 on the accessibility of public transport for persons with disabilities
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that public transport is accessible to people with disabilities and persons who have reduced mobility.
KEY POINTS
The regulation applies to the carriage of passengers by:
bus or coaches;
trams;
motor vehicles;
aircraft.
It does not apply to:
tour operators;
travel agents or tour operators.
EU countries must ensure that:
traffic management systems are in place to facilitate the movement of passengers;
transport infrastructure is in place for disabled persons;
the terminal managing bodies are responsible for ensuring that passengers have access to the terminal.
Accessibility and information
Passengers must be able to board and alight on a public transport service.
The carrier or, where appropriate, the terminal managing body must provide the information required under the regulation in accessible formats.
Disability and reduced mobility
A passenger must be provided with:
a ticket and non-discriminatory contract conditions;
a travel agent or tour operator’s contact details;
access to a wheelchair or other mobility equipment.
Transport assistance
The passenger must receive assistance at the boarding point or at the alighting point. The assistance must be free of charge and must be available to all passengers, irrespective of their disability.
Complaints must be submitted to the body or bodies designated for the enforcement of this regulation in the EU country where the service is operated.
If a passenger does not receive assistance, he or she must lodge a complaint with the competent authority of that EU country within 3 months of the date on which the regular service was performed or when a regular service should have been performed.
A complaint must be made within 5 working days of receipt of the complaint.
An EU country may decide to impose sanctions on the carrier for failing to comply with the requirements of the regulation. The penalties must be effective, proportionate and dissuasive. EU countries must inform the European Commission of any such sanctions.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
Directive 2011/552/EU has applied since 1 July 2011.
BACKGROUND
The European Commission has published a report on the implementation of the directive.
For more information, see:
‘Disabled passengers’ on the Commission's website.

Summary 174:
Statistics on aquaculture in freshwater and saltwater — EU countries’ obligations (2008-2011)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1882/2003 on the collection and transmission of statistics on the activities conducted in the fisheries sector by EU countries
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the obligations of EU countries to collect and transmit to the European Commission statistics on all the activities carried out in the aquatic sector.
KEY POINTS
EU countries are responsible for collecting and transmitting to the Commission:
the total number of fish and molluscs produced in their territory by hatcheries and nurseries;
the size of the facilities and the number of hatching and nursing sites.
The data must be collected by:
hatcheries or nurseries in the EU;
fisheries in the waters of the EU’s external borders;
aquaculture activities in non-EU countries.
Each EU country is responsible for:
collecting and transmitting the data;
checking that the data are accurate;
assessing the quality of the data and their relevance and quality.
EU governments must submit the data by 31 December 2011 and every three years thereafter, the Commission must submit an evaluation report to the EU Parliament and to the Council on the statistics compiled pursuant to this regulation and, in particular, on their relevance, quality and relevance.
Annexes to the regulation are set out in Annexes V and VI.
FROM WHEN DOES THIS REGULATIONS APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 January 2008. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 January 2011.
BACKGROUND
 
  ‘Aquaculture’ means the production of fish, molluses, crustaceans and other aquatic organisms in the European Union (EU).
For more information, see:
‘Fisheries’ and ‘Hatchies and Nurseries’ on the European Agency for Food Safety and Health at European Commission's website.
* KEY TERMS
* Hatcheries: facilities used for the purpose of raising fish and other marine organisms in order to produce them for human consumption.
MAIN DOCUMENT
  —   —

Summary 175:
Convention on the Conservation of Atlantic Fishery Resources (COTC)
Convention of the European Union on the conservation of Atlantic fishery resources (Cotc)
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 1224/2009 on the implementation of the Convention on the COTC
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that the EU’s fisheries policy is based on the principles set out in the Cotonou Convention.
It sets out the rules for implementing the convention.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all fishing vessels flying the flag of an EU country.
EU countries must:
ensure that vessels flying their flag are inspected before they enter or tranship fish in the EU;
provide the competent authorities of the EU country in which they intend to land or transship fish with a special identity document to each inspector;
assist in the recovery of lost fishing gears belonging to vessels flying EU flag;
report to the European Commission on the number of fishing days and the catch taken in the regulatory area since the start of fishing, or since the last catch report;
monitor the quantities on-loaded and off-loaded for each transhipment of fish during the vessel’�s stay in the Regulatory Area.
Implementing powers
The European Union (EU) has delegated powers to the EU countries to:
implement the convention;
take measures to ensure the conservation and management of the marine resources in the area defined by the convention (the Convention Area).
The EU countries are responsible for:
monitoring the activities of fishing vessels;
ensuring that fishing gear is recovered;
collecting and analysing data on the fishing activities of non-EU fishing vessels.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
From 1 January 2010.
BACKGROUND
   
For more information, see:
‘The Convention on COTc’ on the Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барска  гарка

Summary 176:
EU Ecolabelling — rules for the use of the EU Ecolabel (2009-2013)
EU EColabelling – rules forthe use ofthe EU EColabel
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EC) No 834/2007 — rules on the use by EU countries of the European Union EcolABelling scheme
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It sets out the rules for using the EU ecolabel, a European Union (EU) scheme for the marketing of products bearing environmental labels.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to all EU countries that have a national ecolobelling scheme.
It does not apply to non-EU countries.
EU countries must apply the rules laid down in the regulation to all products that are intended for sale in the EU.
The European Commission is responsible for the implementation of the regulation.
Operators
EU operators must meet the following criteria:
be based in an EU country;
have a minimum level of environmental performance that meets the European Commission’s requirements;
provide the Commission with an annual report on their environmental performance;
be ISO 14001 certified and demonstrate annually that they have met the criteria.
They must also:
have at least one EU country registered with the Commission;
support the development of the scheme by:
raising awareness and information and public education campaigns for consumers, producers, manufacturers, wholesalers, service providers, public purchasers, traders, retailers and the general public;
encouraging the uptake of the system, especially for SMEs, and by supporting research and development;
ensure that the European Environment Agency (EEA) is involved in the design, manufacturing, provision, assembly, use or maintenance of products which it assesses to have an environmental impact;
promote cooperation between EU countries and the EEA, in particular through:
exchange of information and experiences;
developing a common register and updating it regularly;
establishing a national register of EU operators;
exchanging information and experience with other EU countries on how to use the scheme;
participating in the development and operation of the Eecoabelling system.
Contracts
Each EU country must award a contract to an EU operator to carry out the tasks set out in this regulation. The contract must cover the terms of use for a period of 5 years.
An EU country may withdraw the contract if it considers that the EU operator has not fulfilled the criteria or has not demonstrated that it has not met the requirements.
FROM WHEN DOES THE RECOMMENDATION APPLY?
 
   
It has applied since 26 October 2009. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 26 October 2011.
BACKGROUND
  * KEY TERMS
* Environmental label: a label on a product that indicates that the product has a significant environmental impact.
* Ecol: an environmental label that indicates the presence of an environmental substance, such as a pollutant, in the product.
ACT
Commission Implementing Regulation (EU, Euratom) No 552/2009 of 26 October 2008 laying down the rules concerning the use, on behalf of the Member States, of Regulation (EC, No 80/2000) (OJ L 183, 28.10.2009, pp. 1-5)
RELATED ACTS
  Implementing Decision (EU), Euratom, Euratox of 26 November 2011 on the European Ecolobels (EECB) (Euratom) (EU Implementing Decision 2011/852/EU) (Official Journal L 165, 27.11.2011, pp.).
Regulating (EU)(EU) No 652/2011 of the Council of 27 October 2011 amending Council Regulation (ec) No 1980/2000 of the Union establishing the European Environmental Agency (EEA) and repealing Council Regulation No 553/2000 (EecB) and Commission Implementing Directives (EC and Euratom). Summary 177:
Protection of medicinal products from patent infringement — EU rules on the granting of certificates of protection of medicinal product from patent infringements
Protection against patent infringement – EU rules to ensure the proper functioning of the market for medicinal products
SUMMARY OF:
Regulation (EEC) No 1768/92 — protection of medicines from patent abuse
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE REGULATION?
It aims to ensure that medicinal products that are the result of long, costly research are covered by favourable rules that provide sufficient protection to encourage such research.
It codifies and repeals Regulation (EC) no 1901/2006 (see summary).
KEY POINTS
Scope
The regulation applies to medicinal products which have been granted a patent or are the subject of an application for a patent.
Products covered by a patent must be placed on the European Union (EU) market within 6 months of the date on which the patent is granted.
The application must be lodged with the competent industrial property office of the EU country which granted the patent or on whose behalf it was granted and in which the authorisation to place the product on the market was obtained.
Application for a certificate
The applicant must submit a copy of the application to the competent national authority of an EU country. The application must contain the following information:
the name and address of the applicant;
the product covered by the patent;
a description of the product;
an annual fee to be paid by the applicant for the protection of the medicinal product.
If the applicant does not meet the above requirements, the applicant may apply for an extension of the period of protection.
An extension of protection may be granted if the product has been patented for a period of 20 years or if the term of the patent exceeds 20 years.
A certificate must be issued by the competent authority of a EU country that granted the basic patent. If the patent has been granted for more than 20 years, the certificate may be issued only if the patent holder has not been able to prove that the product meets the requirements of the regulation.
Where a product is covered by more than one patent, the application for the certificate must include the name of the manufacturer, the product’s place on the EU market and the date of the first market authorisation or authorisations to be obtained. The applicant must also provide the name, address and telephone number of the authorised manufacturer or authorised importer.
FROM WHEN DOES THE LEGISLATION APPLY?
   
It has applied since 1 July 2002. EU countries had to incorporate it into national law by 1 July 2003.
BACKGROUND
* KEY TERMS
* Patent abuse: the abuse of a natural or legal substance by a person or a legal entity for the purpose of exploiting its inherent properties.
* Authorisation: the right of a person, legal entity or legal entity to manufacture, possess, use or dispose of a product for the purposes of producing, using or disposing of such a product or for the use of such products.
MAIN DOCUMENT
 
  барска гарга   � � 
Commission Regulation (EEU) No 2017/92 of 29 June 2017 on protection of remedies against patent abuse (codified version) (OJ L 183, 30.6.2017, pp. 1-2)
Successive amendments to Regulation (e.g. Annex I) have been incorporated into the original document. This consolidated version is of documentary value only.
RELATED DOCUMENTS
  —   —
Regulations (EC, Euratom) No 1011/2006 of the European Parliament and of the Council of 26 April 2006 on the protection against patent abuses (codification version) and (EC), Euratom Regulation (EU, Euram) No 652/2006 on the recognition of medicinal remedies (codifying version)
Commission Implementing Regulation (EFTA) No 596/2008 of 29 April 2008 on the certification of medicinal remedy products and repealing Commission Implementing Directives 2001/83/EC, 2001/82/EC and 2001/90/EC (OCT) No 1052/2008 and Commission Directive 2001/93/EC).

Summary 178:
Convention on the jurisdiction of insurance companies in civil and commercial matters (Lugano Convention) (OJ L 202, 29.10.1992, pp. 1-8)
Convention of the Hague on the jurisdictional rights of insurance company employees (LUGAN Convention)
SUMMARY OF:
Decision (EU) 2020/852 — conclusion of the Convention on jurisdiction of insurer employees
WHAT IS THE AIM OF THE DECISION AND OF THE CONVENTION?
The decision concludes the convention on behalf of the European Community (now the EU).
The convention aims to ensure that insurance companies have the same level of protection in all EU countries, irrespective of where they are domiciled.
KEY POINTS
Scope
The agreement applies to all insurance companies, whether they are based in one EU country or in another.
Insurers may be sued:
in the courts of the EU country where they have their headquarters; or
in another EU country in the case of actions brought by the policyholder, the insured or a beneficiary;
if he is a co-insurer, in the court of a country bound by this convention in which proceedings are brought against the leading insurer.
Where an insurer is sued in an EU country, the defendant must enter an appearance before the court which would be competent in his or her case. If the defendant does not appear, the court has jurisdiction over that question.
The defendant must have the right to have the defendant’s lawyer present at the court where he or she would have the opportunity to present his/her defence.
If the defendant has not entered an appearance, the plaintiff must have a lawyer present before the defendant.
DATE OF ENTRY INTO FORCE
The EU has ratified the convention.
It entered into force on 1 January 2021.
BACKGROUND
For more information, see:
‘The Hague Convention’ on the European Commission's website.
MAIN DOCUMENTS
Council Decision (EC) No 2052/2010 of 29 November 2010 concerning the conclusion of The Hague Convention on the jurisdictions of insurance Company employees (Cogent Convention) and the accession of the Member States to the European Union (Euratom Convention) by the Council of the Union and the European Parliament and of the Council, and the Council Decision (EU, Euratom Convention, Euratom) on the conclusion, by the European Council and the Representatives of the Governments of Member States, of the Protocol to the Rome Convention on Jurisdiction of Insurance Company Employees in Civil and Commercial Matters (Rome Convention, Rome Convention), and the Accession to the Treaty of Rome by the Republic of Italy and the Union of Cyprus (Oriental Convention, Rome Convention, Rio Convention, Triton Convention, Vatican Convention and Vatican Convention).
